1


Introduction



The Relevance of the History of International Law

for Situating Russia’s War Against Ukraine

This book is a critical history of international law in the Russian Empire, the Soviet Union, and to some extent also the post- Soviet Russian Federation, with a focus on the continuity of Russia’s imperial practices and discourses in inter-national law. The context for writing it is, inevitably, the Russian Federation’s full- scale invasion of Ukraine on 24 February 2022. The Russian government has attempted to justify its aggression against Ukraine with a mix of narratives and claims based on international law but also very much on interpretations regarding the history of the Russian Empire and the Soviet Union, including their constitutional history. Arguments on what the right of peoples to self- determination implies in international law are an aspect of these claims, in the context of ethnic Russians and native Russian speakers living beyond the borders of the Russian Federation. When one listens to President Putin or other leading Russian politicians or clerics such as the head of the Russian Orthodox Church, Patriarch Kirill, it seems that Russia’s ‘special military oper-ation’ against Ukraine is all about the legacy of ‘historical Russia’ (code for the Russian Empire) and certain historical and legal continuities which determine claims in the contemporary world as well.1

Russia’s war against Ukraine has given work to international lawyers in gov-

ernments and international organizations who in their memorandums and meetings discuss the legal qualification of Russia’s war against Ukraine, mech-anisms of individual accountability and state responsibility, sanctions, and so on. As the results of votes at the UNGA demonstrate, the global consensus is

that Russia’s war against Ukraine violates the prohibition of the use of force in the UN Charter (Article 2 paragraph 4) and constitutes aggression.2 Since Russia started its full- scale war against Ukraine in 2022, international law scholars have been offering their initial analyses of the situation in journal symposia such as the American Journal of International Law.3 Russia’s use of force and policies in Ukraine is widely seen as violating international law, even its jus cogens norms.4 At the same time, when finalizing this book project in 2025, it was already visible how facts on the ground created by Russia will have some significance in Ukraine, which will be a major challenge to contem-porary international law.

Among Western governments and some scholars, one popular insight is

that Russia’s war against Ukraine is in its essence an imperialist war.5 One may think, for example, of Putin’s ontological claim that Ukrainians do not ‘exist’ as a nation separate from Russians.6 That Russian foreign policy had taken a ‘neo- imperialist’ turn was recognized in international relations literature even before Russia’s full- scale invasion of Ukraine.7 There is an understanding that, under Putin, an ‘imperial presidency’ has developed in Russia, and in par-ticular Russia’s constitutional amendments of 2020 expressed a neo- imperial version of Russia’s identity.8

At the same time, there are quite different understandings of the essence of

Russian imperialism, or indeed imperialism generally. For example, Anastasiya Kotova and Ntina Tzouvala see in Russia’s decision to attack Ukraine a reflec-tion of ‘inner- imperialist rivalries’,9 suggesting that imperialism is a more sys-temic feature of the current world, and that among the great powers Russia has not been alone in acting imperialistically, even in the context of Ukraine. ‘Inner- imperialist contradictions’ was of course already a trope in Soviet

approaches to international law.10 In Western critical anti- capitalist literature, the war in Ukraine is somehow also a result of Western imperialism, aiming to turn Ukraine into a ‘de facto colony of Europe’.11 The rhetoric of US President Trump about Canada, Gaza, Panama, and Greenland indeed demonstrates that imperialism is not confined to Russia.

This book aims to put the Russo– Ukrainian war in its historical context but

also goes much beyond that war, using it as an opportunity to examine the history of international law in Russia and the Soviet Union. What concerns us here is the genealogy or ‘archaeology’ of imperialist ideas and practices in Russia’s and Soviet historical interactions with international law. Taking the imperialist features of Russia’s aggression against Ukraine as a starting point, we are interested in unearthing and clarifying the history of such ideas and patterns in Russia’s interactions with international law. What role has the idea of Russia qua Empire played in its historical interactions with international law and how has this idea transformed over time, including during the crucial Soviet period? These are the central questions which this book aims to answer.

To illustrate the relevance of the history of international law in terms of

understanding Russia’s aggression against Ukraine since 2014— and especially since 2022— five aspects can be highlighted.

First, the history and transformations of Russia’s constitutional identity

and self- understanding. On 1 July 2020, following the initiative of President Putin, Russia amended its constitution. These amendments included refer-ences to state continuity and succession with the Soviet Union, as well as the statement that Russia ‘respects the memory of the defenders of the Fatherland and protects the historical truth’ (Article 67.1 paragraph 3). In an interview justifying the need for constitutional amendments, President Putin called Vladimir Lenin’s decision in and after 1917 to reconstruct the Russian Empire along federalist lines (including the union republics’ constitutional right to se-cession) a ‘ticking time bomb’, and also claimed that in 1991 ‘some republics left the center with more than what they came with’, namely with territorial gifts from Russia.12 This makes the history of the creation of the Soviet Union on the ruins of the tsarist Russian Empire relevant for understanding contemporary

conflicts between the states which once formed part of the Russian Empire and the Soviet Union.

Second, justification of the war by some international lawyers in Russia.

On 7 March 2022 the presidium of the Russian Association of International Law, an organization of scholars and other international law experts in Russia, published a statement justifying and excusing Russia’s actions against Ukraine.13 While this document mostly repeats the Kremlin’s narrative about the ‘special military operation’, things fall into place when we consider the rich history of how Russian and Soviet international lawyers have been engaged with international legal justifications of their country’s wars, military interven-tions, and conquests. As we will demonstrate in this book, this— with justifi-cations of a war that Russia initiated— is how the discourse about European international law started in the Russian Empire in the early eighteenth century. This is also relevant for understanding the fact that the Constitutional Court of the Russian Federation approved Russia’s annexation of four regions of Ukraine in 2022,14 although 143 states out of the UN’s 193 member states con-sidered it illegal under international law in the relevant UNGA resolution.15

Third, treaties and attitudes towards treaties, their violation, and their ter-

mination. When attacking Ukraine, Russia violated (besides the UN Charter itself ) the 1994 Budapest memorandum and the Russian– Ukrainian border treaty of 28 January 2003 in which Russia recognized Ukraine’s 1991 borders. However, there is a fundamental question: what are treaties, philosophically speaking, and how have they been understood in Russian and Soviet tradi-tions of international law? We should also note that, in Moscow’s own nar-rative, it was not Russia but Ukraine that violated international treaties: the Minsk agreements of 2014– 15. The Minsk agreements are in themselves a fas-cinating example of what is tolerable and what is not according to fundamental principles of international treaty law. Although supportive references were made to the Minsk agreements both by the UN Security Council (UNSC)16 and the International Court of Justice (ICJ)17 (‘Package of Measures for the Implementation of the Minsk Agreements’), it should be acknowledged at least in retrospect that Ukraine was forced to sign the Minsk agreements under

military coercion from Russia. That the Ukrainian government did not itself officially say so at the time was the result and expression of the very same co-ercion and power dynamics. This raises fundamental questions about the val-idity of treaties in international law, nowadays under the Vienna Convention on the Law of Treaties. The question of the validity and termination of treaties has a rich history in the Russian Empire and the Soviet Union; this book maps the discussions and arguments in Russian and Soviet international legal theory and practice, spanning several hundred years.

Fourth, how far should Russia’s rights or veto power extend with respect

to its neighbours? In December 2021, before starting its full- scale war against Ukraine, Moscow presented a demand that NATO’s military infrastructure in Central and Eastern Europe be rolled back to the status quo effective in 1997, before NATO’s Eastern enlargement beyond East Germany. Doing so would affect not only Ukraine (which according to this logic would of course never be allowed to join NATO) but also Poland, Hungary, Czechia, Slovakia, Romania, Bulgaria, the three Baltic States, several Balkan states, and of course also Finland and Sweden, which joined NATO after Russia’s aggression against Ukraine. However, the question of legitimate security concerns and spheres of influence also has a fascinating history because in the early nineteenth cen-tury the Russian Empire intervened militarily in Central Europe in defence of the principle of monarchical legitimacy. Moreover, in the twentieth century, the Soviet Union did the same in Hungary and Czechoslovakia in the name of socialist international law and proletarian internationalism. Russia and the Soviet Union have a long history of trying to set conditions for statehood of smaller states in Central and Eastern Europe, including military intervention, occupation, and demands for the neutral status of small states.

Fifth, the discourse which the Russian government used when going to

war against Ukraine can properly be understood considering the earlier his-tory of international legal arguments and discourses, especially from the period of the Cold War. For example, just before the Russo– Ukrainian war, Russia’s foreign minister Sergey Lavrov referred at the OSCE to the principle of indivisibility of security,18 which Moscow said had not been respected vis- à- vis the Russian Federation. Contemporary international lawyers from countries other than Russia may not necessarily be aware of such a principle as part of international law. Apparently, it implies that security- related decisions should not be made against objections and in this sense to the disadvantage of the

other state concerned (a sort of veto power). However, in terms of the history of such claims in Moscow, it is of importance that during the last decades of the Soviet Union, Soviet international lawyers argued that indivisibility of security had become an international legal principle, at least in Soviet security relations with the US and NATO.19 This claim is a somewhat forgotten aspect in the his-tory of Western– Soviet interactions in international law.

Altogether, the history of international law matters for a deeper under-

standing of the Russo– Ukrainian war; indeed, one of the best ways to under-stand this war (which is not the same as justifying) is historically. At the same time, it is not my argument that the history of international law points at the inevitability of the present condition or unavoidability of Putin’s decision to launch his ‘special military operation’. The Russian government’s decision to attack Ukraine was still exactly that— a decision. In 2014 and in 2022, a dif-ferent future for Russia and Ukraine, as well as for Russian– Western relations, would have been possible.



The Book’s Argument and Its Context in the Literature

Even though decisions and specific personalities play a big role in history, each major country’s tradition of international law also involves something of path dependency. Certain historical discourses, patterns, and practices keep returning, even allowing for noteworthy transformations, changes, and new interpretations over time. It would be a mistake not to take such patterns ser-iously as by neglecting them in analysis one ends up being shocked and sur-prised every time they happen again. This book deals with the question of how much of the international legal tradition in Russia and the Soviet Union has revolved around the imperial idea, the Russian Empire’s vast territory, and its leading status in the world. My main argument in this book is that there has been a strong imperial continuity in Russia and the Soviet Union concerning its own state identity as well as in its discourses and uses of international law in geopolitically important contexts. Yet paradoxically, a quite notable part of the historical literature of international law and imperialism has looked the other

way and has not much discussed Russia as an example of imperialism in inter-national law, or seriously problematized the continuity in imperial Russian, Soviet, and post- Soviet traditions of international law.

Some examples from scholarly literature are the following. Concerning

pre- Bolshevik history in Russia, some interpretations diminish the imperialist weight of the Russian Empire in international law before 1917. For example, Arnulf Becker Lorca sees the Russian Empire— in the context of jus publicum Europaeum of the pre- World War I era— only as a ‘semi- peripheral’ state.20 But the issue is more fundamental with evaluations of the Soviet legacy in inter-national law. In 2007, Professor John Quigley of Ohio State University School of Law published a book in which he highlighted the positive and progressive features of Soviet contributions in the realm of law, to an extent also touching upon international legal initiatives.21 In a much more recent book, the editors maintain that the Soviet revolution of 1917 in Russia inspired anti- imperialist movements throughout the world.22 Several chapters in their book deal with Soviet alternative approaches to international law, from legal theory to use of force and investment law. However, when inspiring anti- imperialist move-ments in the world, what was the Soviet attitude towards the earlier imperi-alism of the Russian Empire: was the previous imperialism of tsarist Russia discontinued or not? The question is not tackled much in the co- edited volume, but it also concerns the legacy of the Russian Bolshevik revolution of 1917. Moreover, Western literature contains interpretations which are quite appreciative of aspects of the Soviet approach to the right of peoples to self- determination and somewhat sympathetic towards Soviet attitudes to inter-national law during the Cold War.23 Some views in contemporary scholarship celebrate Lenin’s stance on self- determination as ‘progressive’ while in contrast US President Woodrow Wilson is seen as someone who partly just appropri-ated Lenin’s ideas and turned them into something else, and something weaker, in the political process.24 Moreover, in a recently published edited volume,

Raluca Grosescu and Ned Richardson- Little see socialism (including Soviet socialism) as a ‘driver of progressive projects’ and combatting ‘Western imperi-alism’.25 Umut Özsu observes correctly that during the decolonization period, the Soviet Union sided with non- aligned states at the UN and wanted the right of peoples to self- determination in Africa and Asia to be interpreted broadly.26 Other authors writing on the history of international law and the Soviet Union add more critical notes as well. For example, in his history of the negotiation of the Geneva Red Cross conventions of 1949, Boyd van Dijk maintains that Moscow had no desire to accept any legal restraint on its ongoing efforts to subjugate Eastern Europe and that, ‘like other imperial powers’, it expected to be able to control application of the Geneva conventions in the context of in-surgencies which might happen in territories under its own control.27

In contrast to some of these works— which tend to downplay the imperialist

energy and continuity of the Soviet Union and/ or the Russian Empire— my book shows the continued imperial and imperialist legacy and trajectory of international law in Russia and invites readers to take this imperialist legacy seriously when examining the global history of imperialism and inter-national law.

In 2015 I wrote a book entitled Russian Approaches to International Law,28

to which the current book can be seen as a sequel, also because it is with the same publisher. My 2015 book engaged with the work accomplished by the notion of ‘civilization’ in Russian thinking about international law and exam-ined to what extent Russia’s post- Soviet concept of international law has dif-fered from Western understandings. My 2015 book’s main observation— that at various times, Russia has emphasized its civilizational distinctness from the West, which in turn has had repercussions for its conceptualization and use of international law— has since then been further confirmed and consolidated in state practice and opinio juris in Russia.29 At the same time, my 2015 book

mostly bypassed Soviet theory and practice of international law. In contrast, the present book also reconstructs the Soviet period in Russia’s history and in international law discursively, linking it to debates about imperialism as well as Russia’s pre- and post- Soviet history. Compared to my earlier research, I also present here a more critical view of the leading tsarist international lawyer Fedor Martens, as I discuss his entanglements in tsarist Russian imperialist and colonialist projects.

A connection exists between ideas about Russian civilization and the

Russian Empire. Emphasizing Russia’s civilizational distinctness has been one of the ways to protect and advance the interests of the Russian Empire— while during the twentieth century, the idea of Soviet communism was another.30 Of the two ideas and concepts, the Empire has been the continuous and constant one. When the communist idea was dominant, ideas of Russia’s civilizational distinctiveness, for example among the Russian Eurasianists who were in exile after 1917, were criticized as ‘fascist’31 in Soviet literature. However, nowadays Russia’s President Vladimir Putin, who is influenced by Eurasianist ideas and has favourably referred to the emigrant thinker on Russian statehood Ivan Ilyin (1883– 1954), whose thought at least the North American historian and public intellectual Timothy Snyder has characterized as fascist,32 criticizes Lenin as a political leader who put a ‘time bomb’ under the building of ‘his-torical Russia’.33 But in several ways, both of these ideological perspectives— Eurasianism and Soviet communism— wanted the best for the Empire, even though the domestic nature and ideology of the Empire was different.



What is Imperialism and How Does it Relate to Russia?

An important point to note about debates and interpretations in international law is that the very notions of empire and imperialism are contested.34 For the

following discussion, it is therefore crucial that I lay out how I understand and use those concepts in this book. Semantically, I see a slight difference between the adjectives ‘imperial’ and ‘imperialist’. ‘Imperial’ pertains to the Empire and— as Russia was officially an empire until 1917— there is no controversy in characterizing its pre- 1917 international legal practices and discourses as pertaining to the Empire and thus imperial.35 Historically, powers which call themselves empires are often simultaneously also ‘imperialist’ (expansionist), a circumstance that usually finds a reflection in their international law prac-tice and discourses. However, a further important question is: to what extent can we still also characterize as ‘imperialist’ international legal practices and discourses of major states which explicitly understand themselves as revolu-tionary and ‘anti- imperialist’? The Soviet Union is a major case of interest in this regard. We need to look behind the labels and self- representations and will critically examine practices and discourses in the context of international law.

In 1721, with new conquests in Northeastern Europe, Tsar Peter the Great

declared what was previously known as the Grand Duchy of Muscovy to be the Russian Empire. Ruled by the Romanov dynasty, the Russian Empire for-mally existed until 1917. Over the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries, the Russian Empire continuously expanded territorially by annexing formerly Polish and Lithuanian, Swedish, Ottoman, Persian, and Chinese territories or those belonging to smaller states (for example, the Kingdom of Kartli– Kakheti in today’s Georgia). The history of the Russian Empire and of Russian im-perialism as such has been extensively studied.36 At the same time, the history of imperialism in Russia and its connection with international law has only recently been seriously taken up by researchers.37 The history of European imperialism is nowadays one of the main themes in critical literature on inter-national law, but curiously enough, Russia— and especially the Soviet Union— are notably absent, even invisible, in this literature, where the main energy has

been devoted to the study of the history of West European empires. However, as this book will demonstrate, the Russian Empire played a prominent role in the historical interplay between international law and imperialism. Moreover, the changing challenges to the Empire decisively shaped international legal doctrines and discourses in Russia. This book postulates that, in Russia, no other factor was as crucial for the evolution of the national tradition of inter-national law as the needs of and challenges to the Empire.

The end of tsarist Russia in March 1917 accelerated centrifugal tendencies

in the Russian Empire. In 1917, the Russian provisional government hastily undertook changes in the territorial administration of some European parts of the Empire, which to an extent acknowledged the Empire’s multinational composition and emerging claims for self- determination. Elements of ethnic- based autonomy were introduced in some western territories of the Empire such as the Baltic provinces. However, still no territory was supposed to se-cede from the Russian Empire under the post- tsarist provisional government of Russia in 1917. Then, in November 1917, the Bolsheviks came to power and the Russian Empire became Soviet Russia, which represented a further signifi-cant transformation. At least rhetorically, the Bolsheviks declared their vocal support for the right of peoples to self- determination, even up to the point of separation from Russia. Starting from December 1917, several territories seceded or attempted to secede from the Russian Empire, particularly in its European part. However, in all these non- ethnic- Russian secessionist terri-tories ‘bourgeois’ factions clashed with Soviet- supported forces or war broke out with Soviet Russia, also about the borders of the new states, for example in Poland. The Soviet Russian government militarily and politically supported the communist factions in separatist territories while claiming that national Soviet formations were the manifestation of the proclaimed self- determination of peoples. Between late 1918 and 1922, several separate Soviet republics, such as Russia, Ukraine, Byelorussia, and the Caucasus republics, co- existed alongside each other; the constitutional or international legal nature of their relationship at the time remained ambivalent and was disputed both then and later. After the Bolshevik victory in the Russian Civil War, on 30 December 1922 these Soviet republics concluded a treaty establishing the Soviet Union, a state that existed until December 1991, when the Russian Federation replaced the Soviet Union in international organizations such as the UN as the Soviet Union was dissolved.

The Soviet government had proclaimed the right to separate from Russia

and eventually— but only after military conflicts or civil wars in which the Bolsheviks could not prevail— concluded peace treaties with ‘bourgeois’ 12 IntroductIon

governments of states which had seceded from Russia. The peace treaties of 1920 with some of the newly emergent nations (Estonia, Latvia, Lithuania, and Georgia) highlighted the right of peoples to self- determination as a ground of secession from Russia. However, one needs to look deeper into this, as Soviet practice— for example, in Georgia— very soon, and even during Lenin’s life-time, started to differ starkly from the rhetoric of those treaties. It can be de-bated whether, for Lenin, self- determination of peoples and separation from Russia was a strategic and moral necessity or merely a tactical concession, as there are also citations in his work which highlight that he thought of self- determination of peoples in Russia as something historically temporary.38 Lenin’s views on self- determination were later subject to openly imperialist Soviet reinterpretations. Moreover, Lenin did not decide Soviet policy on self- determination alone: for example, Stalin wrote in 1920 that demands to sep-arate from Russia had to be rejected because they ‘contradict the interests of the popular masses both at the center and the periphery’.39

One of the main intrigues explored in this book is to what extent—

notwithstanding revolutionary domestic changes and the loss of certain ter-ritories at the western border— Soviet Russia and the Soviet Union continued Russia’s imperialist and great power identity in contexts relevant for inter-national law. To be sure, noteworthy and in some sense radical changes took place in Soviet Russian international legal policies and discourses in and after 1917; after all, even the official name of the country itself was changed. However, transformations and breaks of continuity were true in some respects while in other aspects pertaining to the Empire continuity with tsarist Russia was claimed directly, especially in the context of territorial rights and pre-rogatives. Moreover, Soviet Russia and the Soviet Union soon compensated themselves for the old Russian Empire’s territories lost in the west as they ex-panded further into Central Asia in the 1920s, at the expense of the former tsarist Russian vassal states of Bukhara and Khiva, now the Soviet republics of Bukhara and Khorezm, which were soon officially integrated into the Soviet Union. Indeed, the whole political landscape of Central Asia was further re-formed, up to the very names of territorial entities. The biggest transformation in Soviet Russia and the Soviet Union, compared to the tsarist Empire, was constitutional: that the territory of the Russian Empire (minus those non- Soviet states which had genuinely separated from Russia) was reorganized

38 We will come back to these references and their relevance later in the book.

In 1939– 45, the Soviet Union not only reversed most Russian territorial

losses of 1918– 20 but also made new acquisitions, including in territories which had never belonged to the Russian Empire, such as Galicia (the Lviv re-gion in today’s Ukraine and formerly part of Poland and the Austro– Hungarian Empire) and Tuva (formerly a part of Mongolia, which itself had been a part of the Chinese Empire). During the Cold War, rivals of the Soviet Union, es-pecially Mao Zedong’s People’s Republic of China, regularly referred to Soviet practices of international law such as the so- called Brezhnev doctrine— particularly in the context of the Soviet- led military intervention against Czechoslovakia in 1968— as imperialist and hegemonic.40 The communist Chinese concept for such Soviet practices was ‘social- imperialism’. However, during the 1960s, when the PRC started to challenge the Soviet Union’s bor-ders in Central Asia and the Far East, asserting that the borders were the result of unequal treaties of the colonial era, Soviet scholars responded that China itself had been a conquering and expanding empire in the past, in regions such as Xinjang and Tibet.41 In several aspects, as the Ukrainian– American historian Taras Hunczak (1932– 2024) argued, the Russian Revolution of 1917 profoundly transformed Russian imperialism, and— indirectly— imperialism and empires elsewhere.42

After the collapse of the Soviet Union in 1991, the same question arose re-

garding the Russian Federation as was asked after 1917 about Soviet Russia and, starting from the end of 1922, the Soviet Union: as a subject of inter-national law, is it the same state as before, a new one, or a partly old/ partly new one? While questions of state identity are asked from the perspective of history and politics more generally, they also have more specific meanings in the con-text of international law. From the perspective of international legal theory (or doctrine), the mainstream view is that either the old state continues to exist as a subject of international law, or it does not, in which case state succession oc-curs. This concerns inheritance of rights and duties by the new state(s). Today, the question of the Russian Federation’s international legal identity (vis- à- vis

What is Colonialism and How Does It Relate to Russia?

If an empire can experience complex ideological transformations and, in that sense, a self- proclaimed ‘anti- imperialist’ power like the Soviet Union can also pursue imperialist policies, then what about another related concept: coloni-alism? Emerging literature in international relations scholarship has drawn our attention to Russia’s ‘colonialist’ history and related patterns in Russian foreign policy discourse, particularly vis- à- vis Ukraine.43 Two comparative constitutional law scholars— William Partlett from Melbourne and Herbert Küpper from Regensburg— have offered a thought- provoking conceptual framework, interpreting the Russian Empire, the Soviet Union, and the ter-ritories which they formerly owned and dominated through the lens of colo-nialism.44 In a similar vein, the historian and international relations scholar Alexander Etkind has used the concept of colonialism quite broadly in the context of the history of the Russian Empire, for example characterizing in that way not only the Russian occupation of East Prussia in 1757– 62 but also Russia’s annexation of Poland and the Baltic provinces in the eighteenth cen-tury.45 However, contemporaries of these events and annexations did not think of Russia’s military occupations or conquests in Central and Eastern Europe in terms of ‘colonialism’. The idea of colonialism was linked in people’s minds with the idea of civilization. When Muscovy took the Baltic provinces from Sweden in the Great Nordic War (1700– 1721), its main writer and legitimizer, the diplomat Petr Shafirov, made the argument— endorsed by Tsar Peter the Great— that Russia itself could be considered as sufficiently ‘civilized’ and was entitled to take part in European affairs.46 Arguments about colonization in Central and Eastern Europe were rather made by Germans, who also assumed the existence of a west– östliches Kulturgefälle in Europe. For example, the



43 Maria Mälksoo, ‘The Postcolonial Moment in Russia’s War against Ukraine’, 25 Journal of Genocide Research 2022, 471– 81; Kseniya Oksamytna, ‘Russia’s Status as a Colonial Power’, 01.11.2024,

E- International Relations, https:// www.e- ir.info/ pdf/ 106 552.

44 William Partlett, Herbet Küpper, The Post- Soviet as Post- Colonial. A New Paradigm for Understanding Constitutional Dynamics in the Former Soviet Empire, Edward Elgar Publishing, 2022.

What IS colonIalISm and hoW doeS It relate to ruSSIa? 15

nineteenth- century Baltic German international lawyer August Bulmerincq (1822– 90) talked about the Baltic provinces of the Russian Empire as at least historically German (rather than Russian) ‘colonies’, referring to the thirteenth- century German crusaders’ conquests and states in that part of Europe as well as Christianization efforts, but also the perceived cultural superiority of the Germans vis- à- vis the numerically smaller local peoples.47 In that sense, from an international legal perspective at least, it seems historically anachronistic to associate Russia’s eighteenth- century occupation of East Prussia or its annex-ation of Poland and the Baltic provinces with ‘colonialism’.

International law in the eighteenth century and particularly in the nine-

teenth century assigned a special regime for Europe. This was based on the distinction between ‘civilized’ and ‘uncivilized’ peoples and the term ‘colonies’ was used when the land of the ‘uncivilized’ was seized by conquest (and set-tled) by Europeans. Usually, a large sea or ocean lay between conqueror and conquered. A specific feature of the Russian Empire, imperialism, and colo-nialism is that, being a Eurasian continental power, the Russian Empire con-quered adjacent territories, declaring the new territories part of Russia but without calling them ‘colonies’. Nevertheless, when the tsarist Russian Empire expanded to Siberia, the Caucasus, and Central Asia, colonialist practices and attitudes were predominant.48 In conquered Asian and Caucasian territories, the Russian Empire’s practices were as severe as behaviour for which West European maritime empires became notorious in their own conquered terri-tories.49 Additionally, at its Caucasian and Central Asian borders, tsarist Russia also used a specific legal institute of colonial international law, namely pro-tectorates. This happened as far back as the late eighteenth century in Kartli– Kakheti (today’s Georgia) and in the late nineteenth century in Central Asia, with Bukhara and Khiva. Until 1917, the Russian Empire’s laws treated indi-genous peoples in conquered Asian territories as inferior people (inorodtsy).50 The reasons why the Russian Empire preferred not to call its conquests outside Europe ‘colonies’ have been discussed among historians, and the matter was also to an extent debated among the elites of the Russian Empire.51 At the same time, the US historian Michael Khodarkovsky concludes that in substance

48 Alexander Etkind, Internal Colonization, 69– 78.

49 See, eg, on the conquest of Tartarstan and Chechnya in Johannes Socher, Russia and the Right to Self- Determination in the Post- Soviet Space, OUP, 2021, 70 et seq.

50 See, eg, L A Challandes, Russkoe gosudarstvennoe pravo, Tipografia Shnakenburga, 1908, 55– 6.

51 Michael Khodarkovsky, ‘Between Europe and Asia. Russia’s State Colonialism in Comparative Perspective, 1550s– 1990s’, 52 Canadian- American Slavic Studies 2018, 1– 29.

(although not in form), the Russian Empire was no different in its conquests in Asia from colonialist Western European empires.52 Researchers of colonial empires (with ‘overseas’ colonies) admit that in important ways, these empires had much in common with continental empires such as the Russian Empire.53

That imperial Russia before 1917 did not call its conquests in Asia or

Caucasus ‘colonies’ had, at the time, more domestic than international signifi-cance. As pointed out by the Russian Empire’s leading international lawyer, Fedor Fedorovich Martens (1845– 1909)— as well as, later, the German dip-lomat and historian of international law Wilhelm Grewe (1911– 2000)— by the nineteenth century, ‘colony’ had become a term of constitutional rather than international law.54 From the perspective of international law, a colony was still part of an empire’s territory, even though important constitutional differences may have existed between the motherland and the colony in empires that in-cluded colonies. Later, in international law in the 1960s, the ‘salt- water doc-trine’ became central for an understanding of colonies and colonialism in the context of the right of peoples to self- determination.55 In a way, the ‘salt- water doctrine’ of self- determination acknowledged the non- colonial status of the Soviet Union, although substantively, the criterion of an ocean in between the territories remained quite arbitrary. The UN understanding favoured the con-tinental empires, by leaving their colonial practices out of the spotlight during decolonization. However, this distinction is emphasized in Russian inter-national law scholarship: Russia was a ‘unique’ empire because it was not a ‘colonial’ empire.56

One fascinating aspect of the Russian history of imperialism and coloni-

alism in the context of international law is how the Soviets dealt with the tsarist legacy when they came to power. They proclaimed equality between the peo-ples of the former Russian Empire— Lenin famously called the tsarist Empire ‘the prison house of peoples’— and took at least a rhetorical distance from tsarist policies and discourses, referring to both as ‘imperialist’ and ‘colonialist’. After 1917, Soviet Russia highlighted this new perspective in its treaty practice with ‘eastern’ countries. For example, the Treaty of Friendship signed by Persia and Soviet Russia on 26 February 1921 called the previous Russian governments ‘colonizing’ (Article 1) and the Russian Empire’s previous policies towards

However, especially after the victory in the Second World War, a transform-

ation soon emerged in attitudes between those Soviet international lawyers who conceptually prioritized the socio- economic and ideological changes in Russia since 1917 and those who, in the bigger picture of things, preferred con-tinuity with the former Russian Empire. This second group of scholars proudly argued that the Soviet Union had been the same state as the Russian Empire all along. After the Second World War, negative appraisals regarding the tsarist Empire started to fade away in Soviet international law scholarship, with the Russian Empire’s legacy in international law being referred to more and more positively, most strikingly in the work of the Soviet international lawyer— and after 1953 also ICJ judge— Fedor Kozhevnikov (1903– 98).62 In practice, the Soviet government also made sure that, notwithstanding constitutional norms and ideas about the self- determination of peoples and the proclaimed sover-eignty of the Soviet republics, secession from the Soviet Union could only be

Here it is worthwhile examining the international and constitu-tional legal doctrines and methods of argumentation used by Soviet lawyers to explain and justify Soviet state practice, which often failed to correspond with the initial Soviet rhetoric on self- determination.



Why the History of Russian Imperialism Has Often Been

Neglected in the Literature on International Law

As already indicated, one of the challenges at the beginning of this book is that, at least until recently, Russian and Soviet imperialist practices and discourses have been relatively marginal in the Western literature on the history of inter-national law and imperialism. An interesting question is why this has been so. To start with, the literature on the history of international law involves a certain conflation of the concepts of ‘imperialism’ and ‘colonialism’.63 As Russia did not formally have colonies in the same way as did the West European empires, this conflation itself may, for some legal scholars, leave the Russian Empire at the margins— or even out of the picture altogether— of imperialism in the his-tory of international law. In this type of literature, ‘imperialism’ and ‘coloni-alism’ are often treated as two sides of the same coin; for the one to be seen and critically acknowledged, the other ought to appear on the other side as well. In any case, the history of imperialism in international law has so far been written primarily by scholars who have been more familiar with or interested in the history of the West European empires, the British and French, but also the Spanish, Dutch, Portuguese, and Belgian Empires, and also from the perspec-tive of US historical involvement with imperialism.64 In the critical literature on postcolonial international law, Russia is mostly absent— a blank sheet.65



63 See, eg, A Anghie, ‘Imperialism and International Legal Theory’, in: Anne Orford, Florian Hoffmann (eds) The Oxford Handbook of the Theory of International Law, OUP, 2016, 157 (wondering why imperialism has not been a central concern of the theory of international law and postulating that one reason was that ‘colonial questions’ were seen as pragmatic, political issues).

64 Antony Anghie, Imperialism, Sovereignty, and the Making of International Law, CUP, 2005; Jennifer Pitts, Boundaries of the International: Law and Empire, Harvard UP, 2018; Lauren Benton, Lisa Ford, Rage for Order. But further reasons explain why the Russian Empire, and especially the

Soviet Union, have been relatively ignored in the critical literature on the history of empires in international law. The first Soviet leader, Vladimir Lenin (1870– 1924), argued influentially that imperialism was the ‘highest stage of capitalism’; it logically followed that the Bolsheviks finished with imperialism— and simultaneously finished with capitalism— in Russia the moment they came to power. Besides Lenin, the Soviet politician and writer Nikolay Bukharin (1888– 1938) propagated a similar linkage between im-perialism and capitalism.66 Quite interestingly for international lawyers, in the interwar Soviet Union, Evgeny Bronislavovich Pashukanis (1891– 1937), a legal scholar who became a leading voice in Marxist theory of law, and also specifically in international law, dedicated an entire study to the further de-velopment of Lenin’s concept of imperialism, focusing on economic factors of capitalism.67 In this Soviet Leninist approach, imperialism was the problem of Western capitalist states, but no longer a problem of Soviet Russia or the Soviet Union. In post- Soviet Russia, too, the view that the Soviet Union presented a challenge to capitalist imperialist powers has been upheld in the scholarship.68

In his book on neo- colonialism, Kwame Nkrumah— President of Ghana in

the period 1960– 1966 and one of Africa’s leading politicians during the pro-cess of decolonization— proceeded semantically from Lenin’s concept of im-perialism.69 Soviet discourse on self- determination of peoples, which was directed to the outside world, was influential and Moscow was quite convin-cing in criticizing the imperialist record of West European colonial empires. The US scholar Adom Getachew has demonstrated how, during the interwar period, the Pan- Africanist movement already drew on and was deeply influ-enced by Lenin’s account of self- determination.70 Several intellectual leaders of this movement were either employed or heavily influenced by the Communist International (Comintern; also ‘Third International’). At the same time, this Moscow- based organization— conceived by the Soviets as a sort of alterna-tive to the ‘capitalist’ (and thus ‘imperialist’) League of Nations— also created

It helped that Soviet Russia and the Soviet Union were globally the first systemic critics of— Western— imperialism and colonialism.

The international lawyer Ntina Tzouvala has offered a critical reading of the

notion of ‘civilization’ in the history of international law. Her understanding of imperialism is essentially also a Leninist one. She refers to imperialism as a specifically capitalist phenomenon and writes of the ‘tendency of capitalism for unlimited expansion’.72 However, the Soviet Union, which was not capit-alist, was still also expansionist. Apart from the expansionist secret protocols of the Nazi– Soviet Non- Aggression Pact of 23 August 1939— which laid claim to the territory of six neighbouring sovereign states, all of which in 1939– 40 suffered Soviet aggression— Soviet Russia’s general declared aim, ever since the 1918 constitution, was that other countries, too, would establish the rule of the proletariat and then evolve into communism.73 As this book will demonstrate in detail and in the context of international law, in practice communism in the Soviet Union became closely intertwined with the Russian imperialist trad-ition and agenda. Thus, conceptually limiting and linking ‘imperialism’ only to capitalism would make us myopic about the imperialism/ expansionism of non- capitalist powers such as the Soviet Union. Therefore, in this study, I understand ‘imperialism’ as an obsession with territory and territorial ex-pansion notwithstanding the predominance of capitalism, socialism, or other economic systems. Apart from this, as we shall soon see, Muscovy started its territorial expansion long before capitalism: during the feudal era.

We can also illustrate this historical conundrum regarding imperi-

alism during the Soviet period with an anecdote from the diaries of Grigory Ivanovich Tunkin (1906– 93), one of the leading international lawyers of the Soviet Union. In June 1964, Tunkin attended a session of the UN International Law Commission in Geneva, and recorded in his diary a breakfast conversa-tion with Manfred Lachs (1914– 93), the representative of Poland, who later became a judge at the ICJ. Lachs told Tunkin: ‘I consider you to be an example. You represent a great power, one of the two presently existing great powers. But I do not feel in your interventions that you defend the interests of a great power

The Polish international lawyer Lachs himself favourably commented on

creation of the Warsaw Pact in 1955.76 What he may or may not have known was that after 1956 Tunkin qua international lawyer had justified the Soviet military intervention against Hungary.77 Later, after 1968, Tunkin also justified the Soviet- led military intervention against Czechoslovakia.78 With the rise of the Solidarność (Solidarity) movement and strikes as well as the proclamation of martial law in Poland by General Jaruzełski, other Soviet international law-yers implicitly threatened Poland that intervention based on the principle of socialist international law (and proletarian internationalism) remained an op-tion on the table for the Soviet Union.79

Russian and Soviet ‘contributions’ to the history of imperialism and colo-

nialism have remained less than visible in global history, including in the con-text of international law. Vasily Klyuchevskii (1841– 1911) and other famous Russian historians who took a favourable view of the Russian colonization of Siberia and other territories in the East took the concept of ‘colonialism’ and in a way turned it around, arguing that Russia had also ‘colonized itself ’.80 The historian Alexander Etkind also concludes that Russia was at different stages of history, in being both ‘colonized and colonizing’.81 However, to an extent the concept of Russia’s ‘self- colonization’ confuses situations in which the Russian

Empire was in a classic way conquering and colonizing neighbouring terri-tories and peoples. Moreover, a remarkable history remains to be explored fur-ther in the context of international law, namely the history of Russia taking established concepts and changing their meaning for its own purposes.

Proceeding from Lenin’s understanding of imperialism, the Soviet Union

presented itself as a vehemently anti- imperialist power. Criticism of old- world imperialism became prominent as a discourse about Soviet Russia exiting the First World War. For this purpose, the Bolsheviks criticized the war as an im-perialist war as well. In the context of international law, Moscow asserted that it was a ‘great power’ (velikaya derzhava), especially after the victory in 1945, though by its nature a good, humanist, and benevolent power, not an imperi-alist one. For example, during the 1950s– 80s, few articles in the leading the-matic Soviet periodicals Soviet State and Law (Sovetskoe gosudarstvo i pravo) and the Soviet Yearbook of International Law do not juxtapose the Soviet Union and its socialist bloc to the (negatively framed) ‘imperialist states’ of the West. A whole genre of specialized literature in the Soviet Union vehemently criti-cized American imperialism in the context of international law.82 During the Cold War, the Soviet self- image sometimes acquired comical propagandistic dimensions in international legal narratives, for example when Soviet legal scholars critically asserted that ‘imperialist’ powers had the proclivity to sys-tematically violate international treaties whereas the Soviet Union itself ‘always fulfilled its treaty obligations and demanded the same from others’.83 Naturally, views can be found in the post- Soviet Russian international law literature which emotionally reject the idea that the Soviet Union could have been an

empire.84 Even today, in the context of Russia’s current war against Ukraine, some authors in the West are still hesitant to put anything more definitive than a question mark to the statement that Russia is an imperialist power.85



Focus on International Legal Discourse as a Method

The main sources of international law comprise treaties and customary law. However, the problem is that these two do not speak to us directly; we need international lawyers as intermediaries. According to Article 38 paragraph 1 p. d of the ICJ Statute, teachings by the most highly qualified publicists of the various nations are merely a ‘subsidiary means’ for determining rules of law. However, the present study is of the positions taken by Russian and Soviet international lawyers and puts consciously at the centre- stage their analysis and discourse about international law.

The focus on discourses and discursive strategies has become popular in

studies of the history of international law.86 It is easy to see why: it enables us to learn what the arguments were about in struggles concerning international law, how international legal disputes played out in dynamic historical settings, and in what directions international lawyers from various powers tried to de-ploy narratives. International law has not been carved in stone. To advance certain types of claims and outcomes, choices were made, and priority was given to some readings of international law as opposed to others. Usually, such arguments were made in contestation with rival powers. This understanding of discourses as something essential for an understanding of international law is influenced by the tradition of critical legal studies, which sees law as indeter-minate.87 According to the critical school, it is the very essence of international law that as a ‘language’ it enables opposing arguments. This perspective is further highlighted by the fact that judicial settlement of international legal disputes has been a relatively marginal phenomenon in Russian and Soviet his-tory. For a moment in time, the Russian Empire promoted Fedor (Fréderic de)

2024, https:// www.monde- diplo mati que.fr/ 2024/ 01/ FEDIU NIN/ 66451.

Martens (1845– 1909) as the ‘chief arbiter of the Christian world’.88 However, Russia’s own disputes with other nations rarely ended up in arbitration or ju-dicial settlement. The Soviet Union further distanced itself from the legally binding settlement of international disputes. It is also in this context that the views of international law writers and scholars become particularly relevant.

As a key method, this study takes seriously— and explores— the discourses

of Russian and Soviet international lawyers. This enables us to see how things were seen and understood, what normative meaning was given to events, not just what happened. International law is a field in which some assumptions in the discourse analysis literature in international relations can be contested. For example, the Danish international relations scholar Lene Hansen high-lights the political importance of ‘official’ discourses: essentially, what govern-ments say. According to Hansen, academic discourses are (at least relatively speaking) marginal compared to ‘official’ discourses.89 Although practices in different countries and traditions may vary, in international law its prom-inent representatives often represent and mix ‘official’ and ‘academic’ angles. Prominent international lawyers advise and represent governments; indeed, international lawyers with a history of working with the government some-times gain distinct visibility and prominence. Since the international judge as such has been of relatively marginal importance in the history of the inter-national law of Russia and the Soviet Union, at least in politically significant cases, international lawyer– academics could speak and write as if they were ‘quasi- judges’ but simultaneously also as advocates and propagandists for their countries. This is particularly so in centralized totalitarian states such as the Soviet Union, where the role of international lawyer– academics was widely seen as helping and defending their country with arguments echoing the government’s foreign policy views and where dissident views about the interpretation of core state interests in the context of international law were not permitted. Beyond the Soviet Union, Russian international lawyers have often been in dialogue with national doctrines and essentially discussed prob-lems of international law with the perspective of how best to protect Rusia’s na-tional interest.90 In this book, I will critically study these semi- official academic

89 Lene Hansen, Security as Practice. Discourse Analysis and the Bosnian War, London: Routledge, 2006, 57.

90 See, eg, the dialogue with Russia’s 2022 Maritime Doctrine and the best plausible claims which Russia can make in the Sea of Okhotsk in: V V Gavrilov, R M Nurimbetov, ‘Istoricheskie prava gosudarstv na morskie akvatorii i pravovoi status Okhotskoga moria’, Moscow JIL No 4, 2022, 44– 55 at 46, 54.

discourses and arguments about international law and their interlinkages with state practice in Russia and the Soviet Union.

We can wonder— if international law is to a significant extent ‘indeterminate’,

plausibly enabling lawyers and diplomats to present opposing arguments— to what extent the history of international law should also be considered ‘indeter-minate’. Sometimes fierce disagreements arise about how properly to approach the study of the history of international law. Contextualist and critical ap-proaches to the history of international law have been juxtaposed.91 Professor Anne Orford of Melbourne University has discussed the tension between ap-proaches by contextualist historians interested in international law and crit-ical international lawyers interested in the past of their field.92 Some historians have critically asserted that international lawyers do not always ‘properly’ tackle the history of their field methodologically and jump too easily to pol-itical conclusions, or perhaps nurture a political agenda before approaching the history of international law in the first place. In her analysis, Orford dem-onstrates that there is no one- size- fits- all school to study the history of inter-national law; indeed, history itself remains a methodologically diverse field. Orford also argues that any approach to the history of international law inevit-ably involves its own politics. Writing ‘objective’ history, perhaps in the sense of the nineteenth- century Prussian historian Leopold von Ranke (1795– 1886), who aimed at writing history ‘as it actually happened’, is nowadays considered not realistic. Orford makes the study of the history of international law challen-ging when she argues that ‘there is [. . .] no method that will establish that our account of facts or our version of truth is the correct one’.93

That may be true about methods but a study of the history of international

law, or indeed any history, must still be based on the premise that historical truth, at least about events, can be established. History in terms of international law cannot be endlessly indeterminate. We must maintain the capacity to point out when states, diplomats, or international lawyers make things up, when they lie about history and facts. On 1 September 1939, Hitler claimed that Poland had attacked Nazi Germany, but we know for a fact that this was not so and that the opposite was the case. The Soviet history of international law is also rich in claims and arguments about history which are demonstrably untrue; for example, that Finland attacked the USSR in 193994 (or at least ‘provoked’ the

Winter War);95 that the Katyn massacre of some 22,000 Polish officers during the Second World War was a Nazi— not Soviet— crime; or that the ‘elections’ which the Soviet Union organized in occupied Estonia, Latvia, and Lithuania in 1940 were genuine and democratic.96 In such historical contexts, there must remain a method to establish that a certain account of facts is correct, even if the interested power or its advocates among international lawyers have main-tained or even continue to vehemently maintain the opposite. Although Soviet foreign minister Vyacheslav Molotov maintained until the end of his life— apparently in the interests of raison d’état— that there were no secret protocols to the Nazi– Soviet Non- Aggression Pact of 23 August 1939,97 these protocols undoubtedly existed; as a matter of fact, Molotov personally signed them in the name of the Soviet Union, while Nazi Germany and the Soviet Union both acted on the basis of the protocols, and the contemporary Russian government has exhibited the Soviet original copy of the protocols at an archive exhibition in Moscow.

The main approach or method adopted in this book is that I connect the main

events in Russian and Soviet state practice of international law— especially re-lated to the status and expansion of territory— with Russian and Soviet inter-national legal discourses. When I refer to international law discourses, then a key empirical element in this book consists in materials published in leading periodicals of international and constitutional law such as the Revue de droit international et de législation comparée (‘the Revue’) in the late nineteenth cen-tury and the journals Sovetskoe gosudarstvo i pravo (in its early decades after 1917 Sovetskoe gosudarstvo i revolyutsia prava and since 1992 Gosudarstvo i pravo), the Soviet Yearbook of International Law in the twentieth century, and the Moscow Journal of International Law in the twenty- first. This amounts to a vast body of material and a significant amount of detail as the number of texts reviewed on international law is quite extensive. Nevertheless, this con-stitutes the central empirical axis and novelty of this book. These Soviet ma-terials and discourses on international law have been insufficiently examined and considered, especially nowadays, decades after the end of the Cold War.98

However, they reveal important aspects: for example, when leading Soviet international lawyers such as Korovin and Tunkin uninhibitedly discussed the consequences of socialist international law.

The journals and yearbooks considered in this book had different audi-

ences. The Revue, which was published in Belgium, was primarily oriented to (French- speaking) European international law elites while the Soviet journals were primarily oriented domestically and to an extent also to other socialist countries. This is fascinating precisely because this is international law as ex-plained by Soviet lawyers to Soviet and socialist audiences. The Soviet journals and yearbooks on international law represented official Soviet discourse and thinking, at least as presented to Soviet citizens. All these materials reveal sig-nificant aspects of both tsarist Russian— in addition to Soviet— claims in and arguments about international law, as well as often revealing the imperial logic behind such claims. It is particularly fascinating to follow exchanges in which an actual debate took place among Soviet international lawyers, for example, on questions regarding the history of Soviet federalism, the state identity of the Soviet Union (compared to the Russian Empire), and the conditions under which the principle of pacta sunt servanda could be overridden under treaty law and philosophy. Usually, the stances taken in such debates also reflect geo-political challenges to the Empire.

The book follows chronological logic. The following elements pertaining to

the fate of the Russian Empire are highlighted in the international legal dis-courses examined. First, Russia’s identity and self- image and its implications for the state’s international legal subjectivity and claims, especially claims re-garding its territory. In the specific terminology of international law, this con-cerns state identity and continuity (or succession), but also how solutions in Russian and Soviet constitutions reflected their understandings. The main in-sight here is that both the Soviet Union and, after 1991, the Russian Federation adopted flexible understandings of state continuity/ succession which ul-timately ended up defending Russia’s imperial state identity and continuity. Second, attention is paid to the nature of treaties and doctrines about their validity and termination in Russian and Soviet practice and writings on inter-national law. Treaties have served as facilitating and fixing Russia’s territorial conquests; but often before conquest could happen, an earlier treaty needed first to be pushed aside— that is, violated. Thus, we will examine how stable pol-itical treaties were in reality and how easy it was to terminate them unilaterally,



gosudarstvo i pravo in: Kaj Hobér, The Perestroika Era and International Law, Edward Elgar Publishing, 2024, 28, 44, 78.

28 IntroductIon

violate them, or ignore them, according to tsarist Russian and Soviet doctrines and theories of international law. Third, attention is paid to specific Russian and Soviet imperial doctrines, justifications, and framings in international law during each period. These are the doctrines and theories that comprise the foundations (or bigger picture) of international law in a certain way that high-lights the indispensable and legally recognized role of ‘great powers’ (velikie derzhavy) in the international community, Russia’s role in maintaining the bal-ance of power, and specific international legal doctrines supporting Russia’s imperial interests such as the doctrine of monarchical legitimacy in the context of the nineteenth century or the theory of socialist international law, used by the Soviet Union during the Cold War.

For readers who are not familiar with the Russian language, I have added

The Russian Empire until the

Crimean War (1853– 6)



‘Restoring’ Muscovy’s Patrimony

Russian historians of international law have shown a special interest in the treaties which Kievan Rus concluded with Byzantium during the ninth and tenth centuries.1 Academic preoccupation with these treaties was particularly marked during the late tsarist period.2 This apparent search for state conti-nuity or pedigree of sorts reflects— besides the historical prestige of Byzantium and Orthodox Christian regionalism3 in Eastern Europe at the time— the idea that Kievan Rus was a Russian state, a predecessor of the later Russian Empire. But was it? Such an exclusive identity may have made sense in the nineteenth century when the territory of the Russian Empire also included Ukraine.4 Of course, for historians of international law, ancient Byzantine treaties with Kievan Rus are interesting in themselves, even without searching for symbolic state continuities.

30 The Russian empiRe unTil The CRimean WaR (1853–6)

In Russian lands, Moscow started to rise under Mongol– Tartar rule (ca

1240– 1480). According to Rais Abdulkhakovich Tuzmukhamedov (1926– 96), a Soviet international lawyer, the Russian Empire and the Soviet Union bor-rowed extensively from the Mongol– Tartar state, the Golden Horde, its prac-tices of empire- building, and its ways of conducting international relations.5 The idea that Muscovy borrowed extensively from the Golden Horde was also popular with Russian Eurasianists during the interwar period, especially Nikolai Trubetskoy (1890– 1938).6 In Muscovy, the aspiration to be a successor of old (Kievan) Rus7 and at the same time borrowings from the Mongol– Tartar Golden Horde also created a unique mixture of expansionist claims and practices.

At the end of the fifteenth century, Muscovy concluded its first written agree-

ments with European states: in 1490 with Austria and in 1493 with Denmark.8 What ‘international law’ meant in those times is debatable as the grand dukes of Muscovy did not yet use this concept regularly in their international com-munication and disputes with other states; nor indeed did the West European states. Muscovy enthusiastically gathered both the lands of ancient Rus as well as other former constituent parts of the Golden Horde. In the words of the US historian Henry R Huttenbach:

Thus, if the rulers of Muscovy marched westward as the descendants of the Roman emperors and the spiritual heirs of the Byzantine emperors, they marched eastward as the successors of the khans of the great Eurasian empire of the Mongols of which they had once been part.9

For example, in the East, Muscovy conquered the khanates of Kazan (1552) and Astrakhan (1556), neither of which had ever belonged to Kievan Rus. Nevertheless, Muscovy justified its conquests with dynastic claims: that these lands on the Volga River were Muscovite patrimony, still ancient lands of Rus; a statement which was ‘proved’ with reference to dynastic claims reaching back to Kievan days. Andreas Kappeler, a Swiss historian and expert on the

formation of the Russian Empire, remains sceptical about the historical truth-fulness of these claims.10 Another renowned historian of Eastern Europe, Serhii Plokhy, argues that on several occasions such claims were based on Muscovite domestic chronicles which were conveniently rewritten on the occasion of new conquests. For example, when Muscovy’s Tsar Ivan III sub-jugated Novgorod— an independent Russian republic— in 1471, the Russian chronicles were rewritten to highlight Muscovy’s continuity with Kievan Rus and its former territorial possessions.11

In 1547, the Muscovite ruler Ivan Grozny proclaimed himself tsar (or

caesar). The most consequential international legal claim by Muscovy over the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries related to the title of tsar and Muscovy’s demand that European states recognize its ruler with nothing less than that specific title. This was on the one hand a claim that Muscovy’s position would not derive from the West, especially from the Holy Roman Empire or the Pope; the title of tsar came from God.12 For example, the Tsars Ivan III and Ivan Grozny rejected the offers of the Emperors of the Holy Roman Empire to recog-nize their title as tsar because the way they were formulated— from Muscovy’s perspective— would have meant symbolically subordinate status vis- à- vis the Holy Roman Empire.13 Fedor Kozhevnikov, who in 1953 became the Soviet judge at the ICJ, summed up the situation in the following way:

Muscovite diplomacy successfully uses legends and historical facts for the juridical foundation for the legality of recognizing the tsar’s title as Muscovy’s rulers.14

One of these legends invoked in the sixteenth- century chronicle Tale of the Princes of Vladimir was that a forefather of Rurik, from whom the Muscovite rulers claimed direct continuity, had been the Roman Emperor Augustus.15

What is particularly relevant in the context of international law is that

the title of tsar was simultaneously also a claim to the lands which Rus had

arguably lost in the past, when Rus came under Mongol– Tartar rule and things fell apart.16 Thus, a very practical element in the Muscovite rulers’ titles of ‘tsar’ and ‘ruler of all Rus’ in practice meant that Muscovy had territorial claims to its Western neighbours— Poland, Lithuania, and Sweden. Muscovy’s struggle for recognition of the title of tsar was simultaneously also a programme for re-covery of lands arguably lost in the past and now ‘occupied’ by its neighbours.17 Lithuanian expansion to the lands of Rus has also been narrated in Lithuanian historical scholarship.18 Based on these ideas of its own historical patrimony, Muscovy did not recognize, for example, the river Ugra— currently located in the Smolensk and Kaluga oblasts of the Russian Federation— as a legitimate border between itself and the Kingdom of Lithuania, even though it served in that function for a while. Because of Muscovy’s territorial pretensions linked to the title of tsar, for a considerable time Lithuania, Poland, and Sweden— Muscovy’s immediate Western neighbours— refused to formally recognize the claim by the rulers of Muscovy to the title of tsar. For example, the title of tsar appeared in the Muscovite version of the treaty of 1549, but not in the Lithuanian version. In 1551 and 1552 the Lithuanian Rada (Senate) and the Muscovite Duma had to negotiate because negotiations between the sover-eigns had become impossible due to Ivan Grozny’s insistence on the title ‘tsar’. The same situation recurred in later decades.19 In the course of the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries, Muscovy protested on every occasion that its rulers’ title of tsar was, in its own view, not adequately respected by European peers in diplomatic correspondence.20

The memory of these distant disputes and conflicts has not disappeared in

contemporary foreign policy discourse. For example, on 15 September 2022, the (then) Twitter account of the Russian Ministry of Foreign Affairs, citing the Ministry’s spokesperson, Maria Zakharova, announced: ‘The Battle of Orsha of 1522 between Muscovy & the then Lithuania is glorified in Lithuania & Ukraine on an annual basis. they want, in the end it all comes down to peace treaties, which fixed Moscow in achieving its goals.’21

At the same time, the tsars of Muscovy occasionally also made it clear to their

main Western neighbours— Poland and Sweden— that they did not consider their rulers equal to themselves. This illustrates an ambition to be the more sig-nificant state; to establish a hierarchy between powers. For example, Tsar Ivan Grozny wrote to the Swedish King Erik XIV that he was as far above Erik as ‘the sky is from the earth’.22 The tsars refused to address Swedish and Polish kings— sovereigns on Muscovy’s Western border— with the characteristic friendly denomination of the time, ‘brother’. Evgeny Korovin (1892– 1964), a leading Soviet Russian international lawyer, wrote that Muscovy’s diplomatic efforts at the time were motivated by the ‘struggle for equal rights, for the dignity of Russia as a great power, its status among other international actors’.23 This was a popular Soviet trope: ‘The whole history of the struggle of rulers of Moscow for the title of tsar and later emperor was a struggle for independence, equality and sovereignty of the state of Moscow.’24 Even the authoritative course on international law of the perestroika era in the Soviet Union emphasized:

[In the sixteenth century] Russia returned to its place among the great powers. [. . .] the Muscovite state never recognized the right of the Roman Emperor to award the tsar’s (royal) power as was done by many West European coun-tries. The smallest protocolary strangulations of Russia’s independence were decisively brought to a stop.25

What is noteworthy in the history politics of this narrative is that Russia only ‘returns’, an understanding of history that justifies various seizures. They are not conquests but the ‘return’ of territories. This trope identifies Kievan Rus and Muscovy with Russia, talking of the ‘restoration’ of a certain great power status but also a territorial reach which Kievan Rus was assumed to have en-joyed among European states before the Mongol– Tartar invasion.

A comparative context of Muscovy’s disputes regarding the title of tsar

with European states was that, before the seventeenth century at least, it was common in Europe to think of states in terms of their rank and hierarchy within

the European community. In Western Europe, in 1504, Pope Julius II pub-lished a regulation which established twenty- eight ranks for states. According to this scheme, the Holy Roman Emperor was followed by the Roman king; the kings of France, Castile, Aragon, and England; and so on.26 As a result, a monarchy’s place in the ranking order was a legitimate concern. The idea of equality of sovereign states, big or small, had not yet been firmly established in international law.

Muscovy’s political publicists in the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries—

among them, Ivan Peresvetov, Maksim Grek, and Yuri Krizhanich— discussed Messianic ideas about the reach of Muscovy’s power far beyond its borders, and in particular the need to liberate Orthodox Christian popu-lations from Ottoman rule.27 The author of the idea that Moscow was the ‘third Rome’, Philotheus of Pskov (Filofei; 1465– 1542), also spoke out against freedom of science and scientific research, even for the tsars,28 thus expressing the opposite to emerging Renaissance ideas in Western Europe. In his history of international law scholarship in Russia, Vladimir Hrabar (also called Grabar) (1865– 1956) discusses these authors when addressing Muscovy’s proto- international legal claims and positions.29 The main point seems to be that Muscovy had convinced itself that it was morally, dynastic-ally, and— simply in terms of justice— entitled to expand its territories or, as it claimed, to restore its ancient patrimony. In this sense, territorial expan-sion was Muscovy’s key international programme in the early modern era, although this was not framed as conquest but as liberation and restoration of rightful patrimony.

In terms of the birth of international legal arguments, Muscovy’s expan-

sion to the Urals and Siberia from the sixteenth century was not accom-panied by any specific claims or debates which would, for example, resemble Spanish theological– moral quasi- legal debates about the conquest of the New World (America). When advancing in Siberia, the Russians were of course confronted with the indigenous population. However, there emerged no Muscovite legal– political reflections à la Francisco de Vitoria (1483– 1546) or Bartolomé de las Casas (1484– 1566) in Spain on whether Muscovy had the

right to conquer the militarily weak and non- Christian natives in Siberia. In contrast, Antony Anghie— international lawyer and a leading critical historian of European imperialism in international law— has emphasized the import-ance of Vitoria’s work for the emergence of European international law and, in the end, for legitimizing European colonial conquests and imperialism.30 Later, the Soviet and even the post- Soviet Russian narrative often highlighted that when Muscovy and later the Russian Empire expanded to regions such as Siberia, the Caucasus, or what is nowadays Kazakhstan, various ethnic groups and regions became part of Muscovy (Russia) ‘voluntarily’. This highlights at least the discursive difference with West European empires, where violent con-quest as such has at least recently been acknowledged. However, the reality of Russia’s conquest in the East was often different from the purported vol-untary nature of its expansion: indeed, the subjugation of populations was a violent and often lengthy process of conquest.31 Debates about agreements concluded in the process of conquest were a part of this phenomenon because Muscovy considered acts of subjugation as final whereas the local rulers some-times did not, seeing— instead of final subjugation to Muscovy— arrangements for protection which could be renegotiated and even reversed, if necessary by rebellion.

In 1654, Muscovy concluded an agreement with the Ukrainian hetmanate—

the Pereyaslav Agreement— which later became a source of intense academic and political debate, particularly between Russian and Ukrainian authors, ar-guing over its exact nature and consequences.32 The Pereyaslav Agreement be-came a subject of memory debates and even memory wars when the question of Ukraine’s relationship with the Russian Empire or the Soviet Union came up. During the Soviet period, and particularly in 1954, the Pereyaslav Agreement was widely celebrated as the act of Ukraine’s voluntary unification with Russia.33 However, during the Russo– Ukrainian war in 2022 a scene was aired on Russian television in which the chairman of the Russian Constitutional Court, Valery Zorkin, brought a French map from the seventeenth century to the Kremlin; in examining it, both President Putin and Zorkin emphasized

In the late seventeenth century, Muscovy expanded, es-pecially at the expense of the Kingdom of Poland. In particular, the Moscow treaty of 1686 turned out highly successful for Muscovy as it expanded by some 2000 square miles.36 As already indicated, in Soviet Russian historiography these territorial changes were not understood as conquests but as Muscovy re-turning original Russian lands to itself.37



The Great Nordic War and Petr Shafirov: Russia’s First

Argument in European International Law

Muscovy started to talk more specifically in language referring to international law when it started the Great Nordic War (1700– 21) against Sweden. The war started when the army of the Russian Tsar Peter I attacked Swedish forces at the battle of Narva in 1700; a battle which the Russians lost. However, at the latest with the battle of Poltava in 1709, the fortunes of war turned: in the end, Russia won the war and gained Ingria (where St Petersburg was founded in 1703) as well as Estonia and Livonia (which comprised most of today’s Latvia). Sweden recognized the transfer of Ingria, Estonia, and Livonia to Russia in the 1721 Treaty of Uusikaupunki (Nystad).38 As a result of this successful war, Peter the Great declared himself Russian emperor in 1721. From then on, the Russian emperors demanded respect for this title from their European peers, just as earlier Russian rulers had demanded recognition of their title of tsar. The leading tsarist- era Russian international lawyer, Fedor Martens, highlighted the importance of the Peace Treaty of Uusikaupunki (Nystad), as only after
