that did Russia have ‘permanent and regular relations with Western Europe’ and become ‘an indispensable member of the European community’.39

The Great Nordic War is an under- studied example of Muscovy’s history

politics and its early use of international law. Essentially, in the context of this war, Russia claimed that everything was the other way round: it was not that Muscovy had attacked Sweden; rather, it framed Sweden as the aggressive party at fault for the war. Later, especially the Soviet historiography of international law echoed the officially endorsed justifications of how Russia was merely defending its ‘vital interests’ in the Great Nordic War at the shores of the Baltic Sea.40 The echo of this Russian and Soviet framing of the history of the Great Nordic War is also reflected in some Cold War- era international law works in the West. For example, William E Butler— American historian of international law and translator of Russian and Soviet works on international law— referred to the ‘war between Sweden and Russia’ (in that order) in his publication of Petr Shafirov’s text on the just causes of the Great Nordic War, even though it is a historical fact that it was Russia that started the war.41 On the other hand, emphasizing Sweden as the ‘symbolically initial’ party of the war even in the title of Shafirov’s book actually well reflects what Russian propaganda at the time wanted Europeans to think. This way of framing wars was later repeated on other occasions in Soviet history: for example, in a radio speech Soviet for-eign minister Molotov referred to the ‘Polish– German war’ when justifying the Soviet army’s entry into Eastern Poland on 17 September 1939.42

Shafirov’s text was written in the Russian language in 1716 but published

only after Russia’s victory in the Great Nordic War, in 1722. It was apparently also the first ever Russian text translated into and printed in English. Since the mid- twentieth century, Shafirov’s treatise has repeatedly been called ‘the first original work on international law in the Russian language’ and also a ‘work dedicated to the problem of just war and the form of its declaration’.43 In other publications, it is simply referred to as ‘the first Russian work on international law’.44 William E Butler also took over the trope from Hrabar and other Soviet authors since the 1940s that Shafirov’s text was ‘the first original work of an

unofficial nature on international law in the Russian language’, and yet Butler also added that Shafirov’s was ‘an isolated and lonely monument, unemulated by another Russian work of comparable scope or originality for many dec-ades’.45 That Shafirov’s text was of an ‘unofficial nature’ is perhaps true simply in the formal sense that it did not go out in the name of Russia as a state but under the abbreviation ‘PS’. However, the person behind this abbreviation, namely Petr Shafirov, was the vice- chancellor of the Russian Empire. Shafirov had been the Russian negotiator of the 1711 Treaty of Pruth with the Ottoman Empire, which involved a very difficult negotiating situation for Russia and Peter the Great’s army. But Shafirov’s career had been far from linear: he was accused of corruption and at some point, Peter the Great even ordered Shafirov’s mock execution; the Tsar’s pardon and Shafirov’s internal exile in a Russian province were announced at the very moment when he expected his execution to be carried out.

As propaganda and justification material, Shafirov’s text was used domestic-

ally in Russia very widely: its 1722 print run produced 20,000 copies and sold at discount prices throughout the Russian Empire.46 Thus, in practice it was an ex post facto justification of Russia’s war aims in the Great Nordic War. In any case, we should examine Shafirov’s text and its arguments more closely. It turns out that Shafirov’s text was less of a ‘work dedicated to the problem of just war and the form of its declaration’47 but more of a justification for violating treaties which Muscovy had previously— before the Great Nordic War— concluded with Sweden. In contemporary terms, we could compare Shafirov’s text with a passionate and politics- laden oral presentation which a state’s diplomatic rep-resentative would present to the ICJ in contentious proceedings; at least, it is a distant predecessor of this genre.

The background for the emergence of Shafirov’s text was that Peter the

Great and Russian diplomacy were facing an image problem in Europe be-cause in 1700 Muscovy had started the Great Nordic War in violation of pre-vious peace treaties with Sweden, especially the Treaty of Stolbovo (1617)48 but also the Peace Treaty of Cardis (Kärde; 1661). According to these treaties, the border between Russia and Sweden was considered settled and was recog-nized by both parties. True, there had been signs of turbulence, as there had

been quarrels over interpretation of the thirty articles of the Cardis treaty and Muscovy had complained of Sweden’s infringements of some of these articles. However, in 1666 Muscovy agreed to make the Cardis treaty ‘permanent’ and to accept Swedish diplomats in the capital, Moscow, on a permanent basis.49 In October 1683 the treaty of Cardis was confirmed, with great pomp and circum-stance, by a Russian embassy in Stockholm, while a Swedish embassy travelled to Moscow to confirm the treaty on behalf of King Charles XI in June 1684.50

Swedish writers such as the King’s official historian, Olaus (Olof ) Hermelin

(1658– 1709), who had experienced Muscovy’s geographical proximity at first hand as a professor in Sweden’s province of Livonia, attacked Russia for having violated these treaties as well as the laws and customs of war.51 Hermelin ques-tioned whether Russia was a ‘civilized’ country, for example writing about Peter the Great:

Er hat ja diesen Krieg nicht erhoben / sein oder seiner Bunds- Genossen Beschimpffung / davon nichts zu hören/ zu rächen / sondern nur sein barbarisches wesen in die manierlich eingerichtete Europäische Länder einzuführen.52

Hermelin concluded that the Russians were ‘Verstörer des Rechtes aller Völker’.53 For the context of these accusations, we should keep in mind that the question of bonne foi was a central one in eighteenth- century European discus-sion on the nature of treaties; it was damaging for the reputation of a ruler for him to be called ‘perfidious’.54

Before becoming a part of Sweden in the sixteenth century, since the thir-

teenth century, Estonia and Livonia had been ruled by the Teutonic Order and bishoprics which were vassals of the Holy Roman Empire, while Denmark had also ruled Estonia in the thirteenth and early fourteenth centuries. The

Teutonic crusader states in the Baltics were stable polities by the standards of their age and had seen both peaceful times and wars and military raids with Muscovy, as well as their Russian neighbours that had later been absorbed by Muscovy, namely the republics of Novgorod and Pskov. Sometimes the Teutonic Order was even the more successful party in these wars and raids, for example under its Master (Landmeister) Wolter von Plettenberg (1450– 1535). Nevertheless, in 1489 the Austrian emperor Friedrich III sent a mis-sion to Moscow which inter alia demanded that the citizens of Pskov should be prohibited from making military raids into Livonia.55 So even if there had been a tribute situation in the eleventh century in parts of pre- German con-quest Livonia— such as in Tartu, which the Kievan prince Yaroslav the Wise conquered in 1030 and maintained for three decades— there had first and fore-most been centuries of Livonian independence from Rus, Muscovy, and other Russian polities. Nevertheless, in his text, Shafirov made the argument that up until their existence in the sixteenth century, the Teutonic Order had been vas-sals of Muscovy, though admitting that they had not fulfilled their duty most of the time. Moreover, the main reason why the Teutonic Order had been in the position of Muscovy’s vassals was because their state had been established on ‘ancient Russian land’.

This argument by Muscovy and Shafirov was not new; it had been made

by Muscovy’s Tsar Ivan Grozny when he started the Livonian War (1558– 83), with the aim of seizing Estonia and Livonia from the Teutonic Order.56 Even then, the Baltic (Livonian) negotiators rejected the notion of any his-torically established tribute but were eventually forced to accept payment of tribute to avoid Muscovy’s impending military attack, a threat which could in this way only be postponed for some years.57 Just as Muscovy initiating the Great Nordic War against Sweden in 1700 was justified in Soviet Russian his-toriography of international law, so was Ivan Grozny’s starting the Livonian War against the Teutonic Order in 1558.58 Moreover, at least as an echo, the historical interpretation that even in 1700 Muscovy was merely interested in ‘ancient Russian lands’ in the Baltics has not disappeared from Russian pol-itical discourse. For example, in June 2022, President Putin, when publicly meeting ‘young scientists and entrepreneurs’ in St Petersburg, compared

Returning to Shafirov’s text, we can further quote from the original English translation of his text:

Accordingly the Bishops and Heer- meisters (Commanders of the Teutonick Order) who governed over the said cities, owned the Russian Monarchs for their supreme Lords, and paid a yearly Tribute to them as Vassals; Concerning which Affair many original Writings and Records are still extant to this Day in the Russian Chanceries at Moskow, Novgorod and Pleskow. [. . .] And though the said Provinces some times, in troublesome Conjectures, de-parted from the Russian Obedience, yet they were reunited to Russia either by treaties or by Force of Arms.60

Then how did Shafirov deal with the Swedish accusation that, by attacking Narva in 1700, Tsar Peter I had violated perpetual peace treaties between Muscovy and Sweden such as the Treaties of Stolbovo and Cardis (Kärde), which recognized Livonia as part of Sweden? One argument was tu quoque and a reminder of the more distant past, namely that during the earlier Livonian War in the sixteenth century, after Muscovy’s initial successes in the war Sweden had concluded a treaty with Muscovy in 1564 which recog-nized Livonia as belonging to Muscovy (‘as belonging to his Czarish Majesty by Inheritance and Property, and being under his Protection’).61 Later on, in 1572, the tsar’s Ambassador to Sweden had repeated the same claims:

the Russian Ambassador maintained their Master’s ancient and just Pretentions to the said Provinces, asserting that the Heermeister had always been his Czarish Majesty’s Subject and Vassal, and paid Tribute to him.62

If— so went Shafirov’s question— previous treaties could be changed by the party who prevailed militarily in the past, then why not during the Great Nordic War? It did not matter so much that some treaties had been called ‘perpetual’.

Shafirov’s other argument was to distance Muscovy from Tsar Boris

Godunov (1552– 1605), under whose regency the Peace Treaty of Stolbovo with Sweden had been concluded in 1617:

so the Usurper little concerned himself about the interests of the Empire, but only made it his Business how by yielding up one Province and Town after the other, he might live in Peace with the neighbouring Powers. [. . .] This con-duct so much incented the whole Nation, but particularly the principal Men of the Empire.63

As a result, ‘[t] his extorted Treaty was concluded between the two Parties, by the Mediation of the Ambassador of England, on the 27th of February, 1616, in the Village of Stolbowa’.64 Shafirov failed to mention that when the Treaty of Stolbovo had been concluded, it was also widely celebrated in Muscovy. For example, the Soviet international lawyer Fedor Kozhevnikov wrote about this event:

The treaty of Stolbovo may not be considered as a mistake of Russian dip-lomacy. To the contrary, in these historical circumstances, peace with the Swedes was necessary. It then freed Moscow’s hands in the struggle with Polish aggression.65

It has been debated to what extent Shafirov’s treatise on Muscovy’s war aims and justifications in the Great Nordic War was about ‘international law’.66 However, references not only to treaties but also to the concepts of ‘law’ and ‘rights’ were present in Shafirov’s argument, for example when he ar-gued that:

Peter I has in Truth had rightful Causes, supported by all the Laws of the World, to recover those Provinces (which) had been torn off and extorted



63 Ibid, 250.

64 Ibid, 269.

65 Kozhevnikov, Russkoe gosudarstvo i mezhdunarodnoe pravo (do XX veka), 2006, 32.

66 Pärtel Piirimäe, ‘Russia, the Turks and Europe: Legitimations of War and the Formation of European Identity in the Early Modern Period’, 11 Journal of Early Modern History 2007, 63– 86 at 64.

from his Empire. [. . .] There were ancient Causes for it, sufficiently justiciable by the Laws of Nature and Nations, beyond all Reproaches of Conscience.67

Shafirov also referred to the law of nations in his criticisms of Swedish conduct of military operations:

We might allege many other Instances, how the Swedes proceeded contrary to the Usage and Customs of War, and the general Law of all civilized Nations.68

The part on the laws of war and accusations of war crimes was particularly top-ical in Shafirov’s narrative because— for example in Estonia and Livonia in the early 1700 s 69 (the Sheremetev campaign) and in Finland (the so- called Great Wrath in 1714– 21)— Muscovy’s military had been accused of widespread mas-sacres against the civilian population in conquered Swedish territory. However, as is typical of the genre of war justifications, Shafirov turned the accusations around and made war crimes appear as if primarily a Swedish practice.

Shafirov’s text ended with an expression of satisfaction that, after having

gained military victory over Sweden, Russia was now the country of which the Swedes were most afraid:

By the Assistance of Almighty God, Russia has become so formidable that we now see a Nation who were the Terror of almost all Europe, vanquished by the Russians. And I dare say, Thanks to God alone, they dread no power whatsoever so much as Russia.70

One of the conclusions based on the actions of Peter the Great in the Great Nordic War and the war pamphlet of Shafirov was that even after solemn treaties establishing the sovereignty of neighbours and a different state of affairs which had lasted for several decades or even centuries, Muscovy could come back with the argument that certain territories outside its borders were in fact ‘ancient Russian patrimony’, which served to justify conquest. Earlier ‘perpetual’ treaties could be cast aside in this process because, for Muscovy, they apparently only re-flected the balance of power and interests at any given historical moment.

44 The Russian empiRe unTil The CRimean WaR (1853–6)

Shafirov’s treatise can be seen as a foundational ‘speech act’ in the Russian

Empire’s relationship with international law; as a starter of the Russian dis-course on international law. With Shafirov speaking, Russia arrived in the European community of nations and its international law, jus publicum Europaeum. Politically, it is fascinating that Russia comes to be perceived as a ‘European’ state and a participant in jus publicum Europeaum only after it beats Sweden militarily and conquers European (and by predominant popu-lation, Western Christian) territories in the Great Nordic War (the Baltic prov-inces). In our time, Shafirov’s justificatory text has been reprinted in Russia with no critical (but only approving) historical commentary, as an early and authoritative example of international legal scholarship and the international legal publicist tradition in Russia.71 It is that, too, but especially concerning the unilateral termination (violation) of earlier treaties, the reading offered above gives a more critical explanation as to in what sense this is the case.



Eighteenth and Early Nineteenth Centuries:

Further Annexations and the Principle of Monarchical

Legitimacy in International Law

In terms of Russian publicist– academic discourses of international law, there is not much to discuss about the eighteenth and early nineteenth centuries. However, this does not mean that the Russian Empire’s role in the practice of international law in Europe was insignificant during these times.

Since the Treaty of Utrecht (1713), balance of power has been formally rec-

ognized as one of the underlying principles of international law in Europe.72 This principle justified military intervention when some monarch’s might be-came too threatening for other European states but it could also serve as a blue-print for voluntary restraint, for example in matters of inheritance between monarchs. In their treaty of alliance, concluded in Amsterdam on August 4 (15), 1717, Russia, Prussia, and France pledged to stand for ‘general silence’ in Europe (Article 5). This reference has been understood in Russian historiography of international law in such a way that since Peter the Great, Russia had become



71 V A Tomsinov, ‘Predislovie’, in: P P Shafirov, Rassuzhdenie, kakie zakonnye prichiny Petr I, tsar’ i povelitel’ vserossiiski k nachatiu voiny protiv Karla XII, korolya shvedskogo, v 1700 godu imel, Zertsalo, 2008, VII– XII.

72 Grewe, Epochen der Völkerrechtsgeschichte, Nomos, 1984, 393– 4. See also Frederik Dhondt, Balance of Power and Norm Hierarchy: Franco- British Diplomacy after the Peace of Utrecht, Brill, 2015; Stella Ghervas, Conquering Peace. From the Enlightment to the European Union, Harvard UP, 2021, 34 et seq.

eighTeenTh and eaRly nineTeenTh CenTuRies 45

an indispensable empire in the European balance of power system.73 With the treaty of Amsterdam, Russia and the other treaty powers undertook to guar-antee the balance of power already expressed in the earlier Treaty of Utrecht.74

There were specific diplomatic events in which the Russian Empire played

a decisive role. Russia mediated peace between Austria and Prussia at the Congress of Teschen in 1779.75 In 1778, war almost broke out between Prussia and Austria over the inheritance of Bavaria; both parties requested Russia’s mediation. In this spirit, the Congress of Teschen was the Russian Empress Catherine II’s initiative. Eventually, Austria abandoned its claim over Bavaria, enabling war between Prussia and Austria over Bavaria to be averted.76 This event has subsequently been highlighted as a successful example of dispute resolution by mediation. Moreover, the Empress played a leading role in establishing the institution of armed neutrality in 1780. This was undertaken during the American War of Independence in the context of attacks by the British Navy against neutral ships.77

However, application of the principle of the balance of power also led to con-

troversial results in Europe, particularly from the perspective of smaller states. In 1772, 1793, and 1795, Prussia, Russia, and Austria partitioned Poland, which in the end ceased to exist as an independent state. The liquidation of Poland as a sovereign state was justified by reasons of balance of power: along with others, that ‘anarchy’ in Poland, due to its constitutional situation, presented a threat to its neighbours.78 However, the partitions of Poland also led to important legal and moral debates in Europe which reveal much about the transform-ations in international law in Europe during the nineteenth century. Several West European international lawyers and publicists criticized the liquidation of an entire state— which had played a very important role in Central and East European history— as an immoral and even illegal act. The nineteenth century witnessed a struggle of ideas between natural law and legal positivism in legal thinking and thus, especially after the French Revolution, the layers of existing law and just (or future) law, lex lata and lex ferenda, or law and morality gener-ally, became conflated in legal– political debates and arguments, as indeed they almost always do. In terms of legal technique, the partitions of Poland were

formalized through treaties between Russia, Prussia, and Austria: the treaties were concluded at St Petersburg.79 Thus, at least Russia could argue— and in fact did argue when Martens and the Russian Ministry of Foreign Affairs pub-lished these treaties in 1875— that the partitions of Poland were accomplished by one of the main instruments of international law: treaties. It was another question whether these treaties were morally defensible.

Shafirov had observed in his time that Muscovy had gained various territories

‘either by treaties or by Force of Arms’.80 Over the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries, treaties continued to play an important role in the enlargement of the Russian Empire. A case in point is the Kingdom of Kartli– Kakheti (Eastern Georgia), whose ruler Erekle II concluded the Treaty of Georgievsk with the Russian Empire in 1783. Kartli– Kakheti had been a vassal state of Persia, a status that Russia had also recognized for Kartli– Kakheti in its treaty with Persia, the Treaty of Resht (1732).81 However, with the Treaty of Georgievsk, things changed and Georgia became a vassal State of Russia.82 The Georgian international lawyer Levan Aleksidze (1926– 2019) characterized the Treaty of Georgievsk as a treaty of submission which deprived Kartli– Kakheti of its full sovereignty.83 At the same time, Kartli– Kakheti remained a specific subject of international law, a vassal state or protectorate (different scholars had different interpretations about the exact classification of this treaty).84 The kingdom’s right to enjoy foreign relations became restricted. Writing in the Soviet Union in 1963, Aleksidze had to navigate the sensibilities of Moscow when character-izing the treaty and the nature of the Russian– Georgian relationship in the past. Aleksidze suggested that Kartli– Kakheti exchanged a ‘coerced dependence on Persia’ for a ‘voluntary’ one on Russia.85 However, in 1800– 1, the Russian Empire annexed the Kingdom of Kartli– Kakheti regardless; a step which Aleksidze, writing during the final years of Khrushchev’s rule in the Soviet

That the Russian annexation of Georgia violated the terms of the earlier Treaty of Georgievsk was also a view shared by the Iranian legal scholar Reza Sardari.87 Nevertheless, reflecting the political limits of his time and situation, Aleksidze further concluded that the Treaty of Georgievsk was ‘objectively a progressive phenomenon’ as it integrated Kartli– Kakheti with Russia, which was ‘more developed’ than Persia or the Ottoman Empire, and ‘put an end to the physical destruction of the Georgian people’.88 Avoiding such legal semantics and intricacies of argument, in 1983 a Soviet postage stamp simply declared the 1783 Treaty of Georgievsk to be ‘the first manifest of friend-ship and brotherhood between the Russian and the Georgian peoples’.

Another Russian territorial acquisition was Moldova. In 1812, the eastern

part of the Principality of Moldova, known as Bessarabia, was united with the Russian Empire. The Principality of Moldova (in Russian: Moldavia) had been a significant state of the medieval and early modern era, particu-larly under Stefan the Great, who died in 1504. 89 However, for several cen-turies before annexation by Russia, the Principality of Moldova had been a vassal state of the Ottoman Empire. The Soviet historiography of international law highlighted that leading Moldovan intellectuals and statesmen such as Nicolae Milescu (1636– 1708) and Dimitrie Cantemir (1673– 1723) advocated for the principality’s dependency on Russia as opposed to Ottoman suzer-ainty because— an apparent paradox— this would help Moldova ‘to preserve its statehood’.90 However, unification of the principality’s territory with the Russian Empire in 1812 meant that Moldova’s statehood was not preserved. Furthermore, with respect to the Ottoman Empire, the historian Will Smiley has argued that the Russian Empire developed certain regional and bilateral norms and practices together with it, particularly concerning the treatment of prisoners of war.91

The Russian– Jewish emigrant scholar Boris Mirkine- Guetzévitch (1892–

1955), who wrote a study on the influence of the French Revolution on inter-national law in Eastern Europe, observed that imperial Russia’s foreign policy was continuously characterized by the desire for territorial expansion, which Mirkine- Guetzévitch, ‘for the sake of brevity’, called the ‘territorial principle’.92 In the nineteenth century, Russia’s preference for territorial expansion be-came intertwined with battling against liberal ideas in European politics, es-pecially the democratic ideas of the French Revolution. During the rule of Tsars Alexander I and Nicholas I, it was the foreign legal policy of the Russian Empire to keep Europe safe for multinational empires ruled by autocratic em-perors. Therefore, St Petersburg promoted the principle of monarchical legit-imacy in European international law and, if necessary, supported intervention in defence of that principle. When the power of a monarch in Europe was threatened by revolutions and popular uprisings, particularly those fuelled by ethno- national sentiment, the Russian Empire worked towards keeping the monarch in power and keeping control over the situation. This policy was par-ticularly marked during the activity of the Holy Alliance in 1815– 30, which was an initiative of the Russian Emperor Alexander I.93 This means that the Russian imperial vision of the international legal order in Europe at that time was one of multinational empires where populations were ruled by a monarch whose legitimacy came from God, but certainly not from the people(s) as democratic legitimacy. In 1848, Russia suppressed the Hungarian Revolution, to defend the principle of monarchical legitimacy in the Austrian Empire. Historians of international law have argued that during the first half of the nineteenth cen-tury and compared to liberal West European powers, a separate stream of more conservative international law flowed in Eastern and Central Europe,94 shaped particularly by the Russian Empire, together with Austria and Prussia.

The period after the Congress of Vienna was also a period in the history

of international law in Europe which saw far- reaching deviations from the premise that (European) states are equal in their sovereignty. On several oc-casions, the great powers of the Holy Alliance made decisions about smaller powers without consulting them.95 This period was one of those in European history in which the status and special powers of puissances principales de

l’Europe (or premieres puissances or puissances du premier ordre) were institu-tionalized, a ‘directorate of great powers’ to which Russia belonged.96

Already in the late eighteenth century, the Empress Catherine II had proudly

declared that she ‘had possessions in three parts of the world’,97 meaning not only Europe and Asia but also America. The 1823 Monroe doctrine of the US, which opposed any European intervention in the Western hemisphere, was inter alia a response to the European doctrine supporting intervention in the name of monarchical legitimacy. There was a possibility, based on what Russia had proposed and what was decided at the Congress of Troppau in 1820, that the Holy Alliance would support military intervention in South America to preserve the ‘legitimate’ colonial possessions of the Spanish and Portuguese empires and monarchs.98 Moreover, with the Ukase of 4/ 16 September 1821, Russia claimed Alaska and, beyond it, further parts of North America, al-though in 1825 it reached an agreement with both the US and Great Britain which reversed at least some of these extensive claims.99 In 1867 the US pur-chased Alaska from the Russian Empire, which was in deep financial need after its defeat in the Crimean War in 1856.

When using jus publicum Europaeum after the eighteenth century, Russia

could take advantage of the fact that international law at the time did not outlaw war, conquest, and annexations. In this spirit, a special role in extending the Russian Empire was played by treaties. However, in practice such treaties were usually only of a mainly temporary quality. That is, when the balance of power tilted further in Russia’s favour, a previous treaty- based arrangement was un-ceremoniously abandoned. For example, in the Treaty of Küçük Kaynarca (1774), Russia and the Ottoman Empire agreed not to intervene in the polit-ical, civil, and domestic matters of the Crimean khanate.100 However, in 1783 Russia seized and annexed Crimea regardless, ‘practically bloodlessly’.101 When a territory had become Russian, it was defended as Russian territory with all the force of military power as well as legal– political arguments; terri-torial retreat was exceptional. The history of international law in the Russian Empire over the eighteenth to nineteenth centuries is simultaneously a history of territorial expansion.102

The Late Russian Empire (1856– 1917)



1870 and the Problem of Clausula Rebus Sic Stantibus

For European international relations and law in the nineteenth century, the Crimean War (1853– 6) constituted a watershed.1 One of the events that pre-ceded the war— the Russian occupation of the Danube principalities, which were under the suzerainty of the Ottoman Empire— was seen as a violation of international law (treaties).2 In the Crimean War, the alliance of England, France, Sardinia, and the Ottoman Empire achieved a military victory over the Russian Empire. The war ended with the Paris Peace Treaty, which inter alia foresaw the neutralization of the Black Sea; Russia was no longer supposed to keep its navy there and committed not to build new fortifications on the northern shore of the Black Sea as well as to demolish existing ones.3 The point in this peace settlement was not only the navy and fortifications as such but whether Russia counted as a great power in the Black Sea region, without limits set by other European powers.

The Franco– Prussian war broke out in 1870. Russia announced on 30

October that year that it would move its navy back to the Black Sea, contrary to the stipulations of the Paris Peace Treaty. When doing so, St Petersburg referred to the fundamental change of circumstances as justification for its disregard of the Paris Treaty. In November 1870, Russia’s foreign minister, Prince Gorchakov, announced that ‘l’Europe aujourd’hui n’est plus celle qui a signé le traité de 1856’.4 Gorchakov argued that other powers, too, had vio-lated the Black Sea neutrality stipulations of the Paris Peace Treaty and that

On the international legal claims and arguments in the Crimean war, see further Hendrik Simon, A Century of Anarchy? War, Normativity, and the Birth of Modern International Order, OUP, 2024, 154 et seq.

new developments in naval technology put Russia at risk; Russia needed to respond. In the twentieth century, Soviet international legal scholars approv-ingly repeated the official line as stated by Gorchakov: it was not Russia but other powers such as England and France which had ‘systematically violated’ the terms of the 1856 Paris Peace Treaty on the neutrality of the Black Sea.5

As a sidenote, it is of interest that, in 2014, Russia’s foreign minister, Sergey

Lavrov, opened a monument to Alexander Gorchakov at the elite MGIMO University in Moscow, which is designed to work in close cooperation with the Russian foreign ministry. Gorchakov became known for his policy circular of 1856 which said: La Russie ne boude pas; elle se recueille (‘Russia is not sulking, she is composing herself ’).6 This phrase of Gorchakov’s has often been ap-provingly referred to in Putin’s Russia and the apparent parallel found here is that, in some periods, Russia has been kept down by historical events, espe-cially by hostile Western powers— but it rises again, through its own strength and determination. In the context of interest to us here, we can take note of the fact that, in 1870, Russia’s ‘thinking things over’ led to the sudden Russian ‘self- liberation’ from the constraints laid down by the Treaty of Paris (1856). However, if Gorchakov and his doctrine and policies were an inspiration for Moscow’s MGIMO University in 2014, then it also cannot be ignored that even in 2008 the Russian Federation had started to challenge post- Soviet borders in South Ossetia and Abkhazia, contrary to Russia’s earlier agreements and her recognition of those borders, and that 2014 was the year of Russia’s annexation of Crimea and the beginning of the war in the Donbas.

The violation of a relatively recent treaty in 1870 triggered a diplomatic

crisis, and the leading European powers gathered for a conference in London to discuss the situation, including the principled question whether a funda-mental change of circumstances could ever constitute an excuse or justifica-tion for violating or bypassing earlier treaty stipulations. Of course, this whole theme did not come up just in 1870– 1; it had been an older issue in the in-terpretation of state obligations as well as in international law literature.7 For example, the English international lawyer Richard Zouche (1590– 1661) cited a diplomatic incident between England and Holland in 1585 in which the pos-ition of Queen Elizabeth I was understood to mean that promises remained in

force only if the underlying state of affairs remained the same, and that parties were not bound by a contract that might cause public injury.8 However, over the following centuries, at least international legal doctrine moved towards strengthening the principle of pacta sunt servanda. This meant that, from the perspective of this fundamental principle, rebus sic stantibus was a problematic exception.

The London Conference was organized in particular at the initiative of

England and Prussia.9 Its sessions took place from 17 January to 14 March 1871. The conference adopted the following declaration:

[I] t is an essential principle of the Law of Nations that no Power can liberate itself from the engagements of a Treaty, nor modify the stipulations thereof, unless with the consent of the Contracting Parties by means of an amicable arrangement.10

This is an important statement in the historical evolution of international treaty law because, essentially, the Vienna Convention on the Law of Treaties (1969) voices the same restrictive approach towards rebus sic stantibus in its Article 62(1). However, its historical context was also that other European powers were ‘reminding’ the Russian Empire of what treaties meant.

In London in 1871, the Ottoman Empire, as the state geographically most

affected by the Russian revisionist move, argued that too little time had passed since 1856 in order for clausula rebus sic stantibus to even be seriously con-sidered.11 At the same time, it is important to note that Russia’s policy to put its navy back in the Black Sea remained a fait accompli; the Russian move worked out in terms of Realpolitik. Just as with the Great Nordic War, the Russian lesson to the world was that things could be accomplished by bold strategic moves and by letting one’s military power (or the threat of it) do the work. One just had to wait for the right time to arrive. What is also interesting is that al-though Russia signed the London Declaration of 1871, in the substance of the matter St Petersburg apparently did not change its mind to the same degree on the question of the political nature of international treaties and how they can be terminated. Russia continued pushing for an interpretation that highlighted

clausula rebus sic stantibus (fundamental change of circumstances) to allow unilateral revision of treaty stipulations. We can even say that during the late tsarist era this view developed into the Russian doctrine on the law of treaties. Moreover, as we will see later, it continued to be a popular position in the Soviet Union and beyond and was always linked with the size of the territory of the Empire. Erlend M Leonhardsen has observed that Russia seemed to be the most frequent invoker of the principle of clausula rebus sic stantibus during both the tsarist and the Soviet periods, usually in the context of shifts in the balance of power.12

During the First Hague Peace Conference in 1899, the Russian delegation

emphasized that any convention adopted by the Conference could restrict the freedom of action of the parties only as long as circumstances remained un-changed. On the other hand, if the circumstances changed, the rights and ob-ligations deriving from a convention would also have to be modified.13 Later Soviet international law scholarship was also fully aware of the fact that in 1899 the Russian Empire proposed to the First Hague Peace Conference a resolution acknowledging a decisive role for the principle of clausula rebus sic stantibus and hence the volatile and dynamic nature of all political treaties.14 However, this was not received with approval by other participating states at The Hague, not least because other European powers had already expressed their opposite views with the London Declaration in 1871. During the Soviet period, the understanding of the Russian history of international law was so intertwined with state propaganda that Kozhevnikov even argued that during the second half of the nineteenth century— precisely when the Russian Empire by its ac-tions triggered the clausula rebus sic stantibus debate in European diplomacy and international law— ‘loyal implementation of international obligations’ was characteristic of Russian foreign policy.15 Writing during the late Soviet pe-riod, one of the foremost Soviet authors on international treaty law, Anatoly Nikolaevich Talalaev (1928– 2001), asserted that during earlier centuries

Martens on International Treaties

The international lawyer formulating Russian official views on this question at the First Hague Peace Conference was Fréderic de (aka Fedor Fedorovich) Martens (1845– 1909), who was usually wearing two hats at the same time during his career. He was an influential legal counsellor at the Russian im-perial Ministry of Foreign Affairs (MFA) and simultaneously Professor of International Law in the Russian Empire’s capital city, at the University of St Petersburg. Whereas Shafirov in the early eighteenth century had been a high official and influential diplomat at the court of Tsar Peter the Great, Martens played the dual role of both international law expert (professor) and coun-sellor in the foreign ministry. Martens’s popular textbook on international law and his polemical articles invoking international law made no mention of his continuous employment at the foreign ministry, but only of his status as Professor of International Law at St Petersburg University. Practical dip-lomacy dominated in his life to the extent that, at least once, Martens con-fided to his diary that he was ‘bored’ with his lectures at the University of St Petersburg after five months of high- profile diplomatic and academic con-ferences in Western Europe.18 In his scholarly writings on international law, Martens defended the legal views and positions which Russia propagated in its international relations, such as in 1870 regarding the earlier Paris Peace Treaty, as well as at The Hague in 1899 regarding international treaties gen-erally. For example, in his influential textbook on international law, first published in 1882, Martens wrote on the nature of international treaties as follows:



16 A N Talalaev, Pravo mezhdunarodnykh dogovorov. Tom 1. Obshchie voprosy, Moscow: Zertsalo, 2011 (Originally, Moscow: Mezhdunarodnye otnoshenia, 1980), 18.

17 Ibid, 23.

18 E R Voronin (ed) Prakticheskoe mezhdunarodnoe pravo i diplomatia F.F.Martensa. Iz dnevnikov, traktatov, konventsii, Moscow: MGIMO- Universitet, 2023, 374.

martens on international treaties 55

The binding force of international treaties not protected by means of enforce-ment is based on the voluntary limitation of their will by those States which enter into the agreement. [. . .] Interest and own advantage force States to con-clude international treaties, and interest and advantage ensure recognition of their being binding. [. . .] As regards the moral force of obligations, its signifi-cance in international relations should not be exaggerated. [. . .] On this basis one may put the rule that the degree of being binding and implementation of international treaties is determined by the extent of their conformity to the real reasonable needs of States and their mutual relations. [. . .] The true reason for the frequent violation of treaties is not a ‘conflict of interest’, but the fact that treaties often are concluded without knowledge and sufficient respect for mutual reasonable relations and advantage of the contracting par-ties. The 1856 Treaty of Paris serves as evidence in those provisions which concerned Russia and ran counter to its basic rights as an independent power. Russia could and had to reject these provisions, and she acted as pre-dicted in advance and was well- known to the Contracting Powers at the Paris Congress.19

Thus, writing in 1882, Martens made his central argument about treaties that Russia’s ‘self- liberation’ from its treaty constraints in 1870 had been legitimate and was covered by realist philosophy on the role of treaties in international law. In the same textbook, discussing international treaties, Martens concluded that clausula rebus sic stantibus was one of the grounds for the termination of treaties.20 He explained further:

With regard to a change of circumstances under which the conclusion of a treaty proceeded, it should be noted that the last is always signed with the tacit assumption that it will have force so long as the conditions under which it was signed remain unchanged. This so- called clausula rebus sic stantibus, to be sure, often concealed an arbitrary violation of treaties and was under-stood in the sense that any change of circumstances entails a legitimate basis for termination of the operation of a treaty. But abuses do not, of course, prove that the rule itself had no significance. It follows from the essence of



19 F F Martens, Contemporary International Law of Civilized Peoples. General Part, translated and edited by W E Butler, The Lawbook Exchange, 2021, 148, 308– 9. (Equally: F F Martens, Sovremennoe mezhdunarodnoe pravo tsivilizovannykh narodov, Moscow, 1995, Vol I, 150, 266– 7; Martens, Traité de droit international, 1883, 513– 4.)

20 Martens, Contemporary International Law of Civilized Peoples, 2021, 290; Martens, Traité de droit international, 1883, 558.

56 the late russian emPire (1856–1917)

international treaties that a State may reasonably oblige itself only in view of a State goal, and any obligation concluded by it exists as long as it is consistent with this purpose. Therefore, the operation of a treaty terminates under such a change of the mutual provision of the parties which makes the said pur-pose unachievable and the further recognition of the treaty places the State in danger.21

Of course, the problem remained how to distinguish between ‘abusive’ and le-gitimate references to clausula rebus sic stantibus. Nevertheless, Martens as-sumed that this was possible; in any case, he maintained that Russia’s use of clausula rebus sic stantibus in 1870 had not been abusive. Just so that there was no misunderstanding about his views on this central topic of international law, Martens made the same point in his diaries as well: if circumstances under-lying a treaty change, the treaty becomes void.22

In the late Soviet period, when the Soviet Union had territorially expanded

and once its borders after World War II and the Helsinki Accords of 1975 seemed settled, the international lawyer Oleg Ivanovich Tyunov (1937– 2017) observed that Martens’s enthusiasm for clausula rebus sic stantibus in inter-national treaty law was characteristic of ‘bourgeois authors’.23 However, we will see later that Soviet authors on international treaty law, such as Evgeny Pashukanis in the 1930s and Vladimir Shurshalov (1913– 1990) in the 1950s, had also been fully approving of Martens’s views on clausula rebus sic stantibus. So apparently the inclination in Russia to favour the argument of clausula rebus sic stantibus was not linked to the views of the bourgeoisie only.

Martens’s argument on clausula rebus sic stantibus goes hand in hand with

the Russian Empire’s state needs after 1870, when Russia restored its status as a great power in Europe. In this sense, these views of Martens were not random scholarly views; they were simultaneously Russian state doctrine, and their support in Russian scholarship was more widespread.24 A concrete ex-ample in Martens’s diaries illustrating this approach to international treaties



21 Martens, Contemporary International Law of Civilized Peoples, 2021, 337. Martens, Sovremennoe mezhdunarodnoe pravo tsivilizovannykh narodov., 1995, 291; Martens, Traité de droit international, 1883, 560– 1.

martens on international treaties 57

came during the Russo– Japanese war of 1904– 1905, which Russia lost. During the Portsmouth Peace Conference in 1905, the head of the Russian delega-tion, Sergei Yul’evich Witte (1849– 1915), was initially hesitant about the for-mulation that Southern Sakhalin would pass from Russia to Japan ‘en pleine souveraineté et à perpétuité’. Martens wrote about this moment in his diaries:

Gr.[af ] demanded that this article and these words be changed. I explained to S. Yu. that these are ordinary words for territorial concessions that mean nothing. If Russia can take back half of Sakhalin, then it will take it, and words will not mean anything. Witte agreed with me.25

Martens’s views on the role of treaties in international law were grounded in extensive empirical– historical research. His international law scholarship paid particular attention to the study of the Russian Empire’s treaties with European powers because, using the MFA archives, he edited a fifteen- volume collection of Russia’s treaties with the Austrian, German, British, and French Empires. Regarding this series, the editorial choices and Martens’s extensive comments to the treaties were sometimes criticized in other European states for telling the history of these European bilateral or plurilateral treaties pri-marily from the perspective of Russian imperial interests and in that sense failing to achieve a balance on the history of these treaties, alliances, motives of the parties, and other aspects. One critic of Martens’s method on international treaties was the Baltic German historian Carl Schirren (1826– 1910),26 whom Martens called in his diary ‘the worst enemy of Russia’,27 perhaps in this case also identifying his own persona with Russia to an extent. For sure, critics of Martens such as Schirren also had their own national aspirations and policies in mind. However, the aspect relevant for us lies somewhere else: the extensive treaty series by Martens reveals that he indeed saw international law primarily as evolving deals between the European great powers. As history evolved, old treaties would be set aside, and new ones would emerge instead. The strong and powerful European states might just become even stronger and more powerful in the process: a kind of survival of the fittest (or, rather, biggest) in the Darwinian sense. This transactional vision of international law and treaties was favourable for the European great powers, which would, even with occa-sional backlashes (such as the Treaty of Paris for Russia in 1856), win most

25 E R Voronin (ed) Prakticheskoe mezhdunarodnoe pravo i diplomatia F.F.Martensa, . . ., 2023, 477.

26 Carl Schirren, Zur Geschichte des Nordischen Krieges. Rezensionen, Walter G Mühlau, 1913, 125– 206.

27 E R Voronin (ed) Prakticheskoe mezhdunarodnoe pravo i diplomatia F.F.Martensa, . . ., 2023, 396. 58 the late russian emPire (1856–1917)

of the time, as they would always be able to count on their superior military power, population, and resources. Using treaties, these empires might just keep expanding, as if by natural laws of history. This was imperialist international law which structurally favoured the stronger parties, empires, at least in its highly dynamic concept of treaties.

Its 1870 self- liberation from the stipulations of the Paris Peace Treaty of

1856 was not the only occasion when the Russian Empire used the clausula rebus sic stantibus argument in the late nineteenth century. The other example was Russia’s unilateral termination of the free port status of Batumi (Batum) on 23 June 1886. Article 59 of the Berlin treaty of 1 (13) July 1878 had ceded Batumi from the Ottoman Empire to Russia but also envisaged free port (porto franco) status for the city,28 which is nowadays part of Georgia. However, by a unilateral act, Russia brusquely ended this arrangement in 1886. Writing after the Second World War, Kozhevnikov argued— not very convincingly— that Article 59 had not been an ‘imperative’ but a ‘facultative’ obligation for Russia.29 However, Russia’s core argument was that the transit situation in the Caucasus had changed to the extent that outside powers could no longer ex-pect to enjoy Batumi’s free port status.30

At the end of the nineteenth century and during the early twentieth cen-

tury, aggressive moves in violation of earlier treaties became increasingly popular among European powers, for example when the Austro– Hungarian Empire annexed Bosnia– Herzegovina in 1909, violating an earlier treaty stipu-lation. Some considered the proclamation of full independence of Bulgaria on 5 October 1908 to be a violation of Article 25 of the Berlin Treaty of 1878, in which Bulgaria’s status was that of a vassal state of the Ottoman Empire.31 International law literature before the outbreak of the First World War dis-cussed Russia’s Black Sea navy move of 1870 and its treaty law conceptual justification as a central and ‘inspirational’ precedent in this regard.32 When Germany violated Belgium’s neutrality at the beginning of the First World War in 1914, the ground had been prepared, as the practice to which the Russian Empire contributed significantly could be interpreted so that fundamental change of circumstances was indeed a legitimate option in international treaty law and practice in order to bypass earlier agreements and obligations. At least

martens: an international lawyer 59

the great powers did just that, and they could refer to Russia in 1870 as a pre-cedent. ‘Those who are able to, are also free to’ (Wer kann, darf auch), as the German international lawyer Erich Kaufmann (1880– 1972) put it in the con-text of clausula rebus sic stantibus, just before the First World War.33 In 1870, just as many times before and after, the Russian Empire could.



Martens: An International Lawyer of a

Semi- peripheral Empire?

The role of Martens as tsarist Russia’s foremost international lawyer goes be-yond his theoretical– diplomatic views on international treaties and his pos-ition that such treaties can be ‘terminated’ (in reality, violated) when state interests demand it. Martens has become a symbol— and the most prominent representative— of an entire era in the Russian Empire’s relationship with international law. However, there are divergent ways to understand his views, role, and impact, which to a large extent reflects how the Russian Empire’s role in the era of Europe’s empires has remained ambivalent in the historiography of international law.

In his account of the history of international law in countries such as Brazil,

Argentina, Japan, China, Turkey, and Russia, Arnulf Becker Lorca has counted Martens among ‘semi- peripheral international lawyers’.34 Becker Lorca sees in the late nineteenth century ‘non- Western nations and states and their law-yers [. . .] using international law to resist and in the process we will see them transforming international law’.35 Becker Lorca also argues that ‘throughout their writings, semi- peripheral internationalists pursued a distinctively non- European interpretation of the classical European law of nations [. . .] to advo-cate for a change in extant rules of international law and to justify the extension of the privileges of formal equality to their own states in their interaction with Western powers’.36

According to Becker Lorca, as a rule, semi- peripheral international lawyers

argued against those ‘Western’ legal scholars who favoured an international legal order based on inequality.37 He also makes a connection between the



33 Ibid.

34 Arnulf Becker Lorca, Mestizo International Law. A Global Intellectual History 1842– 1933, CUP, 2014, 51, 125.

35 Ibid, 15– 16.

36 Ibid, 50.

37 Ibid, 60– 1.

60 the late russian emPire (1856–1917)

views of Martens and Russia’s ‘semi- peripheral’ place in the world (economic) system— as ‘Russia used international law internationally (vis- à- vis Western powers) to secure international relations based on sovereign equality’.38 Becker Lorca further argues that ‘Russia was not at the forefront of the 19th century expansion of European international law’ and adds that ‘to the extent that the world economic system did not consolidate around a division of labor and trade relations that favored Russian interests, 19th century Russia was semi- peripheral’.39

One can debate whether in economic terms the Russian Empire of the late

nineteenth century was ‘semi- peripheral’ or not. For example, in the context of competing for hegemony over Persia, the (British) Imperial Bank of Persia and the (Russian) Discount and Loan Bank were both used for handing out pol-itically motivated loans for the Persian Shah.40 At the same time, Russia itself was heavily indebted, in particular to France, which made the loans look like a pyramid scheme, with Persia owing money to Russia and Russia itself owing even more money to France. In his study on the economic aspects of imperi-alism, the international lawyer Evgeny Pashukanis pointed out that by the end of the tsarist period, French and English capital had taken hold of the Russian Empire and, for example, Russia’s initiative to build the Chinese Eastern Railway in Manchuria was funded by French capital.41 Of course, Pashukanis framed pre- 1917 Russian– West European economic relations in order to jus-tify the Bolshevik decision not to repay tsarist- era loans.

However, when we talk about an international legal order based on equality,

the question remains: is it apt to characterize the Russian Empire as ‘semi- peripheral’ when it systematically used international legal arguments to ex-pand, both in Europe and in Asia, and competed with the British Empire for the title of territorially the biggest empire in the world? The Russian Empire of Martens’ era systematically pursued territorial expansion and positioned itself as a European empire as against the Ottoman Empire and other Asian states. Therefore, Becker Lorca’s concept of ‘semi- peripherality’, although otherwise an insightful one, does not in my view adequately capture Russia’s role during late nineteenth– early twentieth- century European imperialism in international law.



38 Ibid, 123.

39 Ibid, 122.

40 Firuz Kazemzadeh, Russia and Britain in Persia, 1864– 1914. A Study in Imperialism, Yale UP, 1968, 302 et seq.

41 E B Pashukanis, Imperializm i kolonial’naia politika. Chast’ pervaia: ekonomika imperializma. Kurs lektsii, chitannykh v I MGU, Izdatel’stvo kommunistischeskoi akademii, 1928, 80.

martens: an international lawyer 61

One of the assumptions that Becker Lorca makes regarding the place of the

Russian Empire in the history of international law is to think of Europe and the West as synonyms of sorts. Put differently, Becker Lorca posits that inter-national law became an order of global geographical scope ‘throughout the ap-propriation of the European legal tradition by non- Western legal scholars’.42 But by the early twentieth century, Europe and the West were not synonyms; Russian elites often had a self- image of being both European and non- Western. Of course, there were elements of Russia’s semi- peripherality within Europe while ‘Europe’ remained a contested concept in the Russian Empire, par-ticularly after the lost Crimean War (1853– 6). Martens even recounted in his diaries how in St Petersburg, because of their German names and family origins, he and foreign minister Nikolay Girs (1820– 95) were often not con-sidered genuinely ‘Russian’ enough by other people of influence, and how Tsar Alexander III repeatedly told his ministers that he did not care about Europe and would ‘sneeze’ at it, among other derisive comments.43 There were indeed elements of cultural semi- peripherality and alienation in these Russian debates and realities.

I have not been able to find historical evidence for the following interpret-

ation by Becker Lorca:

However, as a consequence of the rise of positivism in European international law, the previous history of legal interactions between European, Russian and Ottoman powers was erased and Russian and Ottoman membership in the international community became contested. [. . .] Facing uncertainty about their membership in the European family of nations, and realizing the perils of possible exclusion, Russian and Ottoman officials sought to assimilate clas-sical international law with an eye towards securing re- entry.44

One anecdotal example of how Russia was not excluded from European inter-national law is that, from its foundation in 1873, Russian legal scholars were included in the membership of the Institut de droit international, with Count Vladimir Bezobrazov (1828– 89),45 Leonid Kamarovsky (1846– 1912), and of course Martens as well. Regarding the topic of the Russian Empire’s place in



42 Becker Lorca, Mestizo International Law, 2014, 23. Cf. also 43 and 50– 1.

62 the late russian emPire (1856–1917)

European international law, we can listen to Martens himself. Writing from the perspective of the 1880s and seeing his own time already bridging natural law and legal positivism, Martens commented that, after 1721, no European ques-tion of great or small importance was decided without Russia.46 He also ob-served that at the end of the eighteenth century, after Russia’s successful wars against the Ottoman Empire and the partitions of Poland, St Petersburg be-came for a while a diplomatic hotspot where Europe’s most important affairs were discussed and often decided.47 Thus, in his time there was no uncertainty about Russia’s continued membership in the European family of nations; even Russia’s defeat in the Crimean War in 1856 did not change this, especially as St Petersburg recovered and expanded its positions quickly, at the latest after an-other successful war against the Ottoman Empire, in 1878.

However, Martens’ Russia may indeed have been somewhat ‘semi- peripheral’

in its cultural– spiritual relationship with Europe and its international legal tradition(s). For example, at least before Martens, Russia’s intellectual contri-bution to the discourse of international law did not correspond to the Russian Empire’s political– military weight and its sometimes decisive role as one of the makers and guarantors of rules in European affairs. There were both West Europeans and Russians (such as Nikolay Yakovlevich Danilevsky, 1822– 85) who emphatically did not consider Russia to be European. When the German philosopher Nietzsche spoke about the Russian Empire with admiration, juxtaposing it with the tradition of Kleinstaaterei— a system of small states; meant pejoratively— in Germany and Europe, it sounded as if the very essence of Great Russia was something other than that of Europe: genuinely imperial.48 At the same time, Russia was decisively not ‘semi- peripheral’ as an imperialist power in the context of (European) international law, and in that sense its situ-ation was not comparable to China, the Ottoman Empire, Brazil, and other non- European countries. Martens made much out of juxtaposing the ‘civilized’ Russian Empire particularly with the ‘uncivilized’ Ottoman Empire, but to an extent also with China. Thus, nineteenth- century Russia was not ‘resisting the Europeans’ or pushing for ‘equality’ with European/ Western powers, or

46 Martens, Traité de droit international, 1883, 138.

47 Ibid, 147.

48 Nietzche wrote: ‘A sort of will, instinct, imperative, which cannot be otherwise than antiliberal to the point of wickedness: the will to tradition, to authority, to responsibility for centuries to come, to solidarity in long family lines forwards and backwards in infinitum. martens: an international lawyer 63

if so then only for imperial equality with the British Empire, its competitor in Asia. Martens argued systematically as a representative of Russia, as an East European, and a Eurasian— indeed, in this sense non- Western— empire which wanted to expand further, especially to the south and east, at the expense, in its own terminology, of ‘half- civilized’ and ‘non- civilized’ nations, such as the Ottoman Empire and China but also the Central Asian khanates and emirates. Moreover, Martens justified and legitimized the Russian Empire’s historical conquests within Europe, especially regarding Poland, and expressed severe criticism against the by then already fashionable idea of self- determination of peoples, seeing what a danger this idea presented to the future of the Russian Empire, along with other European empires.

Becker Lorca observes correctly that some passages in Martens’s textbook

pay homage to the principle of equality of states.49 For example, Martens ar-gues that the status of ‘great powers’ is not rooted in international law, only in international politics.50 At the same time, Martens considers congresses and conferences as ‘organs’ of the international community,51 knowing— and describing— perfectly well what type of powers had played the leading role on such occasions: great powers, including Russia.

Moreover, Martens mildly criticized implementation of the principle of bal-

ance of power in seventeenth- and eighteenth- century European international law.52 At the same time, against his contemporary critics of Russia— including Argentine’s international lawyer Carlos Calvo (1824– 1906)— he defended the Russian Empire’s biggest acquisition in the European history of balance- of- power politics, namely the seizure of most of Poland in the late eighteenth century. Martens asserted that, by the time of writing, Poland was Russia’s ‘in-alienable property’. According to Martens, after the Partitions of Poland and the Napoleonic wars, the other European powers gave Poland to the Russian Emperor Alexander I at the Congress of Vienna in 1815. Moreover, Martens argued that, whatever autonomy the Poles initially had, they lost it after their unsuccessful uprisings against the Russian Empire in 1831 and 1862.53

In the late eighteenth and throughout the nineteenth century, in Russian-

annexed Poland, several major popular uprisings against Russian rule broke out and were violently suppressed by the Russian army. Alexander Suvorov (1729– 1800), one of the most glorified tsarist Russian generals in history, was



49 Becker Lorca, Mestizo International Law, 2014, 192.

50 Martens, Sovremennoe mezhdunarodnoe pravo tsivilizovannykh nardodov, 1995, 382.

51 Ibid, 297.

52 Ibid, 165.

53 Ibid, 314– 16.

64 the late russian emPire (1856–1917)

the suppressor of the Kosciucko- led uprising in 1794 in which thousands of Polish civilians were massacred by Russian troops after the Battle of Praga (a suburb of Warsaw). Later, other European powers, led by England, France, and Austria, protested the methods used by the Russian Empire when suppressing the January uprising of 1863 in Poland.54 Thus, the question of the status of Poland was an important item in European public opinion in which Martens defended this significant imperial Russian acquisition.

On the level of general and emerging principles of international law,

Martens criticized what he called ‘the principle of nationality’, which later be-came known as the right of peoples to self- determination.55 Martens wrote on this in his textbook:

Without denying the impact on international relations of nationality links and agreeing with the duties of every government to respect the rights and interests of all their subjects whatever their national origin and whatever na-tional language they speak, we all the same cannot adhere to the view that the national composition of a State serves to secure order and law in the domain of international relations. Not to mention that the implementation of the principle of nationality should lead to a radical change of the political map of Europe and consequently inescapably cause international wars, we have no guarantee that the ‘national States’ formed will be imbued not only with an awareness of domestic unity but with respect for the legal order in their mutual relations, that they will recognize mutual rights and duties. To see a panacea in the principle of nationality for the settlement of all international difficulties seems to us to be an unfounded and dangerous conviction in its exceptional supremacy over all guiding ideas of our time. The principle of nationality, lacking a historical foundation, creates nothing stable, but is in a position to destroy much. If it were applied to Italy, it does not follow that it can be applied everywhere with benefit for peoples and with respect for inali-enable requirements of truth and justice.56

This was the defence par excellence of the territorial integrity of the multi-national Russian Empire, especially considering its eighteenth- and nineteenth- century European territorial acquisitions, which gave not a thought to the

55 Cf James Summers, Peoples and International Law, 2nd revised edition, Brill, 2014, 155 et seq.

martens as russia’s Colonialist international lawyer 65

nascent ‘nationality principle’. As we shall see next, the scholarly and publicist activity of Martens was equally thought through vis- à- vis non- European peo-ples and territories, of whom the Russian Empire and its leading international lawyer certainly did not talk from the perspective of any equality.57



Martens as Russia’s Colonialist International Lawyer

At the end of the nineteenth century, whenever a current issue arose among the European great powers and Russia’s interests were at stake, Martens wrote pertinent articles in the Revue de droit international et de legislation comparée, the leading international journal of his time. So now, we will go through key messages in these articles, as they also well reflected Russia’s imperial position vis- à- vis ‘non- civilized’ regions neighbouring Russia.

The context of Martens’ first article in the Revue was the ongoing Russo–

Turkish War (1877– 8). This war led to a notable increase in the power of the Russian Empire, although Russia’s initial territorial gains under the San Stefano Peace Treaty were later reduced in the Berlin Treaty, in which other European powers also participated under the leadership of the German Chancellor, Bismarck. Concerning the war of 1877– 8, a lively debate arose in Europe about the legality of Russia’s actions at the beginning of the war.58 In his article in the Revue, Martens’ main point was clear, though: Russia vis- à- vis Turkey was ‘representative of the European concert, a champion of justice and inter-national law’.59 Martens argued that Russia had conducted a purely humani-tarian intervention to defend its fellow Orthodox Christians under Ottoman rule, and it had done so in the name of ‘the whole of Europe’.60 According to Martens, the right to conduct this intervention against Turkey belonged to the whole Concert of Europe but— in the absence of interest in intervening from other European powers— Russia was also entitled to intervene alone, and it did so ‘in the name of the highest interests of humanity and civilization’.61



57 See for further details L Mälksoo, ‘The Legacy of F F Martens and the Shadow of Colonialism’, Chinese JIL Vol 21, 2022, 55– 77.

58 Skordos, Südosteuropa und das moderne Völkerrecht. Eine transregionale und globale Geschichte im 19. und 20. Jahrhundert, 2021, 106 et seq, 125– 30. See also Thomas Erskine Holland, A Lecture on the Treaty Relations of Russia and Turkey from 1774 to 1853, delivered at Oxford on April 28, 1877, London: Macmillan & Co, 1877 and Serge Goriaïnov, La question d’Orient à la veille du Traité de Berlin (1870– 1876), avec une introduction par Boris Nolde, Institut d’études slaves, 1948.

59 F Martens, ‘Étude historique sur la politique russe dans la question d’Orient’, RDILC 1877, 51.

60 Ibid, 57, 66.

61 Ibid, 65.

66 the late russian emPire (1856–1917)

These arguments involved a long backstory, first in the Peace Treaty of

Küçuk– Kaynarca (1774) between the Ottomans and the Russian Empire which made Russia— at least in its own interpretation— the sole protective power of Orthodox Christians in the Ottoman Empire.62 In the Paris Peace Treaty of 1856, this right was in principle ‘collectivized’ in Europe, but with the war of 1877– 8 Russia de facto again took the matter into its own hands. With the sea battle of Navarino (1827), the Russian Empire gained a victory over the Ottoman navy, an event which has sometimes been considered to be one of the first humanitarian interventions (as its justification was to help the Greek insurgents).63 However, it remained debatable in international law doctrine whether humanitarian intervention had become part of international law.64 Nevertheless, in political terms, Russia and Martens used the doctrine of hu-manitarian intervention quite successfully against the Ottoman Empire.

In 1879 Martens published another paper in the Revue on Russia and

England in Central Asia.65 His main goal was to avert an open military clash there between the two countries, particularly in Afghanistan, where the English had gained the upper hand, and which bordered on the former Central Asian khanates of Khiva— which Russia had conquered in 1873, making it a vassal state— and Kokand, which Russia annexed in 1876. Martens argued that both the Russians and the English were charged by divine providence with civilizing the savage peoples of Central Asia.66 Martens addressed the ‘perverted instincts of the Asiatic hordes’67 and pointed out the ‘ignorance, cruelty and anarchy of the fanatical Uzbeks’.68 The main point of Martens’s article was to say that Russia and England, rather than going against each other in Central Asia, should op-erate there together as civilized— and civilizing— powers. The interests of Russia and England in Central Asia were one and the same.69 Martens also explained why a war between England and Russia over Central Asia would not be a good idea: it would only further encourage the less civilized peoples of Asia. For ex-ample, should England win, this could only happen militarily thanks to recruits from India; however, Martens warned that victory would embolden the Indians and give them confidence to eventually turn against their own masters, the

martens as russia’s Colonialist international lawyer 67

English.70 This was a classic colonial discourse in European international law and there was no real difference in the justification for British rule in India and its influence in Afghanistan, as opposed to Russia’s expansion in Central Asia.

Another article by Martens in the Revue, from 1882, concerned the relations

and conflict between Russia and China, in the context of China’s sudden refusal to ratify the Treaty of Livadia of 1879, which the Chinese envoy had signed at St Petersburg.71 According to Martens, China’s actions against Russia were in a way directed against all civilized/ European nations and had China started and even won a war against Russia, this would inevitably have meant that all other Europeans would have been expelled from China as well.72 Going through the history of Russian– Chinese relations— and especially the treaties of Nerchinsk (1689), Kyakhta (1727), and Kouldja (1851)— Martens concluded that one could not ‘judge the Chinese based on the moral ideas and processes of civil-ized nations’.73 Martens then proceeded to assert that the institute of consular jurisdiction in China was an ‘absolute necessity’ until Chinese laws were rad-ically changed.74 In contextualizing the Treaty of Livadia, Martens observed that international law could not be applied in an unlimited fashion towards the Chinese.75 In ‘civilized Europe’, obligations that were taken upon oneself had to be kept— but, according to Martens, this was not the case in Asia and China. Moreover, a common interest bound all European civilized nations, including Russia, in China, ‘to be true representatives of a superior civilization and of an intellectual and moral culture that engenders more benefits than blind submis-sion to the precepts of Confucius’.76

However, another interesting aspect appears in Martens’s discourse about

Russia, China, and international law, in that— so Martens argued— Russia had been a friendlier, gentler neighbour and civilizer to China than the European maritime empires Britain and France.77 For example, unlike West European empires, Russia had historically agreed to subject its citizens visiting China to Chinese jurisdiction. Martens argued that the conflict between Russia and China was essentially a result of how China had been treated by European ‘civ-ilized powers’ other than Russia.78 Indeed, certain differences did exist between

British and French colonial approaches vis- à- vis China on the one hand, and Russian approaches on the other. While Britain in particular was pushing for the opening of Chinese treaty ports, Russia used the same momentum to annex Chinese territory. The 1879 Livadia treaty crisis itself was a crisis regarding the Chinese territory of Ili, which Russia had previously occupied.79 Before that, the Tientsin treaty of 1858, signed between China and Britain after Britain’s threats of war, had revised and extended previously granted European privil-eges and increased the number of treaty ports.80 However, Russia’s own priority was not open ports in China but expansion of territory. For several centuries, the border between the Russian and Chinese empires had been fixed by the treaty of Nerchinsk (1689). In the Far East, near the Amur River, this border had been much less favourable to imperial Russia than the border fixed by the Treaty of Aigun in 1858. Furthermore, with the Treaty of Beijing, concluded in 1860, Russia made gains on the Ussuri river and reached the Pacific Ocean where nowadays the city of Vladivostok (meaning in Russian: ‘rule the East’) is located. China lost further territories with the protocol of Chunguchak (Tarbagatai) in 1864, which dealt with the border in Central Asia. Overall, with these treaties the Russian Empire gained more than 1.7 million square kilo-metres.81 Later, during the era of Nikita Khrushchev in the Soviet Union and Mao Zedong in the PRC, the PRC complained that these treaties were unequal, and the borders had consequently to be revised or compensated between the two socialist nations. Needless to say, Moscow rejected this assertion.82

With the St Petersburg treaty of 1881, the Russians further gained more than

40,000 square kilometres in Central Asia, in the Ili valley region.83 Moreover, in 1895 England and Russia concluded a treaty in which they determined their mutual spheres of interest in the Pamir region. In that way, the Southeastern border of today’s Tajikistan was established without China’s participation and the Chinese Empire lost its control over the Pamirs. In 1911 Russia pushed the border further— in the treaty of Tsitsihar, between Russia’s Aigun and Mongolia (then part of China)— compared to the earlier treaty of Kiakhta of 1727.

martens as russia’s Colonialist international lawyer 69

The imperial interests of Russia— and Martens— were global. To return to

the publicist activity of Martens as the leading Russian imperial international lawyer of the late tsarist era, another conflict of interest concerned Egypt. Instead of the British– French condominium against the backdrop of Egypt’s formal suzerainty under the Ottoman Empire, Martens suggested the format of international (European) administration which would also have given the Russian Empire a share in the governance of Egypt.84 Martens concluded that a compromise between the European powers was needed to reflect their ‘his-torical mission which is to represent true European and Christian civilization in the lands of the East’.85

Martens’s final article on colonial issues published in the Revue concerned

the famous Berlin (Congo) conference of 1884– 5.86 Martens defended the main direction of the Berlin conference decisions on Africa, arguing that the Berlin Act established conditions in favour of the commerce of Europe and America which had to ‘favor the progress of European culture in the dark con-tinent’.87 Moreover, Martens endorsed the political initiatives of the Belgian King Leopold in the Congo:

It is beyond doubt that thanks to the generosity and political genius of King Leopold, the Free State of Congo will be endowed with a regime that will en-tirely conform to the requirements of European culture.88

What his Congo conference article in the Revue did not reveal explicitly was that Martens had also been influential in terms of formulating the Russian for-eign ministry’s legal– political positions for the conference in Berlin. On 26 October 1884, Martens wrote in his diary:

In the Ministry, they think that the Berlin Conference concerns only Africa. But they completely forget that the same principles that the Conference will propose for the occupation of lands not yet occupied by civilized countries will afterwards probably also be applied in Asia and other parts of the world. Our [Russia’s] future will be in Asia, where we will have to build markets for our goods, and not in Europe. In the Pacific Ocean, we must occupy islands
