and lands that have not yet come under the sovereignty of civilized govern-ments. And still the Emperor thinks that the question of occupation does not concern Russia at all!89

On 29 October 1884, Martens indicated in his diary that he had prepared the Russian Foreign Affairs Ministry’s legal memorandum for the purposes of the Berlin conference. In the diary entry for 1 November 1884, he was particularly proud of the way he had managed to influence the attitudes of the Russian for-eign minister Nikolay Girs (1820– 95):

Without self- congratulation I must say that my memorandum opened the eyes of everybody in the Ministry as to the importance of the Berlin Conference. [. . .] Yesterday I wrote to Girs once more a long letter on the Berlin Conference in which I proposed to him the following thought. It is known that the Belgian King Leopold II gave a push to the question of set-tling Europeans on the banks of the Congo and inside Africa. To that end he sacrificed 17 million francs from his own means. For this reason, during the opening meeting of the Berlin Conference decency requires recognition of this great service by King Leopold in support of spreading the fruits of European civilization in Africa. Russia is not interested in African colonies and because of that she may take upon herself the initiative to propose to the Conference to pay homage to the generous activity of Leopold II in Africa.

Girs had only one doubt: does the Belgian King not have any kind of com-

mercial goals and interests in supporting the Association africaine?90 I com-pletely calmed him down, stating convincingly that the King does not have and may not have any interests of such a kind.91

Martens’s enthusiasm about the Belgian King Leopold’s personal project in the Congo was soon rewarded— the King appointed him a member of the Supreme Council of the Congo Free State which had been proclaimed on 25 May 1885. The Supreme Council was composed of two parts, the Court of Cassation and the Court of Appeal. The three foreigners in the Supreme Council— Thomas Barclay, Alphonse Rivier, and Martens— were confirmed in this position by



89 Arkhiv Vneshnei Politiki Rossiiskoi Imperii (AVPRI), Moscow, Martens diaries, Fond No 340, opis no 787; E R Voronin (ed) Prakticheskoe mezhdunarodnoe pravo i diplomatia F. F. Martensa , 2023, 37.

martens as russia’s Colonialist international lawyer 71

Leopold II on 21 August 1889. The Belgian colonial encyclopedia indicates that Martens remained in this position until his death in 1909.92

In his capacity as a member of the Free State’s Supreme Council, Martens

also published a memorandum exploring the nature of sovereignty that the Congo Free State arguably enjoyed.93 Up until its annexation by Belgium in 1908, the Congo Free State was run as a personal dominion of the Belgian King Leopold II. The Berlin Act of 1885 left unresolved certain debatable questions regarding the limits of sovereignty of the Congo Free State, and it was now Martens’s task to tie up the loose ends in terms of constitutional and inter-national law. The main point of Martens’s memorandum was that, in terms of its sovereign rights and authority, the Congo Free State was like any other sovereign state, with the exception of free navigation on the river Congo and its tributaries as well as prohibition of monopolies.94 This narrative was not much interested in what had happened on the ground in the Congo during King Leopold’s rule, especially the numerous crimes committed there in the name of profit and European civilization.95

Martens argued that the Congo Free State had not given up any rights of ex-

ploitation of natural resources on its territory. This state was the owner of the entire territory and the government could profit from its property, inter alia with concessions for payment. Thus, the main idea of the memorandum was to justify the fact that the main profits from the Congo Free State, whether dir-ectly or through concessions, must go to King Leopold II as sovereign. Private investors and foreign nations had to recognize who the new sovereign was and pay their dues accordingly. The point was also that— thanks to the perspec-tive advocated by Martens, namely that King Leopold was a civilized ruler and the Congo Free State ‘a normal state’— the rest of the ‘civilized world’ need not pay extra attention to what was going on there. In other words, Martens’s legal memorandum offered carte blanche to King Leopold’s interests and economic policies in the Congo. Moreover, it has been pointed out that Leopold suc-ceeded only through a specifically international tactic to gain sovereignty over Congo.96 Hence, his engagement of Martens from the Russian Empire was part of this successful internationalizing tactic.

At the same time, Russia as a ‘civilized’ Empire working together with other

‘civilized’ (West European) empires to divide and rule the world— and as constantly appears in Martens’s international legal discourse— was to an ex-tent also a deceptive façade. In his recently published diaries, Martens did not have to pretend and could write as he really thought about his own Russian Empire, himself, and the world. As a Lutheran with Baltic German as well as ethnic Estonian origins,97 he complained about the limits to freedom of reli-gion in Russia and saw in it ‘proof of the ugly and barbaric state of the internal life of modern Russia’.98 His comparison of internal life in France and Russia did not turn out to be in any way flattering for Russia.99 Martens pointed out that Emperor Nicholas II knew perfectly well that, under his rule, individuals could be arrested and held for months and even years without a court judg-ment, under administrative supervision.100 Moreover, at the First Hague Peace Conference Martens was unhappy about the secretive style of Staal, president of the Russian delegation:

It’s terribly amusing that people keep secrets at the end of the nineteenth century— and in a free country. We can ban everything, persecute and des-troy everything and everyone. Not here in Holland.101

In 1894 Martens— who in his textbook had warned of the ‘principle of nation-ality’— criticized the domestic imperial policies of Emperor Alexander III con-cerning the minority peoples of the Empire:

I cannot at all sympathize with the internal policy of the late Sovereign [i.e., Alexander III], which was extremely destructive precisely for the unity of Russia. The systematic persecution of Jews, Finns and Baltic Germans signifi-cantly increased the centrifugal tendencies within the Empire.102

Nevertheless, at the best moments of his career as the Russian Empire’s leading international lawyer, Martens enjoyed far more access and visibility than one might typically expect of international law professors and their publications:

Today I was received by the Emperor to present him with the twelfth volume of my ‘Collected Treaties’. The Emperor was cordially gracious. I allowed my-self to express the idea that we do not need to make haste with our activities in Asia. Our strength lies in the state of things, and Asia must belong to us.103

Martens was not the only Russian international lawyer during the late tsarist period who looked at international law from the imperial perspective and bearing in mind imperial interests, although he was by far the best known. For example, Vladimir Hrabar (1865– 1956) wrote about the equality of states in international law after small states, led by the Brazilian Ruy Barbosa (1849– 1923), clashed with the European great powers at the Second Hague Peace Conference in 1907. Hrabar advocated clearer and more realistic rec-ognition in international law that there could not be full equality between the great powers and smaller states.104 Imperial needs and realities were shaping Russian scholarship of international law in other ways, too. Even then, Kiev’s professor of international law Vasily Andreyevich Nezabitovsky (1824– 1883) and later also Tartu’s (Yur’ev’s) constitutional law professor Léon Challandes (Lev Adamirovich Shalland) (1868– 1919) were discussing the nature of state territory in international legal theory,105 a theme which is of course particularly interesting for empires preoccupied with their own vast territory, and which continued to interest Soviet scholars in the twentieth century and Russian legal scholars beyond.106



Imperialist Practices, Protectorates, and Spheres of Interest

In the late nineteenth century, the Russian Empire fully participated in the imperialist and colonialist practice of European international law, including acquiring territories by treaties. Acquiring some territories took decades and even centuries. For example, in Kazakhstan the first such treaty had been

signed on 10 October 1731 when the Russian representative Aleksei Ivanovich Tevkelev signed a treaty with Abū’l- Khair Khan (1693– 1748) who was leader of the Kazakh Little jüz107 in present- day western and central Kazakhstan. But the process of annexing the whole territory of today’s Kazakhstan was completed only in the 1860s.108 The Soviet legal scholar M A Sarsembaev of Kazakh State University estimated that the treaty between Abū’l- Khair Khan and the Russian Empress Anna Ivanovna (represented by Tevkelev) must be considered an international treaty which was voluntary and not forced upon the Kazakhs.109 It would be interesting to learn more about whether con-temporary Kazakh historians share this viewpoint, and how Kazakhstan’s unification with Russia in the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries is inter-preted today.

As already indicated, Russia also used the institute of protectorates in its ter-

ritorial expansion and imperial encounters. In 1868, a Russian protectorate was established over the Emirate of Bukhara and, in 1873, over the Khanate of Khiva, both in Central Asia.110 Both protectorates were also mentioned in Russian constitutional law treatises of the late tsarist period.111 Russian offi-cial ideology on this is noteworthy (and the more elaborate international legal views of Martens, cited above, directly reflected this state ideology). On 21 November 1864, foreign minister Gorchakov explained it in a circular dispatch to Russian diplomatic representatives abroad:

The position of Russia in Central Asia is that of all civilized States which are brought into contact with half- savage, nomad populations, possessing no fixed social organization. In such cases it always happens that the more civilized State is forced, in the interests of the security of its frontier and its commercial relations, to exercise a certain ascendancy over those whom their turbulent and unsettled character make most undesirable neighbors. [. . .] It is a peculiarity of Asiatics to respect nothing but visible and palpable force.112



107 Jüz (or Juz) were structural divisions within the Kazakh community dating back to post- Mongol times. See Didar Kassymova, Zhanat Kundakbayeva, Ustina Markus, Historical Dictionary of Kazakhstan, Lanham: The Scarecrow Press, 2012, 129 et seq.

imPerialist PraCtiCes and ProteCtorates 75

In 1864, Gorchakov compared this Russian expansion to that of the US (in the West), France (in North Africa), the Netherlands and Britain (in India) and considered it motivated less by ambition than by necessity.113

Establishing a protectorate could be a delicate matter and often ended

up with salami tactics being pursued. Russia’s 1868 treaty with Bukhara hid Article One in a secret supplement, which declared that the emir of Bukhara would pay a war indemnity ‘as a sign of his sincere desire to live in friendship with the Russian Empire and to acquire the protection of His Imperial Majesty the Great Emperor of All the Russians’.114 On 28 September 1873, a new treaty was concluded with Bukhara. Whereas the Khivan treaty established a Russian protectorate over that country by depriving the Khan of control of Khiva’s for-eign relations, the Bukharan treaty preserved the formal sovereignty of the emir’s country.115 However, from 1895, when Russia undertook control and protection of the Bukharan– Afghan frontier, Bukhara may be regarded as a de facto Russian protectorate.116

In 1873, Russia took over Khiva: Article 1 of the Russo– Khivan treaty of 12

August 1873 reduced Khiva to the legal status of a Russian protectorate. The Khan declared himself the ‘obedient servant’ of the Russian Emperor and re-nounced his right to conduct foreign relations:

Saiyid Muhammad Rahim Bohadur Khan acknowledges himself to be the obedient servant of the Emperor of All the Russians. He renounces the right to maintain direct and friendly relations with neighboring rulers and khans and to conclude with them any commercial or other treaties; he will not undertake any military actions against them without the knowledge and per-mission of the supreme Russian authority in Central Asia.117

Article 2 settled the problem of the Russo– Khivan boundary, giving exten-sive territories to Russia (for example, the eastern Caspian shore). Besides a number of valuable commercial privileges, Russia gained control of Khiva’s external affairs and of navigation on the Amu- Darya River. Russian subjects were granted special legal status, while the abolition of slavery in Khiva was also made as a legal commitment to Russia.118 Writing in 1882, Martens

explained that the Caspian Sea had already become ‘a Russian sea’ after con-clusion of the treaty of Turkmenchay with Persia in 1828.119 As for Khiva, Martens highlighted the fact that, in its civilizing mission, Russia had made efforts to stop the slave trade in Central Asia.120 However, from the perspective of Russia’s advances in Khiva and Kokand, Britain— as Russia’s rival empire in the region— pointed out that the Russian government had given pledges not to move further militarily or annex further territories, pledges that were violated in the process.121 In some cases protectorates were also annexed. Between 1868 and 1875 Kokand was politically Russia’s favourite among the Central Asian khanates but in February 1876 Kokand was annexed to Russian Turkestan as the Fergana Oblast.122

Similarities between Russian and Western colonial practices and events were

manifest not only in form but also in abuses. In the Caucasus, imperial Russia’s conquest of Circassia was particularly bloody and ended with the expulsion of the Circassians to the Ottoman Empire in 1864. Sarkisyanz observes that the massacres of the Yomud Turkomans— ordered by Konstantin von Kaufman, the first Governor- General of Russian Turkestan in 1873— and the massacre of the Tekke Turkomans under General Mikhail Skobelev in 1881 accorded with contemporary ‘pacification’ practices in the colonial territories of Western powers.123 For example, on 24 January 1881, the Russians stormed the fortress of Geok- Teppe. Thousands of Tekke Turkomans fled into the desert pursued by Cossack cavalry. In addition to 6500 bodies found in the fortress, 8000 fu-gitives were caught and hacked to death by Russian soldiers; in contrast, only 59 Russians were killed. Firuz Kazemzadeh, the Iranian historian of Central Asia, concludes: ‘Thus was civilization introduced to the Turkomans.’124 In 1898, in the Andijan uprising, some 1500 local armed men attacked Russian forces in a fortification which was formerly part of the Khanate of Kokand. Commenting on this event, a Soviet legal scholar maintained that it was organ-ized by a ‘nationalist clique’ with the purpose of separating Uzbekistan from Russia and bringing it to dependence from the Ottoman Empire.125 Later, a

vast Central Asian rebellion took place against Russian settlers in 1916.126 In sum, the Russian Empire’s encounters in Central Asia were quite typical of the violence of other European colonial empires in their overseas territories.

In the final decades of the Russian Empire, territorial deals and encoun-

ters in Asia increasingly involved other European colonial empires, which had expanded simultaneously. For example, on 27 February 1895, Britain and Russia signed an agreement in London defining ‘the Spheres of Influence of the two Countries in the Region of the Pamirs’.127 In St Petersburg on 31 August 1907, Britain and Russia signed a convention on Persia, Afghanistan, and Tibet. Although the preamble referred to the ‘integrity and independ-ence of Persia’, the treaty carved Persia into three spheres of influence. The northern, and largest, was reserved for Russia. In addition, there were British and neutral spheres.128 During the decade before the Russian revolutions of 1917, the Russian Empire regularly intervened in the political and economic matters of Persia,129 for example in 1907 against the Persian Constitutional Revolution.130 It is of particular interest that Soviet scholars of international law, especially during the interwar period, critically pointed out such facts of tsarist imperialism without inhibition. The Persian– Soviet treaty of friendship of 26 February 1921 also reads as a condemnation of such earlier tsarist pol-icies, for example Article 8 paragraph 1:

Federal Russia finally renounces the economic policy pursued in the East by the tsarist Government, which consisted in lending money to the Persian Government, not with a view to the economic development of the country, but rather for purposes of political subjugation.131

To give another example— based on the interwar narrative of the Soviet inter-national lawyer Evgeny Korovin— Japan and tsarist Russia had four predom-inantly secret treaties: 1907, 1910, 1912, and 1916.132 We should note that the

treaties were concluded immediately after the Portsmouth Peace Treaty which ended the Russo– Japanese war of 1904– 1905. In the 1907 treaty (Izvolsky– Motono), the secret part of the treaty stipulated that Manchuria was divided into two spheres of influence: the northern part to Russia and the southern part to Japan. Russia recognized Japan’s special interests in Korea, while Japan recognized Russia’s interests in Outer Mongolia. The 1910 secret treaty went a step further and— building on the previous spheres of interest— ‘allowed’ Russia to take all necessary measures there. The 1912 secret treaty (Motono– Sazonov) was concluded after the 1911 revolution in China. Again, special interests of both powers in Manchuria and Mongolia were reiterated, but now Inner Mongolia was also divided into two spheres of influence. Furthermore, on 3 July 1916 two treaties were concluded in St Petersburg, one public, the other secret. The secret treaty stipulated that both countries did not want to see any third foreign states in China and would act together against them. In the words of the Soviet international lawyer Evgeny Korovin, the whole of China was now the object of an imperialist treaty between tsarist Russia and imperial Japan.133

Finally, it is also relevant to see how the Russian Empire’s territorial com-

position was dealt with in imperial constitutional law and legal literature on the subject. To start with, Russian constitutional law was somewhat scant, but following the 1905 revolution, on 26 April 1906, the Russian Basic Law was adopted. Its Article 1 declared the Russian state to be unitary and indivisible. However, when for example Baron Boris Nolde (1876– 1948), in the imperial capital St Petersburg, discussed the constitutional law of the Russian Empire, the great majority of his attention went to the history of autonomy as well as au-tonomy claims in the Western border territories of Russia.134 For Baron Nolde, the Russian Basic Law implied clearly: neither the Emperor not the state duma could allow any Russian territory to secede. However, annexing new territories was constitutionally acceptable because adding new territories would not have negatively affected the state unity of the Russian Empire.135 When the Russian emperors had given privileges while conquering or annexing places such as the Ukraine, the Baltic provinces, Georgia, or Finland, these were ‘never re-garded as eternal or unchangeable norms’.136 This attitude went hand in hand with Martens’s view— already discussed— of the changeability of international



133 Ibid, 53.

134 Boris Nolde, Ocherki po russkomu gosudarstvennomu pravu, Tipografia PRAVDA, 1911.

135 Ibid, 251.

136 Ibid, 279.

mandelstam in Paris in 1919 79

treaties as well. Nolde described how initially, when Ukraine was made part of the Russian Empire after 1654, it maintained its autonomy, with its own state organs, budget, and laws.137 However, from the time of Tsar Peter I, the Russian authorities started to curb Ukraine’s autonomy, especially in the context of re-bellions and wars such as the Great Nordic War, in which the Ukrainian hetman Mazepa sided with Sweden. In the time of Empress Catherine II, the elimin-ation of Ukraine’s autonomy within the Russian Empire was completed.138 In the Baltic provinces, too, there had been a treaty (or agreement) about privil-eges and autonomy which Peter the Great concluded with the Baltic German Noble Corporations (in German: Ritterschaften) in 1710; not an international agreement, because the organizations of the Baltic German Barons had not been subjects of international law, but still a binding agreement. Nevertheless, at the end of the nineteenth century, Tsar Alexander III liquidated this agree-ment or quasi treaty- based autonomy in the Baltic provinces— another case of using clausula rebus sic stantibus by the Russian Empire, this time domestic-ally. Thus Nolde concluded that the history of the Baltic provinces in the nine-teenth century was a steady process of replacing local law with the central laws of the Russian Empire.139 In other words, in the mind of Baron Nolde, writing in 1911, the Russian Empire was on its way to becoming at least constitution-ally more unitary. However, it was not long before the First World War and the changes that it brought about shook the foundations of the status quo in the Russian Empire.



Mandelstam in Paris in 1919: the Liberal Swansong of the

Old Russian Empire in International Law

In terms of international legal arguments, the symbolic swansong of the old Russian Empire had already been heard after Tsar Nicholas II abdicated in March (February according to the old Russian calendar) 1917 and the Bolsheviks had killed him together with his family in Yekaterinburg on 17 July 1918. The period between March and November 1917 was a very interesting time in Russia’s international transformation, when the provisional govern-ment tried to introduce reforms that took into account demands for autonomy

137 The French translation of Nolde’s history of Ukrainian engagement with and liquidated au-tonomy in the Russian Empire is: Boris Nolde, L’Ukraine sous le protectorat russe. Traduit du russe par Maurice Gehri, Payot, 1915.

138 Nolde, Ocherki po russkomu gosudarstvennomu pravu , 1911, 322– 9.

139 Ibid, 409.

80 the late russian emPire (1856–1917)

of the peoples of the Empire.140 For example, ethnic Estonians— who had his-torically been split between the two provinces of Estonia and Livonia— were now organized to live in a single united province, for which purpose the northern part of the province of Livonia was attached to Estonia. This was al-ready a Russian Empire without an Emperor: the short- lived Russian republic qua Empire.

In 1917, during the regime of the Russian provisional government, the head

of the legal department of the Russian MFA was Andrei Nikolaevich (André) Mandelstam (1869– 1949), an international lawyer educated in the imperial capital, St Petersburg, inter alia by Martens.141 In 1893, Mandelstam started to work at the Ministry of Foreign Affairs of the Russian Empire. Academically, he specialized initially in private international law and in 1900 at St Petersburg University defended his master’s thesis on the Hague conference and the codifi-cation of international private law, Martens being one of the two opponents.142 When the Bolsheviks came to power in Russia, Mandelstam escaped first to Constantinople and then to Paris. When the Paris Peace Conference convened in 1919, Mandelstam was already living in Paris, and in the context of the peace conference, he wrote a memorandum on whether and how the principle of self- determination of peoples— which had famously been proclaimed by US President Woodrow Wilson— should be applied to Russia.143 Mandelstam’s short answer was that, except for Poland— to which statehood had been prom-ised in Wilson’s fourteen points and which by 1919 had already been recog-nized de facto by the Western allies— no other territory should be allowed to secede from Russia based on the right of peoples to self- determination. For example, Mandelstam maintained:

It seems to us that the absolute independence of Latvia, Lithuania and Estonia gravely endangers the vital interests of Russia and, consequently, creates an international situation which is most dangerous for permanent peace.144

mandelstam in Paris in 1919 81

Mandelstam elaborated that US President Wilson may not have meant the ‘absolute’ right of peoples to self- determination because this would mean not only secession but also that a polity could in principle choose to live out-side the community of nations.145 Following the school of Martens on this issue, Mandelstam emphasized that one had to distinguish normatively be-tween different situations. The first case was trusteeship of civilized peo-ples over non- civilized ones or those with ‘rudimentary civilization’ such as, Mandelstam wrote, the Senegalese, the Moroccans, the Kyrgyz, the Bashkirs, and others. Mandelstam maintained that they did not deserve the same political regime as, for example, the civilized Poles, the Yugoslavs, the Czechoslovaks, or the Finns.146 These nations were ready neither for in-dependence nor even for autonomy. It was in the interests of those peoples themselves that a more civilized nation should exercise trusteeship over their affairs, asserted Mandelstam.

Furthermore, Mandelstam added that the other situation had until recently

been possible, too: that a non- civilized people would exercise power over civilized ones, as had been the case with the Ottoman Empire over peoples which were ‘infinitely superior’ to it: the Greeks, the Arabs, the Armenians, the Syrians.147 Again like Martens, Mandelstam revealed his particularly negative attitude towards the Ottoman Empire. He had been a Russian diplomat there during the First World War and later also wrote about the massacres of the Armenians in the Ottoman Empire.148

The third option, Mandelstam posited, was when civilized peoples exer-

cised power over other (usually smaller) civilized nations, as in the case of the Austro– Hungarian and Russian Empires. But, according to Mandelstam, these two former empires nevertheless deserved entirely different solutions after the First World War, as far as the right of peoples to self- determination was concerned.149 In Russia, he argued, there were many peoples with ‘rudimen-tary civilization’ such as the Kyrgyz, the Bashkirs, the Buryats, the Kalmyks, the Tartars, the Voguls (Mansi), the Ostiaques (Khanty), the Mordva, the Cheremiss (Mari), the Cherkess, the Lesgines, and the other ‘tribes’ of the Caucasus, among others. Their level of development did not merit autonomy



145 Ibid, 26.

146 Ibid, 29.

147 Ibid, 30.

148 See André Mandelstam, Le sort de l’empire Ottoman, Paris: Librairie Payot & Co, 1917; André N Mandelstam, La Société des Nations et les Puissances devant le Problème Arménien, Paris: A Pedone, 1926.

149 Mandelstam, Mémoire sur la délimitation des Droits de’l État et de la Nation d’après la doctrine du Président Wilson , 1919, 33.

82 the late russian emPire (1856–1917)

but trusteeship in the name of the international community— but of course provided by the Russian state.150

Then there were peoples with high levels of education and sense of nation-

hood but who did not yet (or any more) enjoy autonomy in the Russian Empire. The Poles deserved independence but not within the borders claimed by ‘Polish imperialism’.151 Mandelstam argued further that Russia’s loss of Finland would involve the gravest consequences for Russia and, in fact, for Finland itself as well, because of Finland’s inevitable economic feebleness.152 On Estonia, Mandelstam argued at length with Kaarel Robert Pusta (1883– 1964), a member of the Estonian diplomatic delegation in Paris and later Estonia’s foreign minister and member of the Institut de droit international, over their mission to achieve recognition for the Republic of Estonia.153 Mandelstam’s argument was that whatever the Estonians wanted to achieve politically and economically, they could achieve in a federal Russia under the supervision of the League of Nations. In any case, full separation of Estonia would go against the vital interests of the Russian people.154 Lithuania, too, should stay with fu-ture democratic Russia, argued Mandelstam.155

Concerning Latvia’s independence claims, just as Mandelstam rejected the

arguments of Pusta, Estonia’s diplomat in Paris, he similarly rejected the plea for independence by Latvia’s diplomat Zigfrīds Anna Meierovcs (1887– 1925), whose arguments, in Mandelstam’s view, had ‘neither juridical nor political value’.156 The secession of the Baltic provinces of Estonia and Latvia would have grave consequences for Russia’s national security.157

Even more consequential was Mandelstam’s analysis as to why Ukraine

should not be allowed to separate from Russia:

If we rise above the struggles of the moment, if we consider the permanent interests, the eternal ideals of the two peoples, it becomes clear that a com-plete separation can only occur to the detriment of either of the two. [. . .] There is no need for a long demonstration to prove that the independence of Ukraine is equivalent to the death of Great Russia. A glance at the map is

151 Ibid, 38.

152 Ibid, 44– 6.

153 In January 1921, the leading European powers gave collective recognition de jure to the Baltic States.

154 Ibid, 52.

mandelstam in Paris in 1919 83

enough. [. . .] The League of Nations could not grant Ukraine this complete separation unless it denied Russia the right to life.158

Writing in Paris in 1919, Mandelstam concluded that the situation in the former Russian Empire differed ‘radically’ from the former Austro– Hungarian Empire,159 although he failed to specify wherein lay this radical difference. Martens too had argued in 1882 that even if the principle of nationality worked for Italy, this did not mean it was necessary or desirable elsewhere (read: in Russia). Whereas in 1882 Martens’s general argument against self- determination of peoples had, somewhat nebulously, been the ‘inalienable requirements of truth and justice’,160 Mandelstam in his 1919 memorandum before the ‘civilized world’s’ diplomatic community resorted in his closing ar-gument to the special qualities of the Russian soul and literature:

But the Russian people have their own soul, the Russian soul, the soul of Tolstoy, of Pushkin, of Lermontov, of Vladimir Solovev, of Gogol, of Dostoevsky, of Turgenev.161

One could assume that this soul was so special that the Empire was a necessity and secessions were undesirable. This quote also fits well with the post- 2022 debates on whether and how Russian philosophy and literature have historic-ally also contributed to and expressed Russian imperialism (or colonialism), and how often people are unaware of the imperialist views which several of these great writers exhibited. Ukraine under Russian military attack even adopted a law on decolonization concerning symbols of Russian culture in Ukraine. Mandelstam’s argument demonstrates that at least the ‘soft power’ which radiated from the names of these famous writers was also used in the context of international legal arguments and diplomacy, to defend the Empire.

André Mandelstam later became one of the first proponents of human rights

in international law— the rapporteur of the Institut de Droit International’s (IDI) report on human rights in its 1929 New York session, as an international human rights proclamation a predecessor of the Universal Declaration

159 Ibid, 77.

160 Martens, Contemporary International Law of Civilized Nations, 2021, 117. (Martens, Sovremennoe mezhdunarodnoe pravo tsivilizovannykh narodov, 1995, 121; Martens, Traité de droit international, 1883, 196– 7.)

161 Mandelstam, Mémoire sur la délimitation des Droits de’l État et de la Nation d’après la doctrine du Président Wilson , 1919, 80.

of Human Rights.162 The Soviet scholar Evgeny Korovin took note of the fact that in 1921, at the IDI, Mandelstam propagated recognition of the in-dividual in international law, in order to obtain international legal status for Russian ‘counter- revolutionary emigrants’.163 Mandelstam’s opposition to the independence of the Baltic States in 1919 did not prevent him from later be-coming Lithuania’s legal agent in the Panevežys– Saldutiskis Railway Case at the PCIJ, in which judgment was issued on 28 February 1939.164 The other ex-iled Russian constitutional and private international law scholar, Baron Boris Nolde, represented Estonia in this case.

In our context, the overall impression of Mandelstam’s views was that

human rights were entirely compatible with the idea(l) of the integrity of the Russian Empire as well as the hierarchy of peoples based on their perceived ‘civilization’. Indeed, in his understanding, a liberal empire may even have been the most trusted provider of human rights. As an imperial internation-alist, Martens had already indicated in 1882 that rising self- determination claims meant trouble for the Empire. Having lost his homeland, and with the Bolsheviks in power in Russia in 1919, Mandelstam still faithfully continued in this imperial Russian tradition, resisting with his memorandum secessions from Russia.

Nevertheless, another question which international lawyers such as Martens

and Mandelstam must still have asked themselves— even if not in public— was: how realistic was a ‘liberal Empire’ in Russia? The imperial mindset ran very deep and there seemed to be no alternative among leading ‘bourgeois’ Russian lawyers, as indeed was also shown in the international legal views of the Russian community in exile after 1917. For example, writing from exile in Prague in 1923, Mikhail Arturovich Tsimmerman (1887– 1935) narrated how the First World War had been a clash of European empires, including the Russian Empire. However, at least in its German- sponsored Brest- Litovsk treaty version of March 1918, Tsimmerman posited that application of the right of peoples to self- determination (only) to the Russian Empire had been ‘com-pletely artificial and unilateral’.165 Tsimmerman himself had been a prisoner of



162 Helmut Philipp Aust, ‘From Diplomat to Academic Activist: André Mandelstam and the History of Human Rights’, EJIL Vol 25, 2014, 1105– 21; Dzovinar Kévonian, ‘André Mandelstam and the Internationalization of Human Rights (1869– 1949)’, in Pamela Slotte and Miia Halme- Tuomisaari (eds), Revisiting the Origins of Human Rights, CUP, 2015, 239– 66.

war of the German Empire during the First World War and had been an officer in the White Army resisting the Bolsheviks in southern Russia in 1918– 20.166 Extensively discussing the post- First World War peace treaties in Europe, Tsimmerman focused on the major peace treaties such as the Versailles and Saint Germain peace treaties of 1919, as well as the League of Nations, leaving the Soviet peace treaties with those states which had separated from Russia in 1920 completely out of his analysis, as if he did not recognize them, even though the emergence of new ‘small states’ out of European empires was the very subject of his book. Even contemporary Russian historians in exile who otherwise recognize, for example, the illegality of the Soviet occupation of the Baltic States in 1940, such as Andrey Borisovich Zubov (b 1952), argue that the fact that secessionist border states like Estonia concluded peace treaties with the Russian Bolsheviks in 1920 was a ‘moral mistake’.167 However, as the views of Mandelstam also reveal, the Russian ‘White’ movement was not endorsing secessions from the Russian Empire.

At the end of the day, the question remains whether— compared to the

Russian Bolsheviks, to whom we turn next— imperial legal scholars such as André Mandelstam were just more principled and honest in resisting attempts to secede from the Russian Empire. In their view, no territory was permitted to leave the Russian Empire, nor were they willing to make even tactical compromises.

4


From Soviet Russia to the Soviet Union:

The Interwar Period and the

Second World War



Taking a Distance from Tsarist Russia

During the First World War, Russia’s Bolsheviks heavily criticized the war as ‘imperialist’ and made the point that workers across borders had no need for or interest in this war— that the war was being waged in the interests of cap-italists. This was one of their main political slogans when paving the way to-wards power in Russia. They also criticized the tsarist government and again in 1917 the Provisional Government, initially highlighting breaks rather than continuities with their policies and with their way of seeing the world, including in aspects relevant for international law. On coming to power in November 1917, the Bolsheviks issued manifestos such as the Decree on Peace (8 November 1917), the Declaration of the Rights of the Peoples of Russia (15 November 1917) and the Appeal to the Muslims of Russia and the East (3 December 1917), which in their rhetoric stood for a world without annex-ations, endorsed the right of peoples to self- determination up to the point of the right to separate from Russia, and supported the equality of peoples with different cultural backgrounds, European or non- European, Christian or not. The Bolsheviks understood themselves as fiercely ‘anti- imperialist’, a self- understanding that remained central for Soviet discourse until the very end of the Soviet Union in 1991. However, this does not mean that the earlier dis-course of ‘civilized’ and ‘uncivilized’ states was replaced overnight— during the interwar period at least, Soviet legal scholars writing on international and constitutional law occasionally still resorted to distinctions between ‘civilized’, ‘semicivilized’, and ‘uncivilized’ nations,1 just as— for example— their Russian ‘bourgeois’ colleague André Mandelstam had done in Paris in 1919.

1 EA Korovin, Sovremennoe mezhdunarodnoe publichnoe pravo, Moscow/ Leningrad: Gosudarstvennoe izdatel’stvo, 1926, 7; N. Cheliapov, ‘Partia i natsional’nyi vopros’, SGIRP, No 5– 6, 1930, 101– 21 at 103.

Russia, the Soviet Union, and Imperial Continuity in International Law. Lauri Mälksoo, Oxford University Press.

© Lauri Mälksoo 2025. DOI: 10.1093/ 9780198955962.003.0004

Taking a DisTance from TsarisT russia 87

These new understandings also manifested themselves in concrete policies

relevant for international law. For example, when the Bolsheviks accessed the archives of the Russian Ministry of Foreign Affairs, they made public a whole list of (more than a hundred) secret treaties which Russia had concluded with Britain, France, Japan, and other powers both during and before the First World War.2 Nikolay Grigorevich Markin (1893– 1918)— a revolutionary naval officer and agent of Trotsky, the Soviet commissar for foreign affairs— played a distinct role in the process, securing the texts of the secret treaties and helping to make them public. These treaties included designs for further territorial expansion by Russia and its treaty partners: treaties with Britain and France regarding the territories of the Ottoman Empire (Russia was prom-ised Constantinople as a prize for the Allies’ expected common victory in the First World War) and agreements with Japan on the partition of Manchuria and other territories in China. Publishing these agreements involving previous Russian governments had a great propaganda effect and made the point that the Bolsheviks had in mind a quite different understanding of Russia’s national interest than the traditional diplomats of the Romanov Empire and the 1917 Provisional Government.

However, the most immediate situation to tackle was the war itself. In March

1918, the Soviet government signed treaties with the Central Powers— led by imperial Germany— at Brest- Litovsk, ending the war on the Eastern front.3 With these treaties, the Soviet government approved significant territorial losses in favour of Germany. A separate Treaty of Brest- Litovsk was concluded between Soviet Russia and Ukraine, which had emerged, supported by imperial Germany, as a new state in the territory of the former Russian Empire. This also meant that Soviet Russia recognized Ukraine’s independence. However, there was a significant nuance— a twist, even: on 4 (17) December 1917, Soviet Russia had also recognized the ‘independence’ of Soviet Ukraine.4 At least two parallel and competing ideas existed of what Ukraine was meant to be: a pro- Soviet Russian Ukraine or a Ukraine aiming at genuine separation from Russia (but at the same time, geopolitically, also aligned to imperial Germany). As we shall see next, this competition of parallel claims to self- determination— one dependent on, the other antagonistic to, Soviet Russia— arose in most of



2 See M P Iroshnikov, A O Chubarian, Tainoe stanovitsia iavnym. Ob izdanii sekretnykh dogovorov tsarskogo i vremennogo pravitel’stv, Nauka, 1970.

3 See Borislav Chernev, Twilight of Empire: The Brest– Litovsk Conference and the Remaking of East- Central Europe, 1917– 1918, University of Toronto Press, 2017.

4 S M Ravin, ‘Istoricheskii opyt sovetskogo federalizma’, SGP 1957, No 11, 74– 85 at 79. See also B M Babii, ‘Obrazovanie Ukrainskogo Sovetskogo sotsialisticheskogo gosudarstva’, SGP No 3, 1958, 43– 53. 88 from sovieT russia To The sovieT union

the border territories of the former Empire. Soviet Russia appropriated self- determination claims in these secessionist territories to label national self- determination and even statehood as a status which was not meant to denote a genuine transfer of sovereignty from (Soviet) Russia.

In November 1918 a revolution broke out in Germany as well, and on

13 November the Soviet government annulled the Brest- Litovsk Peace Treaties with Germany, the other Central Powers, and Ukraine. The Treaty of Brest- Litovsk— the first Soviet experience in treaty- making under mili-tarily unfavourable conditions and in contradiction to earlier Tsarist Russian commitment to the allies that a separate peace with the enemies will not be concluded— deeply influenced Soviet thinking about treaties, especially pol-itically important treaties. In Soviet scholarship on international law, in any discussion on the law of treaties, it became a standard trope to point out that the Soviet government had every right and legitimacy to annul the Brest- Litovsk Treaty in November 1918. A somewhat trickier question was exactly why or on what legal– philosophical basis this was so. Lenin’s explanation had been that Soviet Russia ‘traded territory’ (parts of the former Russian Empire) ‘to win time’, to consolidate the contested Bolshevik regime in Russia. From the Bolshevik comments, it appeared that they saw the Brest- Litovsk treaties and their concessions to Germany and the other Central Powers— as well as an independent national Ukraine— as tactical, not final. In terms of the bal-ance of power, the Brest- Litovsk treaties were snapshots of the status quo. The Bolshevik expectation was that the communist revolution would soon break out in Central and Western Europe, when everything would fundamentally change in any case.



Reconciling Revolutionary Imperialism with Self- determination

Claims in the Former Russian Empire

When we think of what imperialism means or how it might be perceived by other states or populations struggling for self- determination and statehood, then it is of interest to observe that although Soviet Russia defined itself as a fiercely ‘anti- imperialist’ power, it also wrote into its first constitution of 10 July 1918 that its aim was the victory of socialism in all countries.5 If imperialism means expansion, then Soviet Russia’s disregard for national borders in the



5 E Korovin, Mezhdunardonoe pravo perekhodnoga vremeni, Moscow/ Petrograd: Gosudarstvennoe izdatel’stvo, 1923, 15.

reconciling revoluTionary imperialism 89

name of world revolution was also perceived as expansionist and in that sense quasi- imperialist, at least in the Central and East European states bordering Russia and by ‘bourgeois’ political forces who did not share Bolshevik aims. Peter Kleist, a German scholar writing in 1934, called the Soviet approach ‘revolutionary imperialism’.6 Jean- Yves Calvez, a French legal scholar, wrote in 1953 that the Soviet state— if it still was a state— was by vocation a ‘world re-public’ and by definition an international state.7 One of Lenin’s last wishes was that the Soviet Union would extend further and in his commemorative speech of 26 January 1924 after Lenin’s death, Stalin promised to do exactly that.8

Internationalist transboundary sentiments were also present in the works of

early Soviet international law scholars. As Evgeny Korovin, the first important Soviet international lawyer, put it in 1923:

The international proletariat was always a faithful ally of Soviet Russia. More than once, during the years of its ordeals, with warm and brotherly calls, Russia appealed to them over the heads of bourgeois governments.9

Korovin also pointed out that while the institute of nationality existed in inter-national law, Soviet Russia treated persecuted communists in foreign countries in principle as if they were its own nationals; state borders were insignificant in terms of this class relationship.10 The neighboring powers in Europe and elsewhere perceived this approach by a rhetorically fiercely ‘anti- imperialist’ power as interventionist and expansionist in its own way.

Besides Ukraine already mentioned above, the Bolsheviks were soon con-

fronted with independence declarations by national political movements in territories of the former Empire. For example, Finland proclaimed independ-ence on 6 December 1917. Soviet Russia recognized Finland as a state on 18 (31) December18 1917.11 However, civil war later broke out in Finland and the borders with Russia needed to be determined; the Finnish– Soviet Peace Treaty of Dorpat (Tartu) was concluded on 14 October 1920.

For territories occupied by imperial German forces in 1917– 18 such as

Lithuania, Latvia, and Estonia, the chance to establish their statehood de facto



6 Peter Kleist, Die völkerrechtliche Anerkennung Sowjetrußlands, Ost- Europa- Verlag, 1934, 77. 7 Jean- Yves Calvez, Droit international et souveraineté en URSS. L’évolution de l’idéologie juridique

soviétique depuis la Révolution d’Octobre, Librairie Armand Colin, 1953, 63.

8 Ibid, 55.

9 E A Korovin, Mezhdunardonoe pravo perekhodnoga vremeni, Gosudarstvennoe izdatel’stvo,

11 S M Ravin, ‘Istoricheskii opyt sovetskogo federalizma’, SGP 1957, No 11, 74– 85 at 79. 90 from sovieT russia To The sovieT union

only came when Germany had signed the armistice treaty at Compiègne in November 1918. Based on the conditions of the armistice, the German army had to withdraw from the former Russian Empire’s occupied territories. In most territories in which secessionist political movements had proclaimed independent states— Estonia, Latvia, Ukraine, among others— the Soviets responded by proclaiming parallel Soviet republics of the same titular nation or increased their military pressure if those republics had already been pro-claimed sooner than the Soviet proclamations. For example, although the ‘bourgeois’ Republic of Estonia had been proclaimed in Tallinn on 24 February 1918, Narva in November 1918 saw the Soviets proclaim the Workers’ Republic (Commune) of Estonia, which the Soviet Russian government in Moscow promptly recognized as an independent state on 7 December 1918. War broke out between Soviet Russia and the emerging ‘bourgeois’ Republic of Estonia and lasted more than a year. Only after the ‘bourgeois’ forces prevailed mili-tarily in what Estonia came to see as its War of Independence did Soviet Russia recognize the Republic of Estonia as a matter of law (but also the ‘bourgeois’ government), in the Peace Treaty of Tartu (the Estonian– Soviet Russian peace treaty) of 2 February 1920.

On 22 December 1918 Soviet Russia recognized Latvia and Lithuania, and

on 4 February 1919 Byelorussia, as independent states— but again with the same caveat that it was the Soviet- proclaimed formations that were recognized. However, in Latvia and Lithuania the ‘bourgeois’ governments prevailed as well, and Soviet Russia recognized the Republics of Latvia and Lithuania de jure in 1920. In Byelorussia the Soviet government prevailed and so it was re-integrated in the same political space as Soviet Russia.

From the moment of their creation, the national Soviet republics and for-

mations worked closely together with the Soviet Russian government and re-ceived ‘military aid’ from it, although in fact they were in important ways part of the Soviet Russian government. In the words of the Soviet legal scholar S M Ravin, discussing the history of the Soviet federation: ‘The friendship of the Soviet peoples and their state connections strengthened under the condi-tions of civil war.’12 The Soviet legal scholar B Levin, writing on the occasion of the fortieth anniversary of the Estonian workers’ commune, wrote that the commune testified to the emergence of the first forms of international legal as-sistance between socialist republics, being based on the principles of prole-tarian internationalism.13 Other Soviet legal scholars commented that, once



12 Ibid, 80.

13 B I Levin, ‘Sorokoletie Estliandskoi trudovoi kommuny’, SGP No 11, 1958, 19– 26 at 19.

reconciling revoluTionary imperialism 91

the Soviet government had recognized Soviet Estonia, Latvia, and Lithuania in December 1918, ‘close cooperation was established between them and Soviet Russia in the fight against counterrevolution and foreign intervention’.14 One scholar from Byelorussia maintained that a key feature of the foreign policy of Soviet Byelorussia before the formation of the USSR was that it was ‘carried out under the leadership of the Communist Party, in brotherly union with the Russian SFSR, on the basis of mutual help from all Soviet republics’.15 Professor O I Chistyakov of Moscow State University summed the situation up in the fol-lowing fashion:

Thus on the ruins of the former Russian Empire emerged new independent socialist states. However, the creation of independent Soviet republics did not mean their separation from Russia. To the contrary, its consequence was the unification of the Byelorussian, Latvian, Azerbaijani and other peoples with the Russian people and all other peoples of the Russian Federation at a new, higher level.16

Christyakov also explained how, before the formation of the Soviet Union, other— non- Russian— ‘independent’ Soviet republics outsourced some key functions of their government to Moscow (the new capital of Soviet Russia, instead of St Petersburg):

Federal relations between the independent Soviet republics were at that time quite unique. Their characteristic feature was that all independent Soviet re-publics recognized as common and federal the organs of power and admin-istration of the Russian RSFSR. In that way, the highest organs and law of the Russian Federation featured simultaneously in a double role: they served the Russian RSFSR itself and at the same time the common needs of the federal association of independent Soviet republics.17

The so- called national Soviet republics worked closely together with Soviet Russia; especially in military matters (the Red Army), no genuine distinction existed between them and the Soviet Russian centre.

92 from sovieT russia To The sovieT union

Some years later, on 30 December 1922, the republics in which the Soviets

had prevailed were all also formally merged with Soviet Russia into one state, the Soviet Union. The main insight in this context is that the Soviet Russian government rhetorically supported the right of peoples to self- determination ‘up to the point of separation from Russia’ but in practice this was more a fic-tion or an act of ‘doing things by words’, as it could only be independence of a certain kind acceptable to the Russian Bolsheviks. Soviet Russia recognized the ‘independence’ of other Soviet republics, although dominated over them militarily and exercized otherwise control over them. At the same time, in the territory of the former tsarist Empire, the Red Army fought against ‘bourgeois’ forces who genuinely wanted to separate from Russia. Thus, a principled dif-ference in perspective emerged: what the nationalist ‘bourgeois’ republics saw as their wars of national liberation from (Soviet) Russia, Soviet Russia and local communists framed as class wars in the territory of the former Russian Empire. It is also important to keep in mind that the struggle over the future of the territories of the former Empire was not merely based on ethnicity. The Soviet Russian leadership included a significant number of prominent individ-uals representing non- Russian ethnicities. For example, the first Minister of Justice (and notable Soviet legal theoretician), Pēteris Stučka (1865– 1932),18 was ethnic Latvian by origin. In that sense, there was something genuinely internationalist in the new revolutionary government of Russia, or the conti-nuity of the Empire in a new form.

The same story repeated itself in other border areas of the former Russian

Empire, where nationalist ‘bourgeois’ formations were struggling for power against Soviet Bolshevik formations militarily supported by Soviet Russia. After the Second World War, when the Soviet Union was at its peak level of self- confidence, Soviet legal scholars looked back at the years after the 1917 October Revolution and spoke frankly about the non pro- Soviet Russian self- determination claims in the former Empire:

History demonstrated that Armenia, Georgia and Azerbaijan separating from Soviet Russia depended entirely on foreign states. It is enough to say that the very act of the proclamation of independence of Georgia, Azerbaijan and Armenia in May 1918 was to a significant extent dictated by Germany and Turkey.19

18 See, eg, Petr I Stucka, Die revolutionäre Rolle von Recht und Staat— Theorie 1, Übersetzung und Einleitung von Norbert Reich, Frankfurt: Suhrkamp Verlag, 1969.

19 A Z Begiyan, ‘Obrazovanie sovetskikh respublik Zakavkazia— torzhestvo leninskoi natsional’noi politiki i printsipov gosudarstvennoga suvereniteta’, SGP No 2, 1961, 48– 58 at 49.

sovieT use of TreaTies in The space of The empire 93

Sometimes the unit of self- determination was not so obvious at the begin-ning, at least from the perspective of Soviet policy. For example, in February 1919 the Lithuanian– Byelorussian Soviet Republic was proclaimed but it soon dissolved in July 1919, also because most of Lithuania at the time was already controlled by the Polish army.20 Then the Byelorussian Soviet Republic was proclaimed instead, as a ‘bourgeois’ government had effectively established itself in Lithuania. The Byelorussian Soviet Republic was proclaimed as a counterproject to the ‘bourgeois’ National Republic of Byelorussia, which was proclaimed in March 1918 and, according to Soviet legal scholars, was ‘under the influence of the Germans’ and proclaimed by ‘enemies of the Byelorussian people’.21 The National Republic of Byelorussia soon had to retreat as the Soviets consolidated their power and Moscow restored its influence.



Soviet Use of Treaties in the Space of the Empire

One interesting aspect of Soviet state practice in the early years was the some-what liberal use of the institution of ‘treaties’ in the space of the Empire. Concluding a treaty seems to indicate that the other party is a subject of international law. Indeed, early Soviet Russia had no problem with this as-sociation being invoked in various situations in the territory of the former Empire. Moreover, Soviet Russia wanted to encourage this association: the former Russian Empire had now become a territory of various sovereign— but Soviet— entities which concluded treaties with each other on a formally equal basis. Later, during the final decade of the Soviet period, legal scholars from in-creasingly frustrated non- Russian Soviet republics— such as Levan Aleksidze from Georgia— started to emphasize that the Soviet Union was created through treaty by genuinely sovereign republics. Aleksidze rejected the view of some Soviet constitutionalists from Moscow that even in the period 1918– 22 the bond between various Soviet republics with Soviet Russia was that of con-federation rather than federation.22 By the early 1980s, this historical debate was already a proxy debate about conditions in the Soviet Union and whether other Soviet republics were ‘equal’ to or dominated by Russia. In contemporary Western scholarship, for example, Professor Kaj Hobér of Uppsala University



20 V A Krutalevich, ‘Stanovlenie natsional’noi gosudarstvennosti belorusskoga naroda’, SGP No 12, 1967, 49– 57 at 52.

21 L A Rudzitsky, V A Dorogin, ‘Obrazovanie Belorusskoi SSR’, SGP No 4, 1957, 12– 19 at 16.

22 L A Aleksidze, ‘Znachenie obrazovania SSSR dlia obespechenia mira i mezhdunarodnoga pravoporiadka’, Soviet YBIL 1982, 11– 28 at 16.

94 from sovieT russia To The sovieT union

has supported the view that before 1922 Soviet republics were indeed sover-eign states and treaties between them were international treaties.23

When Soviet Russia concluded ‘treaties’ with other Soviet formations in

the territory of the former Russian Empire, the agenda was to maintain and strengthen close ties with Soviet Russia rather than endorse a genuine seces-sion. The treaties also reflected Soviet Russia’s balance of power with national Bolshevik forces. In this sense, we can speak of a certain specific Soviet Russian appropriation of the language of national self- determination, the format of international treaties, and formal attributes of the sovereignty of non- Russian Soviet republics, all while substantive ties with Soviet Russia were maintained and over time strengthened. It was a compromise of power: places in the im-perial periphery were recognized as ‘national’ and nominally even ‘sovereign’ but Soviet Russia got to maintain very close substantive ties with these other Soviet territories, exercising military– political control. It was more of a legal– theoretical fiction to say that Soviet Russia also developed federal relations with the USSR,24 as Soviet scholars postulated; in terms of power, both were en-tangled. These aspects seem important to highlight considering the opinion— sometimes expressed in Western academic literature— that Lenin’s concept of self- determination was more far- reaching and genuine (‘up to the point of sep-aration from Russia’) than, for example, the designs of US President Woodrow Wilson for the empires defeated in Europe and the Middle East during the First World War.25 Looking at actual Soviet practice, it is doubtful whether Soviet Russian readiness to support the self- determination of non- Russian peo-ples was ever genuine in the sense of accepting their full and unconditional secession.



Reimagining Soviet Russia’s Political Relations in the East

In light of troubles with ‘bourgeois’ liberal aspirations to national self- determination, particularly at the former Empire’s western borders, Soviet Russia increasingly turned its attention eastwards. After the Bolshevik Revolution, non- Bolshevik state formations emerged, for example Kokand

For example, writing in 1948, the Soviet legal scholar Radzhapov characterized Kokand Autonomy in the following way:

The bourgeois ‘Kokand Autonomy’ government like other ‘national’ bour-geois governments in border regions was anti- people, counterrevolutionary, and set for itself the goal of tearing Turkestan away from Soviet Russia.26

On May 1, 1918, Turkestan was declared an autonomous ‘republic’ within Soviet Russia. Moreover, the Bolsheviks came to power in the former tsarist protectorates of Bukhara and Khiva (by then renamed Khorezm, according to the ancient name of Khiva) and Soviet Russia concluded ‘union’ treaties with these workers’ republics, at the same time recognizing their statehood and ‘in-dependence’. The ‘union’ treaty with the Khorezm Soviet National Republic was concluded on 13 September 1920 and the treaty with the Bukhara Soviet National Republic on 20 March 1921.27 In the Treaty of Alliance with Khorezm, the Soviet government

unconditionally recognizes the complete independence of the Khorezm Soviet People’s Republic, with all the consequences stemming from such rec-ognition. For the realization of the slogans for which the Russian workers are fighting, the RSFSR renounces for all time all those rights which were established by former Russian governments with respect to the Republic of Khorezm.28

The Soviet Russian– Bukharan Treaty of Alliance of 20 March 1921 recog-nized Bukhara as ‘completely independent’29 and referred to the two coun-tries’ ‘common struggle and unification of forces against the imperialist world bourgeoisie’.30 These territories, which had been Russian protectorates during the imperial period, were now recognized as new workers’ states but also

According to the treaties, Soviet Russia rep-resented the two republics in their international relations.31 But this was not so different from what the tsarist- era concept of protectorate implied, even though rhetoric had changed, of course. During the 1960s, a wave of scholar-ship in the Soviet Union characterized the marriage of self- determination and Sovietization in Central Asia in the early 1920s:

Soviet Russia took part in the closest way in forming and strengthening Soviet republics in the national border territories, providing them necessary help.32

Some years after the union treaties with Bukhara and Khorezm, these Central Asian polities were formally made part of the Soviet Union, and their terri-tories were reorganized in 1924 according to Soviet nationality policies and concepts. It was during these years and based on Bolshevik decisions that Kazakhstan, Uzbekistan, Turkmenistan, Tajikistan, and Kyrgyzstan were born as Soviet republics. In May 1925, the Uzbek SSR— which at that time included the Tajik ASSR, as well as the Turkmen SSR— 'voluntarily entered the Soviet Union, where they became sovereign members of the Union with equal rights’, as the Soviet legal scholar A Gordienko put it in 1967.33 Here was a strong element of revolutionary nation- making, and even the names of whole nations or nationalities could acquire new meanings over time: for ex-ample, Kazakhs had also been called Kyrgyz in the early twentieth century.34 Again, alternative ideas to the Soviet understandings of self- determination ex-isted: for example, some political forces promoted the idea of a united Turkic republic in Central Asia. The Soviet communist dismissal of this idea was unequivocal:

This proposal was artificial and did not reflect the reality on the ground as in Central Asia, one Turkic nation never existed.35

1920– 1 Peace Treaties at the Western Border of the

Empire and Their Aftermath

In republics where the ‘bourgeois’ nationalist forces prevailed in military conflict with Soviet Russia and the local Bolsheviks (which the narrative of the Soviet government and Soviet legal scholars critically presented as these same ‘bourgeois’ forces having been helped by outside powers such as Britain and France), Soviet Russia concluded peace treaties with those republics in 1920, as well as in March 1921 with Poland (the Peace of Riga).37 Finland and Poland had a unique constitutional history in the Russian Empire, which may explain why the Soviet Russian peace treaties with these states did not include an explicit reference to the right of peoples to self- determination as a ground for their secession. At the same time, the 1920 Soviet Russian peace treaties with the Republics of Estonia, Latvia, Lithuania, and Georgia included pre-cisely this reference as the Soviet acknowledgment of the principle based on which these republics separated from Russia ‘voluntarily and for ever’, as the text of the treaties expressed it.38 Later, the Soviet narrative presented these peace treaties as a generous gift by Soviet Russia which ‘gave’ independence to these new smaller new republics.39 However, it is important to keep in mind that in all of these republics ‘bourgeois’ independence was achieved only after military conflict with Soviet Russia. Several early Soviet international law-yers highlighted their disagreement with the way some ‘bourgeois’ territories separated from Russia. For example, Korovin wrote in 1923 about the post- revolutionary military conflicts and civil war:

The Entente tried— by the hand of Kolchak, Yudenich, Miller, Wrangel, Poland and the Baltic vassals— to strangle the world hearth of revolution.40

At the Genoa diplomatic conference on 20 April 1922, the Soviet Russian dele-gation presented a memorandum according to which, because of the First World War, ‘the Russian people lost huge territories which were important for its state development’.41 These comments did not demonstrate genuine recon-ciliation with secessions from Russia.42 In several aspects, Soviet Russian legal scholars also added a certain conditionality to their interpretation of Soviet peace treaties with ‘bourgeois’ republics such as Poland and Estonia. They emphasized that when Poland was (re- )born as a state and received recogni-tion as a matter of law— at the Versailles Peace Treaty of 1919 but especially in the 1921 Soviet Russian– Polish Peace Treaty of Riga— of borders which in-cluded sizeable Ukrainian, Byelorussian, and Jewish minorities, it was in the context of Warsaw respecting the rights of those minorities. However, Soviet legal scholars early on complained that the Polish government had ‘systematic-ally’ violated those minority rights.43 Second, Soviet internationalists referred to the conditionally formulated mention of future ‘permanent neutrality’ in the Peace Treaty of Tartu with Estonia (Article V), suggesting that for Estonia the pursuit of treaty- based neutrality as mentioned in the peace treaty with Soviet Russia constituted a pactum de contrahendo: a binding legal instru-ment under international law.44 In September 1939, when the Soviet Union demanded that its military bases be established in the territories of the three Baltic States, its negotiators inter alia argued that the Baltic States had not man-aged to remain properly ‘neutral’ when the Second World War broke out. The Baltic States had declared themselves neutral at the beginning of the Second World War, but their neutrality was not treaty- based and in that sense not per-manent. Regarding some other neighbours, especially Afghanistan, during the interwar period the Soviet Union managed to arrange it that the state con-cerned accepted neutral status. For example, on 24 June 1931, the Treaty of Neutrality and Mutual Non- Aggression was concluded between Afghanistan and the Soviet Union; it was extended in 1955, by which time both states were members of the UN.45

shorT life of sovieT TreaTies 99

Short Life of Soviet Treaties in Former Russian Imperial Space

As we have seen, Soviet Russian treaties with other Soviet republics in the ter-ritory of the former Russian Empire highlighted close cooperation with Soviet Russia, especially in military matters and in terms of common communist party politics. These treaties were designed to facilitate reintegration of na-tional Soviet republics with Soviet Russia, which concluded treaties of alliance with the following Soviet republics: Azerbaijan (30 September 1920), Ukraine (29 December 1920), Byelorussia (16 January 1921), Armenia (2 December 1920), Bukhara (4 March 1921), Khorezm (13 September 1920), and the Far Eastern Republic (14 May 1920). A protocol of 22 February 1922 entrusted the RSFSR with representing all these republics at the Genoa Conference.46 During the Cold War, Triska and Slusser, the leading Western researchers of Soviet treaties, came to the following conclusion:

These treaties, which resembled in form international treaties but were in fact closer to constitutional than international law, were agreements concluded for the purpose of furthering the Communist revolution territorially.47

One of the foremost Soviet authorities on international treaty law, Anatoly Talalaev, described the fate of Russia’s treaties with the Far Eastern Republic: by November 1922, the Far Eastern Republic had fulfilled its historical role as a bulwark ‘against imperialist aggression by Japan in the Far East’ and ‘liquid-ated itself ’; it was integrated into the Russian RSFSR on 15 November 1922 and earlier treaties were terminated.48

Soviet Russian treaties with new neighbouring ‘bourgeois’ republics pro-

claimed Soviet recognition of self- determination and nationhood separate from Soviet Russia ‘for ever’. However, the story of the Soviet Russian peace treaty with the (Menshevik- led) Democratic Republic of Georgia proved a sobering one. Professor Levan Aleksidze of Tbilisi University— who was one of the most prominent Soviet international lawyers from non- Russian repub-lics and who in the 1960s had published on the history of Georgian– Russian

treaties— was among the legal scholars who, in the early 1990s, analysed the circumstances of how the Soviet government was established in Georgia, in violation of the Soviet Russian– Georgian peace treaty. Aleksidze co- authored a Georgian governmental report on the takeover of the Georgian Democratic Republic by Soviet Russia in 1921.49

The story itself was the following. On 26 May 1918, the National Council

of Georgia proclaimed the independence of Georgia. On 7 May 1920, Soviet Russia concluded a peace treaty with the Democratic Republic of Georgia, which also implied Soviet Russia’s recognition of Georgia’s independence as a matter of law, based on the right of peoples to self- determination (as in a previous Soviet Russian treaty with the Republic of Estonia). The Georgian– Soviet Russian treaty also had a ‘secret supplement’ in which the government of Georgia pledged not to create any obstacles to communist propaganda and activities in the country. However, in one of the first academic monographs on international law in the Soviet Union, published in 1923, the Soviet Russian international lawyer Evgeny Korovin made public the content of this ‘secret supplement’.50 In 1920 and 1921, Georgia’s independence was recognized by Germany, Belgium, France, Great Britain, Italy, Japan, Poland, Austria, Romania, Luxembourg, and Mexico. The Democratic Republic of Georgia was in the process of becoming a member of the League of Nations in Geneva. However, on 25 February 1921, the Soviet Army entered the Georgian capital Tbilisi and established control over Georgia. Military operations in Georgia ended in the second half of March 1921 and the government of the Democratic Republic of Georgia was forced into exile.51 Thus, at the end of the day the solemn treaty between Georgia and Soviet Russia concluded on 7 May 1920, with references to self- determination and recognizing the independence of the Democratic Republic of Georgia, meant little, as within less than a year from conclusion of the treaty the Soviet Russian military took control of Georgia anyway, and the Bolsheviks Sovietized Georgia. What mattered was the actual and changing balance of forces, not solemn promises in a treaty. On the level of principles, too, Korovin, in his 1923 treatise on international law, postulated that international law had always been based on the balance of power.52 Later,

the Soviet Georgian legal scholar V Parkosadze admitted that suppressing the Menshevik- led Democratic Republic of Georgia had not been an easy task:

In Georgia, crushing the bourgeois– landowners’ state apparatus, both at the center as well as in the provinces, happened in quite complicated conditions because the influence of Menshevik and petty bourgeois elements was still quite strong.53

In Armenia,54 the main competitor of the Bolsheviks was the national party of Dashnaktsuyun, colloquially known as the Dashnaks. In February 1921 they seized power in Armenia but, in the words of a Soviet Armenian legal scholar:

The Red Army defended the independence of not only the Russian people but also of other peoples of Russia. [. . .] The Russian people and its Red Army gave brotherly assistance to Armenia. With the help of the Red Army, on 2 April 1921 Soviet power was restored in the capital of Armenia.55

On 12 March 1922, a union treaty was signed, and the Transcaucasian Federal Union of Soviet Socialist Republics was formed between Georgia, Armenia, and Azerbaijan. This ‘federation within a federation’ lasted for fourteen years until a new Soviet constitution was adopted in December 1936 and the three Caucasian republics independently became federal units of the USSR.56



Territorial Rights: Emphasizing State Continuity

with Tsarist Russia

Soviet Russia tried to keep as much of tsarist territory as possible, but not all territory could be kept. Poland, Finland, Estonia, Latvia, and Lithuania sep-arated from Russia in 1917– 19; in other places, Moscow was able to halt sep-aration (Ukraine, Georgia, Byelorussia, and elsewhere). In this context, it also became a subject of debate whether Soviet Russia (and, since the end of

54 See on the proclamation of Armenia’s statehood: Gegam Petrosyan, Zakavkaz’e v diplomaticheskikh peripetiakh: stanovlenie armianskoi nezavisimoi gosudarstvennosti ( fevral’ 1917- iun’ 1918 gg.), Izdatel’stvo EGU, 2019.
