102 from sovieT russia To The sovieT union

1922, the Soviet Union) had maintained state continuity with tsarist Russia or whether the old Empire had come to an end. Some of the Soviet policies seemed so radically different both from tsarist Russia as well as other capit-alist countries that some foreign legal scholars even assumed that Russia’s state continuity had been broken; they postulated that the Soviet Union was a new, revolutionary state.57

Besides nationalization policies in Soviet Russia (the liquidation of private

property on land and means of production), the Soviet government also an-nounced that it would not pay back the loans of tsarist Russia and the provi-sional government as these had arguably been made in the interests of Russia’s previous ruling classes but also with the purpose of keeping the Russian Empire fighting the Central Powers in the First World War. Before the First World War, France in particular and French citizens individually had made loans on a massive scale to Russia, France’s ally. The Soviet Russian decision not to repay this money was a heavy financial blow to many lenders. It was also considered illegal according to the traditional rules of state succession in international law in Europe, as even a radical change of government was not recognized as an acceptable excuse to default on payments regarding previous foreign loans. In addition, when concluding treaties with its neighbors, new and old, Soviet Russia annulled previous debts owed to it: for example, Article V paragraph 3 of the Soviet Russian– Turkish Treaty of 16 March 1921, con-cluded in Moscow, proclaimed:

The Government of the RSFSR hereby declares, in particular, that it considers Turkey free from any monetary or other obligations originating from any international pacts concluded between Turkey and the tsarist government.

The peace treaties with Estonia, Latvia, and Lithuania, concluded in 1920, also stated that these new nations were not responsible for any parts of the tsarist Russian debt. France was not happy about this way of getting rid of tsarist- era debt and in 1922 attempted to collect a portion of it from the Baltic States dir-ectly, via rules of state succession.58 Elements of this kind in state practice ini-tially indicated that Soviet Russia wanted to break with state continuity with the Russian Empire and even refused state succession on what classical inter-national law would otherwise have mandated, especially succession of loans by previous governments.



57 Peter Kleist, Die völkerrechtliche Anerkennung Sowjetrußlands, Ost- Europa- Verlag, 1934, 16, 80.

58 E Korovin, Sovremennoe mezhdunarodnoe publichnoe pravo, 1926, 34.

TerriTorial righTs 103

However, in relations with other powers and especially concerning ques-

tions of a territorial nature, it was soon clear beyond doubt that Soviet Russia asserted continuity of rights with the Russian Empire. Where the Russian Empire had been involved in a treaty- based solution to certain territorial situ-ations and had acquired certain rights or at least a place at the decision- making table, and then after 1917 foreign powers attempted to regulate the same issue without the Soviet Russian government (initially not recognized by the Western powers), the Soviets protested and complained that Russia’s historical rights had been violated. This was a pattern which played out in multiple dif-ferent circumstances.

For example, in 1920 states gathered in Paris and decided on the sovereignty

and regime of the Spitzbergen (Svalbard) islands: sovereignty went to Norway. On 12 February 1920 the Soviet government protested that it had played no part in these deliberations, even though the Russian Empire had been involved in regulation of the area in 1872.59 The Soviet government protested that it would not allow ‘under any circumstance that its international relations should be disposed of by others without its approval’.60 The same logic applied to the situation concerning the neutrality of Aaland Islands, where a treaty con-cerning their neutrality was made between France, Britain, and Russia (1856). In 1921, Germany, Denmark, Estonia, Finland, France, Britain, Italy, Latvia, Sweden, and Poland concluded a new treaty on the same topic.61 On 28 June 1920, the Soviet Russian government protested tits lack of inclusion as the matter also concerned Russia’s tsarist- era treaty rights:

There is no treaty stipulation based on which the sovereignty of the Russian republic over the Aaland Islands would have ceased to exist. For that reason, no decision concerning these islands will be effective if the Russian govern-ment was not invited to participate in it.62

The situation with the River Niemen (Nemunas) belonged to the same cate-gory. The international status of the River Nemunas was established by Article 14 of the acts of the Congress of Vienna, inter alia carrying the signature of the Russian Empire’s representative. In August 1922, the Soviet government announced that it would not allow any decision on the question of the River



59 R V Dekanozov, ‘Demilitarizatsia i neitralizatsia Spitsbergena’, Soviet YBIL 1966/ 7, 181– 94 at 182.

60 I S Peretersky, ‘Znachenie mezhdunarodnoga dogovora dlia tret‘ego (ne zakliuchivshego etot dogovor) gosudarstva’, SGP No 4, 1957, 71– 80 at 75.

61 Korovin, Sovremennoe mezhdunarodnoe publichnoe pravo, 1926, 60.

62 Peretersky, ‘Znachenie mezhdunarodnoga dogovora dlia tret‘ego . . . ‘, 1957, 75. 104 from sovieT russia To The sovieT union

Niemen without its participation and ‘in any case will consider non- binding for itself all kinds of agreements made to the detriment of the legal rights of the Soviet government’.63

In the same way, in 1922 the Soviet government protested when the

Washington conference discussed the question of the Chinese Eastern Railway in Manchuria but Soviet Russia was not invited nor otherwise formally part of the resolution, even though the railway had been built with funds from the Russian Empire. Similarly, on 2 September 1926 the Soviet government protested that Spain had not invited it to the revision conference of the 1906 Algeciras Act. Russian participation in 1906 would have given an equal right to participation in 1926 as well, so the Soviet government argued. As a con-sequence, the Soviet government reserved for itself the right not to recognize any result of the revision conference arrived at without its participation.64 In its relations with Romania, the interwar Soviet Union also argued for the con-tinuity of rights based on the Constantinople convention of 1881, concerning regulation of the use of the River Danube.65

These cases were at least partly about the Soviet government’s desire to

achieve the international recognition that it initially lacked. At the same time, these examples also demonstrate that Soviet Russia and the Soviet Union pro-ceeded from an assumption of continuity of rights with tsarist Russia, espe-cially in territorial matters. However, territorial issues were key in terms of the survival of the Empire. Only in terms of tsarist Russian debts which the Soviet government refused to pay was there a strong Soviet claim to a break in con-tinuity of Russian obligations. Soviet legal scholars later came up with various theories and justifications as to why the Soviet Union was the same state as the Russian Empire, as a subject of international law— but yet at the same time need not be responsible for tsarist- era debts.

In certain territorial and self- determination questions concerning the former

Russian Empire, the issue remained unresolved, as Soviet Russia and later the Soviet Union did not recognize territorial changes undertaken without its ap-proval. In 1812, by the Treaty of Bucharest, the Russian Empire had annexed Bessarabia, part of a former protectorate of the Ottoman Empire: Moldova. In January 1918, Romania annexed Bessarabia, but until 1940, when the Soviet Union seized this territory back, the Soviet government did not



63 Ibid, 75– 6.

64 Ibid, 76.

65 M A Lebedev, ‘Nekotorye voprosy pol’zovania inostrannoi territoriei v sovremennom mezhdunarodnom prave’, Soviet YBIL 1981, 282– 7 at 287 (Also referring to P G Fandikov, Mezhdunarodno- pravovoi rezhim Dunaya, Moscow: Gosyurizdat, 1955).

comBaTing imperialism aBroaD 105

recognize Bessarabia as part of Romania. Soviet legal scholars later asserted that Bessarabia had been ‘an inseparable part of Soviet Russia, connected with it through a shared historical fate’.66 The Soviets argued that self- determination did not apply here as the Romanian and Moldovan languages were similar but not identical; they promoted the idea that the Moldovans were an ethnicity separate from the Romanians and ordered that the ‘Moldovan’ language be written not in the Latin alphabet but in the Russian alphabet, kirillitsa. Because of the territorial dispute over Bessarabia, the Soviet Russian legal discourse in the 1930s portrayed Romania (a nation much smaller in size than Russia) as another ‘imperialist’ state. However, according to the Soviet scholar I Levin, Romania’s was an ‘imperialism’ which was not able to act independently but rather carried out the tasks given to it by bigger imperialist powers.67

During the Genoa Economic and Financial Conference of April and May

1922— its first major encounter with the capitalist world— Soviet Russia pre-sented credentials from eight other Soviet national republics (Ukraine, Byelorussia, Georgia, Armenia, Azerbaijan, Bukhara, Khorezm, and the Far Eastern Republic) as well as its own.68 However, their constitutional relation-ship with Soviet Russia was soon about to change. On 30 December 1922 the union treaty forming the Soviet Union was concluded by Soviet Russia, Soviet Ukraine, Soviet Byelorussia, and Soviet Transcaucasia.



Combating Imperialism Abroad and Keeping

the Empire Safe at Home

It is interesting to follow how understandings of self- determination and sov-ereignty under the conditions of federalism evolved in Soviet constitutional and international legal thought. For example, I Levin, writing in the journal Sovetskoe gosudarstvo i pravo in 1934, postulated that socialism would eventu-ally lead to the merger of all separate nations into one humankind— but would do so through ‘dialectical development’, because first there would be a tem-porary blossoming of national cultures.69 Levin postulated that national cul-tures would die, but only after exhausting their potential. Under the conditions

66 P V Voronin, A A Karlov, ‘Sozdanie moldavskoi sovetskoi natsional’noi gosudarstvennosti’, SGP No 6, 1967, 12– 20 at 14.

67 I Levin, ‘Natsional’nyi vopros v Rumynii’, SGRP 1931, No 10- 12, 186– 209 at 189.

of capitalism, the manifestation of self- determination was to establish a sep-arate nation state; under the conditions of socialist revolution, the manifest-ation of self- determination was to create a federation. Levin maintained:

Thanks to it (i.e., the federation which it creates - L.M.), only socialism is cap-able of resolving the question of nations and nationhood, not destroying the common economic territory in the process and avoiding political and eco-nomic Balkanization. [. . .] The proletariat decisively rejects all nationalist metaphysics of the nation, which has now become the ideological and polit-ical weapon of fascism. The question of nations and nationhood at this stage is completely subjugated to the tasks of the revolution.70

Another Soviet legal scholar, Mikhail Yakovlevich Rapoport (1889– 1969),71 who worked at Leningrad State University, expressed the same thought in 1940, when he wrote after the conclusion of the Nazi– Soviet non- aggression pact that recognition of the right of peoples to self- determination constitutes ‘as is well known, only a part of the general question of the proletarian revo-lution’.72 What was really meant is that by that time little remained of the right of peoples to self- determination in Soviet practice and thinking, at least in the ‘bourgeois’ sense of the concept.

With great publicity, Soviet Russia also renounced earlier ‘unequal treaties’

with neighbouring nations in Asia: the ‘uncivilized’ peoples of Martens and also of André Mandelstam. This was one of the most visible changes and dif-ferences in Soviet foreign legal policy compared to the tsarist Empire. New treaties were concluded, for example with Afghanistan, Persia, and Turkey as well as with China and Mongolia. During the Soviet period, these treaties were extensively referred to and celebrated as manifestations of progressive principles which the Russian Bolsheviks introduced towards Russia’s neigh-bours in the ‘East’.73 During the Cold War, these treaties were interpreted in the West to the effect that their main goal was to replace British influence in these

territories with Soviet influence.74 In the preamble to the treaty between Soviet Russia and Atatürk’s Turkey, signed in Moscow on 16 March 1921, both states referred to ‘their solidarity in the struggle against imperialism’. The Western powers had at the time not yet recognized either the Soviet Russian or the Turkish governments as a matter of law. The historian Samuel J Hurst argues that these conventions were an extension of Lenin’s argument that socialists and anti- colonial nationalists shared an anti- imperialist cause.75

Based on early Soviet practice in international law, what can be concluded

regarding imperialism? Some authors argue that the Soviet Russian (as well as Mexican) revolutions became an inspiration in anti- imperialist struggles elsewhere:

The two revolutions represented the possibility of radical social and eco-nomic change at the national level, created legal systems that survived for many decades, inspired transnational movements striving against imperi-alism and for social revolution, decisively changed international relations, and motivated international legal innovators to create new forms, principles, and institutions.76

However, paradoxically— as the Soviet Russian case demonstrates— a nation, while able to inspire transnational movements striving against imperialism, at the same time can itself continue to be imperialist, at least from the perspective of its own neighbours and formerly suppressed peoples.

With some nations, such as China, disputes emerged during the interwar

period over whether the Russian Bolsheviks were willing to change their ac-tual policies based on their proclaimed progressive principles (for example, on the question of the Eastern Chinese Railway in Manchuria).77 The new treaties condemned the tsarist inequalities of the past but themselves still also con-tained elements which were not reciprocal, especially in security matters. For example, the Persian– Soviet Russian Treaty of Friendship of 26 February 1921 contained a list of condemnations of tsarist Russian policies; indeed, Soviet

legal scholars liked to emphasize how progressive this was.78 However, some of the treaty’s security- related articles put limitations on Persian sovereignty, for example Article 6:

If a third Party should attempt to carry out a policy of usurpation by means of armed intervention in Persia, or if that Power should desire to use Persian territory as a base for operations against Russia, or if a Foreign Power should threaten the frontiers of Federal Russia or those of its Allies, and if the Persian Government should not be able to put a stop to this menace after having been once called upon to do so by Russia, Russia shall have the right to advance her troops into the Persian interior for the purpose of carrying out the military operations necessary for its defense. Russia undertakes, however, to withdraw her troops from Persian territory as soon as the danger has been removed.79

As a sidenote, Persia— or Iran, as the country has been known since 1935— played an important role in the life of the Soviet international lawyer Fedor Kozhevnikov, who from late 1934 to January 1937 was the Soviet trade rep-resentative in Tehran.80 Based on Article 6, cited above, the Soviet Union oc-cupied Northern Iran from 25 August 1941, after Nazi Germany invaded the USSR in June 1941.81 In a coordinated action, the other part of Iran was occu-pied by the United Kingdom; both nations had already agreed on spheres of in-fluence in Persia before the First World War. Thus, the early Bolshevik treaties played a significant role in practice and proved useful for the Soviet Union even twenty years later. The Iranian government formally denounced the 1921 agreement in March 1959, referring to its clauses such as Article 6. One reason was that at the end of the Second World War, the Soviet Union was in no hurry to remove its forces from Iran, and in 1946 the United Nations had to ask the USSR to withdraw them from Iran’s territory.82

to fascinate scholars. Professor Bill Bowring of Birkbeck College has taken a

somewhat favourable position on the Soviet role in establishing the right of peoples to self- determination under international law. Bowring argues that the USSR played a leading role in the emergence of the right of peoples to self- determination, ‘in the teeth of fierce resistance from the great colonial powers’.83 Bowring further argues that, for Lenin at least, self- determination was not a mere slogan, but a principle he put into practice with immediate ef-fect within the former Russian Empire following the Bolshevik Revolution.84 In that sense, Bowring considers it unjust and incorrect when the role of Soviet Russia or the Soviet Union is diminished when the emergence of the right of peoples to self- determination in international law is discussed, as seems to be the case in a leading monograph on the history of self- determination by Jörg Fisch.85 However, Bowring nevertheless also admits that the Soviet position on self- determination was still also ‘contradictory’ and even ‘hypocritical’.86 A closer historical reading of the Soviet practice of self- determination after 1917, as offered here, suggests that one should indeed be critical of Lenin’s and the Soviet approach to self- determination: the Bolsheviks did not conceive of it as acceptance of genuine secession from the Russian Empire. To verbally support the right of peoples to self- determination, for example in treaties, and yet fight militarily to keep or bring the former tsarist Empire’s territories to-gether was a novel tactic by the new, revolutionary rulers of the Empire.

5


Interwar Soviet International Law Scholars

and the Buildup to the Nazi– Soviet

Pact of 1939



One of the best- known music pieces of the Soviet period is ‘Time, Forward!’ by Georgy Sviridov, the main melody of which was also used for the Soviet TV evening news programme Vremya. But how much did the Soviets want to bring international law forward, compared to the earlier Russian imperial period? In the following, we will explore what the two leading Soviet inter-national law scholars of the interwar period— Evgeny Korovin and Evgeny Pashukanis— wrote about matters related to the transformation of the Russian Empire, the state identity of Soviet Russia and the Soviet Union, and condi-tions of termination of treaties. The assumption here is that their views on international law also reflected the Zeitgeist of interwar Soviet Russia and the Soviet Union.



Imperialism- related Themes in the Work of Evgeny Korovin

The first Soviet legal scholar to write about international law in a way that also became accessible in other countries was Evgeny Korovin (1892– 1964). His 1923 book International Law of the Transition Period was translated into German and became quite well known, at least in Europe, also because of its main message: the book declared an end to common international law with capitalist countries.

Writing in 1923, Korovin postulated that Soviet Russia was ‘encircled’ by

imperialist powers,1 and defined the phenomenon of imperialism in the fol-lowing way:



1 E A Korovin, Mezhdunardonoe pravo perekhodnoga vremeni, Moscow/ Petrograd: Gosudarstvennoe izdatel’stvo, 1923, 6.

Russia, the Soviet Union, and Imperial Continuity in International Law. Lauri Mälksoo, Oxford University Press.

© Lauri Mälksoo 2025. DOI: 10.1093/ 9780198955962.003.0005

ImperIalIsm-related themes In the Work of evgeny korovIn 111

Imperialism as the newest stage of capitalist development, with its unstop-pable expansion (Drang nach . . .), the conquest of colonies, markets, oli-garchy of financial capital, monopoly of international trusts, with its ideology of Weltstaat (world state) and world trust, with all the stages which lead to it (spheres of influence, colonial and postcolonial division of the world, great power concert) is in its essence hostile to international law.2

Korovin also maintained in his 1923 text that in terms of international legal subjecthood, Soviet Russia was the same state as the old Russian Empire:

[O] ne does not need to be a qualified legal scholar to understand that the ‘state of Russia’ has not existed since 1917 and does not need recognition of its existence in anything new or factual or juridical.3

In Korovin’s early Soviet Russian narrative began the trope that military inter-vention was a vicious thing in the hands of capitalist imperialist states whereas in the hands of ‘progressive’ states such as Soviet Russia, it was a tool for progress:

The utter disapproval of Russian workers to every kind of interventionist campaign by the Entente does not amount to a rejection of intervention as a method of class struggle, but rather a rejection and judgment of (Entente) intervention alone. In circumstances that are well known, intervention may become the greatest weapon of progress, a surgical incision, facilitating the labor pains of a new world, but in the hands of the Entente is a synonym for utter retrogression.4

In this understanding, for example, the Soviet Russian reconquest of inde-pendent Georgia in 1921 had apparently been nothing other than a ‘surgical incision’ in the name of historical progress and world revolution. Korovin developed his views further in his textbook on international law published in 1926.5 An intriguing assessment of the beginning of this book is that inter-national law is no longer seen as universal; Korovin calls it ‘contemporary European– American international law’.6 He sees among Soviet republics an

2 Ibid, 10.

3 Ibid, 20.

4 Ibid, 59– 60.

5 E A Korovin, Sovremennoe mezhdunarodnoe publichnoe pravo, Moscow/ Leningrad: Gosudarstvennoe izdatel’stvo, 1926.

6 Ibid, 3.

112 InterWar sovIet Il scholars

alternative international law emerging, an inter- Soviet law or the ‘federation law of the new expanded Soviet Union’.7 The point was that— just as the Soviet Russian constitution of 1918 suggested— the Soviet Union could expand fur-ther. In our context of exploring the Russian and Soviet relationship with im-perialism and colonialism, what is also interesting is that Korovin takes an intermediary position on the colonial question. Because why, in his view, is international law not universal?

Korovin writes:

In any case the fact remains indisputable that full international communi-cation between any states, for example West European democracies and Eastern despotisms where the whole of societal life is based on the Shariah, is unattainable and cannot be achieved in any other way but at the price of any of the contracting parties changing their internal character. Such states, only partially participating in the international communication of the European type, include Persia, Afghanistan, Siam, China, Morocco, etc.8

Thus, in Korovin’s early Soviet Russian interpretation, inequalities between states were due not only to the imperialist attitude of the capitalist states but also to their cultural– developmental differences. However, Korovin is also critical and sarcastic about Western colonial countries:

The colonial history of practically any imperialist state teaches us that half- savage people may, even sometimes successfully, oppose and make agree-ments with cultured predators and the state machine serving them.9

In 1926, Korovin also further elaborated his views on the issue of state conti-nuity between the Russian Empire and Soviet Russia. Korovin argued that it was settled that the Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic was the con-tinuator state of the Russian Empire, the same state as tsarist Russia in inter-national law:

In the international legal sense the RSFSR is the same legal subject as the Russian Empire: this is the answer in predominant theory and practice. [. . .] Its borders were considerably diminished, its internal order changed



7 Ibid, 7– 8.

8 Ibid, 7.

9 Ibid, 20.

ImperIalIsm-related themes In the Work of evgeny korovIn 113

fundamentally [. . .] but the Russian state as a subject of international rights and obligations has remained unchanged.10

The aspect which brought about a certain change in Korovin’s view was the for-mation of the Soviet Union on 30 November 1922. The reason why the Soviet Union could be considered as a new state, in Korovin’s view (even though he admitted that many considered the Soviet Union and not just Soviet Russia, which formed part of it, as the continuator state of the Russian Empire), was that the union treaty of 30 November 1922 specified that joining the Soviet Union was open to all socialist Soviet republics, both those existing and those that might emerge in the future.11 Thus, Korovin’s theory of distinguishing be-tween Soviet Russia and the Soviet Union in terms of state identity enabled keeping the door open for the Soviet Union’s further expansion, beyond the borders of the former Russian Empire and without direct association with the Russian Empire’s pre- 1917 past.

Having suggested that the Russian Empire and the Soviet Union were not

the same state as subjects of international law (though Soviet Russia was iden-tical to the tsarist Empire), Korovin immediately added a reservation to this statement, namely that the Russian Empire and the Soviet Union still had much in common:

Does it mean that there is no state succession at all between Russia and the Soviet Union? Not at all, as the RSFSR is a participant in establishing the new union state— the successor to the Russian Empire— as a consequence of which the USSR succeeds the latter so to speak at the second level.12

In Korovin’s interpretation, Soviet Russia was the state continuator of tsarist Russia and the Soviet Union was its state successor: a complex analytical construction.

What Korovin also brought to Soviet discourse on international law in 1926

was a different kind of realism than that, for example, which Martens had ex-pressed in his public writings. Martens was a realist, too, in the sense that for him international agreements were a result of the balance of power, wars, and the determination and negotiating skills of emperors, their foreign ministers, and their diplomats. However, the realism of Korovin as a Marxist scholar



10 Ibid, 29.

11 Ibid, 30.

12 Ibid, 30.

114 InterWar sovIet Il scholars

was more structural, about the nature of the international community. For ex-ample, Korovin did not want to downplay the fact that, in the history of inter-national law, European empires had often talked about the equality of states whereas in reality, in several epochs— including, in Korovin’s view, during his own early League of Nations era— the great powers dominated small states.13 Another example: the institution of permanent neutrality in international law was, in Korovin’s view, a restriction of the sovereignty of neutral states, achieved in the interest of the great powers, which wanted a buffer zone be-tween them.14

Concerning treaties and the Russian imperial legacy of the events of 1870,

the end of the neutrality of the Black Sea and the London declaration of 1871, Korovin maintained in 1926 that it was possible to terminate treaties uni-laterally when circumstances changed fundamentally (clausula rebus sic stantibus).15 He also wrote a separate article on this issue, using clausula rebus sic stantibus to justify the Soviet government’s decision not to repay Tsarist- era debts.16

In other words, in this regard nothing had changed in early Soviet legal

views compared to the late tsarist approach (Martens). Korovin simply men-tioned well- known cases from state practice such as the tsarist Russian ones on Black Sea neutrality and the free port status of Batumi, but also how in 1919 China repudiated earlier coercive treaties with the Russian Empire, namely those concluded in 1913 and 1915.17

Korovin rationalized clausula rebus sic stantibus in the following way:

Under the reservation of unchanged circumstances, this situation is con-ditionally expressed— is indisputable for international phenomena— that at some moments in history the vital interests of the participants appear so much stronger than legal obligations taken upon oneself earlier, which im-periously tilts them towards the first rather than the second. [. . .] Also in the international sphere there is a situation in which one legal order is replaced by another which differs qualitatively and in underlying principles. [. . .] This inevitably involves annihilation of all those legal relations based on a

14 Ibid, 56.

15 Ibid, 95.

Interlude 115

precondition of the social unity or identities of the social edifice of the treaty partners.18

As examples, Korovin, writing in 1926, gave the Soviet Russian 1917 pub-lication of and refusal to implement Tsarist- era secret treaties as well as the Soviet annulment of the Brest- Litovsk Peace Treaty with imperial Germany in November 1918, concluded in March of the same year.19 In a later work on imperial Japan and international law, Korovin reminded his readers of what Bismarck had apparently once said about international treaties: most treaties are like agreements between the horse and the rider.20 This was meant mostly as an ironic criticism of other, imperialist states in history and the present— but if indeed this is what international agreements represented for imperialist states, how could the Soviet Union have practised a fundamentally different approach to treaties?

To wrap up on the interwar views of Korovin, he was in favour of state conti-

nuity between the Russian Empire and Soviet Russia, and the main reason why, in 1926, he suggested that the Soviet Union should be treated as the successor state to the Empire was the initial Soviet hope that more states would become socialist and then join the Soviet Union, a new federation which would expand further than the Russian Empire ever reached. Second, Korovin— also based on Soviet practice— supported the notion of terminating treaties because of a fundamental change in circumstances: another important continuity with tsarist Russian international law doctrine, particularly that of Martens.



Interlude: Taracouzio and Looking at the Soviet Practice of

International Law from the US

What was known about and thought of Soviet international law doctrine in the West? One work which dealt with this topic explicitly was written in the US by a former officer in the White Army during the Russian civil war, Timofei Andreevich Tarakus- Taracuzio (also: Tarakusev), or, as he became known in the US, Timothy Taracouzio (1897– 1958), a scholar with Georgian roots.21 He wrote his book on the Soviet Union and international law at



18 Ibid, 95.

19 Ibid, 95.

20 Korovin, Iaponia i mezhdunardnoe pravo, Sotsekgiz, 1936, 54.

21 T A Taracouzio, The Soviet Union and International Law. A Study based on the Legislation, Treaties and Foreign Relations of the Union of Socialist Soviet Republics, The Macmillan Company, 1935. 116 InterWar sovIet Il scholars

Harvard University and later became head of the Russian division of the Naval Intelligence School in Washington, DC. Taracouzio emphasized the historical and ideological newness of the Soviet regime but did not really examine the question how much of old Russian ideas about international law the Soviets built on and continued with. He was more a chronicler of early Soviet prac-tice of international law but did not attempt to put Soviet theoretical views on international law into a historical or comparative perspective. Although his book was published in New York in 1935, the manuscript was apparently completed earlier: for example, Taracouzio’s narrative was still based on the as-sumption that the Soviet Union was not a member of the League of Nations,22 even though it had become a member of the League in 1934. On international treaties, Taracouzio observed correctly that under the principle of rebus sic stantibus the Soviet authorities consider their social revolution as sufficient justification for denouncing treaties entered into by the tsarist government of Russia.23 But Taracouzio had nothing to say or predict in terms of what one could expect of Soviet behaviour in terms of adherence to Soviet treaties, even though there was already enough material to be discussed— both in practice as well as in the writings of international lawyers such as Korovin— so that by 1939 the question had become one of the central issues in European politics. Taracouzio also at least implicitly assumed that the old Russian Empire ended as a state in 1917; in its stead, several successor states came later, including— but not only— the Soviet Union.24 However, interpretations of the inter-national legal subjecthood of Soviet Russia and the Soviet Union had already taken a different turn in the Soviet Union.



Evgeny Pashukanis and Empire

Another important Soviet international law author during the interwar period was Evgeny Pashukanis (1891– 1937), who among other things criticized some of Korovin’s views quite forcefully.25 It was an interesting characteristic in the interwar Soviet international law scene that Pashukanis attacked Korovin and,



22 Ibid, 291.

23 Ibid, 250.

24 Ibid, 24.

25 E Pashukanis, Ocherki po mezhdunarodnomu pravu, Sovetskoe zakonodatel’stvo, 1935; reprinted with foreword by Theodor Schweisfurth as E B Pašukanis, Umrisse des Völkerrechts, Berlin Verlag, 1971, 191, Pashukanis writes about Korovin: ‘The conclusion is quite strange for an author who wants to be considered a Marxist.’

evgeny pashukanIs and empIre 117

in turn, Andrey Vyshinsky ferociously attacked Pashukanis, who had already been executed by the Soviet government in 1937.26 Rivalries among leading Soviet international and public lawyers also mirrored rivalries and changing power relations in the Soviet political leadership, in particular the Stalinist Great Terror.

Pashukanis has received considerable attention as a Marxist theoretician

of law,27 and has been considered ‘perhaps the most important Marxist legal theorist’ and a ‘key reference point’.28 During the later years of the Cold War, interest in him revived among Soviet legal scholars.29 However, his main work on international law (1935) has not been easily accessible in the West: it was republished in Berlin in 1971, but still only in Russian, and even this reprinted volume is rare and not accessible through most libraries.30 Only Pashukanis’s Soviet encyclopedia entry on international law from 1926 has been translated and referred to more widely in Western scholarly literature.31 In the encyclo-pedia entry, Pashukanis gave an ultra- realist account of the essence of inter-national law between capitalist states, though in this text he had very little to say in terms of how revolutionary countries like Soviet Russia would do it better. According to Pashukanis, the basic trait of imperialism was that the interaction between capitalist states was a constant struggle: ‘every exchange is the con-tinuation of an armed conflict and the prelude to the next.’32 International organizations and institutions— even commissions for the management of treaty rivers such as the Rhine and the Danube— historically merely reflected which states were on top and when. Just as Korovin had done in the 1920s, Pashukanis observed that international law existed only to the extent that there was a balance of power:



26 See A Vyshinsky, ‘Osnovnye zadachi nauki sovetskogo sotsialisticheskogo prava’, Sovetskoe gosudarstvo No 4, 1938, 4– 5 at 36– 9.

Pashukanis and the Marxist Critique of Law, Routledge, 2025.

30 E Pashukanis, Ocherki po mezhdunarodnomu pravu, Moscow, 1935.

31 E Pashukanis, ‘Mezhdunarodnoe pravo’, in: Entsiklopedia gosudarstva i prava, Izdatel’stvo kommunisticheskoi akademii,1926, vol 2, 858– 74, republished in: Piers Beirne, Robert Sharlet (eds) Pashukanis: Selected Writings on Marxism and Law, Translated by Peter B Maggs, Academic Press, 1980, 168– 82.

32 Ibid, 169.

118 InterWar sovIet Il scholars

No matter how eloquently the existence of international law is proved, the fact remains of the absence of an organizational force which could coerce a state with the same ease as a state coerces an individual person. The only real guarantee that relationships between bourgeois states (and in the transitional period with states of another class type) will remain on the basis of equivalent exchange, i.e. on a legal basis (on the basis of mutual recognition of subjects), is a real balance of forces. Within the limits set by a given balance of forces, separate questions may be decided by compromises and by exchange, i.e. on the basis of law. [. . .] In critical periods, when the balance of forces has fluc-tuated seriously, when ‘vital interests’ or even the very existence of a state are on the agenda, the fate of the norms of international law becomes extremely problematic.33

For Pashukanis, bourgeois liberal idealism about international law was basic-ally a lie and a smokescreen for actual power relationships:

Open denial of international law is politically unprofitable for the bourgeoisie since it exposes them to the masses and thus hinders preparations for new wars. It is much more profitable for the imperialists to act in the guise of paci-fism as the champions of international law.34

However, it implicitly followed that since the international community of (cap-italist) states was so rotten and aggressive, and international law as supported by the ‘imperialists’ such a lie, then the only socialist country in the world— the Soviet Union— had to play along. In his lengthier 1935 work on international law, Pashukanis further developed his radically sceptical ideas. If, for Martens, international law and treaties had been an expression of competition among the European great powers, then additionally Pashukanis emphasized the class component in the struggle between powers:

Questions of domestic and foreign policy that are resolved by the actual bal-ance of class forces will be resolved by sharpened class struggle. Anyone who denies it is either lying to himself or, as happens more often, is trying to lie to others. Not a single juridical formula, no matter how ostentatiously it may be proclaimed or confirmed, stands outside the actual balance of powers that lie behind it. So, for example, we take well into account that not a single treaty on



33 Ibid, 1926, 179.

34 Ibid, 1926, 180.

evgeny pashukanIs and empIre 119

non- aggression, neutrality, and the like, concluded by the Soviet Union with capitalist countries, in itself guarantees non- use of force against us.35

But in these conditions, could capitalist states rely on treaties with the Soviet Union? Furthermore, for Pashukanis, it followed that

One should not regard international law as outside politics. In an epoch of imperialism, international law is one of the forms, one of the means, with the help of which imperialist States struggle among themselves, and ensure the division of spoils, namely territory and unrestrained profit.36

In this light, Pashukanis also saw the history of Soviet Russia, which had to hand over Russian imperial territories in 1918– 20:

It is true that the proletarian state in capitalist encirclement must sometimes carry out the politics of compromises, i.e. temporary concessions, necessary to ensure and strengthen its existence. [. . .] The amounts of concessions are determined by the specific balance of power. At the time of Brest, the Soviet republic had to make big sacrifices, ‘to give up’ space [. . .] in order to gain time. These times have long passed. Now the balance of power is completely different. Now our international position is formulated by the formula of comrade Stalin: ‘We don’t want foreign land. But our own land, not an inch of our land will we give to anyone.’37

Thus, it implicitly followed that Soviet Russian territorial concessions in the peace treaties of 1920– 1 were temporary, due to the unfavourable balance of power at the time. Because what really was ‘foreign land’ and ‘our land’ from the Soviet Russian perspective, after such temporary compromises had been made at the expense of former tsarist territory? Furthermore, Pashukanis highlighted the thought of Stalin that a state had to be strong in order to sur-vive in the international community; or, in the words of Stalin: ‘In our time, they do not take into consideration the weak; only the strong are taken into consideration.’38

So, for Pashukanis, international law as such was characterized by the fol-

lowing specific features: extreme instability and weak enforcement in practice;

35 Pahsukanis, Ocherki po mezdhunarodnomu pravu, Sovetskoe zakonodatel’stvo, 1935, 5– 6.

36 Ibid, 9.

37 Ibid, 18.

38 E Pashukanis, ‘Vstuplenie SSSR v Ligu natsii’, SGP No 6, 1934, 23– 8 at 22.

120 InterWar sovIet Il scholars

when stronger states violate international law against weaker states, this usually goes unpunished. Moreover, international legal disputes often do not reach a final settlement.39

Pashukanis laid out how, since 1917, Soviet Russia had ostentatiously pro-

claimed that it would terminate all unequal relations in the East. However, what this meant in practice often remained contested. Pashukanis recounted how in 1929 ‘Chinese militarists’ violated the treaty on the mutual adminis-tration of the Chinese Eastern Railway, to which the Red Army was forced to give a military response. For Pashukanis, the Soviets wanted only respect for the treaty with China, which foresaw common administration of the railway. Nevertheless, on 23 March 1935 the Soviets sold their rights to the Chinese Eastern Railway (in Manchuria) to the Japanese, for 140 million yen, of which one- third was paid in cash and the rest in goods.40 Based on publications in the journal Sovetskoe gosudarstvo i pravo, differences with China over the adminis-tration of the Chinese Eastern Railway preoccupied Soviet legal scholars quite intensely.41 Perhaps this was also because the differences with the Republic of China revealed that foreign policy was not so simple and Manichaean as declaring the tsarist government ‘imperialist’ and the Soviet government as being against imperialism.

In 1934, the Soviet Union became a member of the League of Nations.

However, Pashukanis also warned that this in no way meant that the Soviet Union was responsible for the League’s past decisions. ‘The USSR especially has no responsibility for the Versailles Peace and other peace treaties signed without its participation’, Pashukanis explained,42 echoing views already ex-pressed in Soviet foreign policy. However, the peace treaties in the Versailles system were inevitably intertwined with Soviet treaties as well; for example, Soviet Russia had agreed on its border with Poland in the Peace Treaty of Riga of 18 March 1921 and although this did not concern Poland’s Western border, it still implied recognition of Poland in its existing borders, which in turn was also a result of the Versailles Peace Treaty. At the same time, on 28 October 1920 the League of Nations (also suggesting this to be valid ‘for Russia’ as well) had approved the allocation of Bessarabia as part of Romania. The interwar Soviet Union did not recognize these 1920 borders with Romania and Pashukanis’s assessment of this border delimitation was disapproving:



39 Pashukanis, Ocherki po mezhdunarodnomu pravu., 1935, 22.

40 Ibid, 53– 6.

41 N Terentyev, ‘KVZhD’, SGP 1933, No 4, 88– 96; I Trainin, ‘Imperializm na Dal’nem Vostoke i SSSR’, SGP 1932, No 7– 8, 147– 68.

42 Pashukanis, Ocherki po mezhdunarodnomu pravu, 1935, 62.

evgeny pashukanIs and empIre 121

Thus, a sovereign state without its approval received a ‘deputy’ and thus im-posed upon, in this way a commission was proposed to carry out border de-marcation ‘on site’, i.e., on territory under not only juridical but also factual Soviet rule .43

During the interwar period, several Soviet legal scholars protested over Romania’s control of Bessarabia, reflecting the fact that the Soviet government did not recognize that sovereignty over Bessarabia had officially passed to Romania.44

In 1919 in Paris, the Russian emigrant André Mandelstam had considered

many of Russia’s nationalities not ready for any kind of self- determination or independent statehood, both culturally and in terms of civilization. Pashukanis and other Soviet international lawyers chose different language to address this issue. ‘After the October Revolution, the Bolshevik party adopts the principle of federation of Soviet republics as one of the means to resolve the national question’, whereby ‘every republic may at any moment in time return full sov-ereignty to itself on the international legal plane’.45 However, according to Pashukanis, a number of Soviet peoples were still backward, and this now had to be confronted within the Soviet Union, through Soviet politics:

The proletariat is not satisfied with formal equality but is struggling against survival of the inequality which exists between backward and advanced peoples. In the Soviet Union, we help the workers of more backward ethnic groups to build their culture, to build their Soviet statehood.46

In other words, the mission civilisatrice still continued, but in Soviet style, as some peoples in the Soviet Union continued to be more advanced than others, presumably first and foremost the Russians.

We now come to the question in Pashukanis’s treatise on international law

which has been of interest to us throughout this study: what is the nature of international treaties, and can a state unilaterally free itself from its treaty obli-gations? On this question, Pashukanis writes:

In reality the force of an international treaty is determined by certain material interests and the forces standing behind those interests. A treaty is operational

43 Ibid, 126.

44 I Levin, ‘Natsional’nyi vopros v Rumynii’, SGRP 1931, No 10– 12, 180– 209.

45 Pashukanis, Ocherki, 93– 4.

46 Ibid, 192.

122 InterWar sovIet Il scholars

and binding as long as those interests and forces are present. No state has ever signed a treaty if it does not have an interest in it or if some kind of power does not force it. [. . .] Therefore each international treaty— as long as it reflects the existing interests of the parties and is dictated by them, and as long as it re-flects the political situation of existing power— constitutes a long- term design for known relations.47

In this perspective, full continuity exists with the Tsarist- era views of Martens on Martens; a continuity between the tsarist and Soviet flexible and realist ap-proaches to international treaties according to which treaties cannot be much more than a reflection of the balance of power at the time of their signing. As leading Soviet international lawyers, both Korovin and Pashukanis held the view that at least the Soviet state— as the first ever proletarian state— could ter-minate treaties unilaterally, and that clausula rebus sic stantibus reflected the reality of international legal practice.

Pashukanis then cites further historical examples regarding violation of

earlier treaties in the context of clausula rebus sic stantibus. In his view, among such cases were the proclamation of the independence of Bulgaria in 1908 and Italy switching alliances during the First World War (1915), as well as France’s choice to stop making payments for its military debt to the US (1932).48 Based on these examples and earlier practice, Pashukanis concludes:

In that way even bourgeois internationalists are forced to recognize the ex-istence of historical events that destroy the force of any international treaty. [. . .] One can say that a state born out of revolution has an enhanced right to terminate treaties which were concluded with respect to the earlier existing social structure. It is exactly on such positions that the Soviet government stood and still stands.49

Pashukanis then applied these general views generously to the Soviet Union’s existing treaties with the capitalist world. For example, although the Soviet Union had become a member of the League of Nations in 1934— which meant that the League’s Covenant had become binding on it, too— Pashukanis wrote in 1935 that ‘the USSR knows very well the limited importance of the articles of the Covenant of the League of Nations’.50 In the earlier revolutionary years,

47 Ibid, 155.

48 Ibid, 160– 1.

49 Ibid, 161.

50 Ibid, 184.

the 1939 nazI–sovIet pact 123

Lenin had called the League of Nations an ‘organization of international rob-bers’.51 In the Soviet perception, this was also because— as one German so-cialist international lawyer from the GDR put it— in 1920– 1, the League of Nations acted as an ‘organized center of intervention against the Soviet state’.52 When the Soviet Union joined the League of Nations in 1934, the Soviet for-eign minister Maxim Litvinov (1876– 1951) made it clear in his country’s offi-cial statements in Geneva that the League of Nations system entailed elements of which the Soviet Union would not have approved if it had been able to ne-gotiate the foundations of the organization in 1919: the mandate system for former colonies of empires defeated in the First World War (Article 22 of the Covenant) as well as the legal possibility to go to war under Articles 12– 15 of the Covenant. Litvinov also criticized the fact that racial equality was not rec-ognized in Article 23 of the Covenant.53

Pashukanis’ explanation of why and under what conditions the USSR joined

the League of Nations was cynical:

It is clear to the workers of the USSR and the whole world that when the Soviet government decided to respond positively to the invitation to join the League of Nations, then this is not because of a change in our principled attitude towards the League, but because the specific international legal situation changed.54

On treaty commitments, it was clear from the approaches of Korovin and Pashukanis— as two leading interwar Soviet international lawyers— that the Soviet Union would abandon the principle of pacta sunt servanda in geopol-itically critical conditions. Soviet treaty practice since 1939 has reflected this explicitly.



The 1939 Nazi– Soviet Pact as a Manifestation

of Soviet Imperialism

On 23 August 1939, Nazi Germany and the Soviet Union concluded a non- aggression pact. This had a secret protocol according to which six smaller

51 E Pashukanis, ‘Vstuplenie SSSR v Ligu natsii’, SGP No 6, 1934, 23– 8 at 23.

52 Peter Alfons Steiniger, Oktoberrevolution und Völkerrecht: eine populärwissenschaftliche Studie, Staatsverlag der Deutschen Demokratischen Republik, 1967, 125.

53 Pashukanis, Vstuplenie SSSR v Ligu natsii’, 1934, 27. See further M M Litvinov, Vneshniaya politika SSSR. Rechi i zayavlenia 1927– 1935, Gosudarstvennoe sotsial’no- ekonomicheskoe izdatel’stvo, 1935, 77 et seq.

54 Pashukanis, Vstuplenie SSSR v Ligu natsii’, 1934, 24.

124 InterWar sovIet Il scholars

states, situated between or near them in Central and Eastern Europe and born towards the end of the First World War were allocated to spheres of influence between the two powers, with the guarantee of non- intervention by the other party. Colloquially, this agreement is also called the Molotov– Ribbentrop Pact, the Nazi– Soviet Pact and sometimes also the Hitler– Stalin Pact. The idea and initiative for the secret protocol and division of the spheres of influence was Stalin’s. However, Hitler, due to his own imperialist and revisionist plans for Germany, especially his plan to attack Poland, went along with it.55 Based on Soviet assurances achieved by this agreement, on 1 September 1939 Germany attacked Poland militarily and later, after the Polish army and population had already suffered heavy losses against the Germans, the Soviet Union moved in on 17 September 1939, occupying and annexing Soviet- allocated terri-tories in Eastern Poland.56 The Soviets waited to invade until they thought they could argue that the Polish state no longer existed— a characterization with which the Polish government in exile vehemently disagreed. Three days after Germany invaded Poland, German foreign minister Ribbentrop inquired of the Soviets what they were waiting for; they, too, should seize their agreed prey in Poland.57 However, the reason for the Soviet delay was that the Soviet Union could now argue that Poland had already ‘collapsed’ and the extent of the Soviet Union’s involvement was limited to ‘liberating’ Ukrainians and Byelorussians in Eastern Poland. Nevertheless, Soviet campaign against Poland was a real war; the Red Army lost 737 dead and 1,862 wounded, or 2,599 casualties.58 The two powers, Nazi Germany and the Soviet Union, even held a common military parade in Brest- Litovsk as a symbolic reminder of the place where the two powers had left off with their bilateral treaty- making and allocation of territories in March 1918. Later, on 28 September 1939, in another secret protocol, the two powers changed aspects of their spheres of influence so that Lithuania was allocated from the German to the Soviet sphere of influence, while Germany received additional territories in Poland.

The 1939 Nazi– Soviet pact was born out of both Nazi and Soviet rejection of

the Versailles system. As early as 1922, Soviet revolutionary and Comintern ac-tivist Karl Radek (1885– 1939) had written that one of the goals of the Versailles Peace Treaty had been ‘the destruction of Russia as a great power’.59 Vyacheslav

55 Claudia Weber, Der Pakt. Hitler, Stalin und die Geschichte einer mörderischen Allianz, München: CH Beck, 2019, 65– 7.

56 See further Roger Moorhouse, Poland 1939. The Outbreak of World War II, Basic Books, 2020.

57 Weber, Der Pakt, 2019, 81.

58 Sean McMeekin, Stalin’s War, Penguin Books, 2021, 101.

59 K B Radek, Likvidatsia Versal’skogo mira: doklad IV Kongressu Kommunistischeskogo Internatsionala, Petrograd: Izdanie Kommunisticheskogo Internatsionala, 1922, 21.

the 1939 nazI–sovIet pact 125

Molotov (1890– 1986), the Soviet foreign minister whose signature is on the Nazi– Soviet pact, even called Poland ‘a bastard of the Versailles treaty’.60

With the Nazi– Soviet non- aggression pact, Hitler’s Germany ensured that

when it seized Poland by military force, it would not also face a war with the Soviet Union. But why was this secret agreement interesting and important for the Stalin- led Soviet Union? Later, Russian foreign policy apologists explained that the pact was necessary in order to postpone the inevitable German ag-gression against the Soviet Union and to gain strategic depth for the military defence of Moscow and Leningrad in case of a future Nazi German attack. At the same time, the secret agreement of August 1939 also followed a highly spe-cific Russian/ Soviet imperial logic: to take back as much as possible from what used to be the territory of the Russian Empire until 1917. True, regarding the main object of prey, Poland, the line of the Nazi– Soviet pact largely followed the Curzon line of 1919, as proposed by the British foreign minister as to where the ethnic Soviet– Polish border should run.61 During the war between Soviet Russia and Poland (1919– 21), the Red Army almost reached Warsaw, but after the Polish ‘miracle on the Vistula’ the fortunes of war turned, and Poland achieved for itself more favourable borders than the Curzon line and fixed them in the Soviet– Polish Riga Peace Treaty of 18 March 1921.

From the Soviet side, the 23 August 1939 Nazi– Soviet secret agreement was

a demonstration of two key international legal ideas in Soviet thinking and practice— first, that the Soviet Union still saw itself as a continuator state of the Russian Empire as far as territory was concerned; second, that the Soviet Union understood treaties such that if the geopolitical interests of the prole-tarian state so demanded, previous treaties could be violated.

The territories affected by the secret protocols had formed the basis for his-

torical German– Russian territorial deals, especially the three partitions of Poland in the eighteenth century, which remained effective until 1918. Besides its part of Poland, what the Soviet Union asked for in the secret protocols was freedom of action in the territory of the old Russian Empire: Finland, Estonia, Latvia, Lithuania, and Bessarabia. The only place where the Soviet Union was willing to ‘lose out’ compared to the former territory of the Russian Empire was the territory of Poland itself. For example, since the late eighteenth cen-tury, the Warsaw region had belonged to the Russian Empire, whereas now, based on the secret protocols, it was allocated to the Nazi German ‘sphere of influence’.



60 Weber, Der Pakt, 2019, 50.

61 Ibid, 93, 98, 216.

126 InterWar sovIet Il scholars

We know already from the Melian dialogue of Thucydides that if an empire

can expand and gain territory without war, then it will prefer to do so. And so, for the Soviet Union too, the first move in its sphere of influence was to present ultimatums to the governments concerned and ask for establishment of military bases in their territory. The three Baltic States gave in to these Soviet demands and so, in autumn 1939, Soviet military bases were established in Estonia, Latvia, and Lithuania, with Soviet guarantees of preservation of state sovereignty. Finland refused to give in to the Soviet demands for military bases and territorial concessions, so the Soviet Union started a war against Finland on 30 November 1939 (the Winter War). Because of the Soviet attack against Finland and the Soviet refusal to engage in the dispute resolution mechanism foreseen in the Covenant of the League of Nations, the Soviet Union was ex-pelled from the League as an aggressor state on 15 December 1939. However, the Indian Marxist international law scholar B. S. Chimni has still criticized the expulsion of the Soviet Union from the League of Nations as “selective”; in con-trast, Italy had not been expelled after its aggression against Abessinia.62

In June 1940, Moscow presented ultimatums to the governments of the three

Baltic republics, demanding a complete overhaul of their governments. When the ultimatums were accepted, the Soviet Union militarily occupied the three states, Sovietized them, and not long afterwards, in August 1940, annexed all three. In terms of the Soviet discourse, one noteworthy testimony from this time is what was recorded by Vincas Krėvė- Mickevičius (1882– 1954), a famous writer in Lithuania who was appointed head of the ‘people’s govern-ment of Lithuania’ on 24 June 1940. The value of his testimony can also be crit-ically challenged because he had himself collaborated with the Soviet power in 1940 and gave his testimony during the Nazi German occupation of Lithuania during the Second World War. But what he said as testimony fits a Soviet pat-tern and seems plausible. Krėvė- Mickevičius met with the Soviet foreign min-ister Molotov in Moscow on 30 June 1940 to discuss the next steps regarding the future of Sovietized Lithuania. During their conversation, Molotov ex-pressed the opinion that the Russian tsars had aimed to reach the coasts of the Baltic Sea since the reign of Ivan the Terrible, but did not do so for their own personal desires but because the needs of development of the Russian state and nation demanded it; it was no different now, in 1940.63 This is quite a succinct expression of the continuity in tsarist and Soviet imperial(ist) identities. In

62 B S Chimni, ‘Inclusion and Exclusion in International Organizations’, in: Jan Klabbers (ed) The Cambridge Companion to International Organizations Law, CUP, 2022, 76– 97 at 87.

63 Boris Meissner, Die Sowjetunion, die baltischen Staaten und das Völkerrecht, Verlag für Politik und Wirtschaft, 1956, 82 (With specific references to the original testimony indicated).

the 1939 nazI–sovIet pact 127

addition, Molotov explained to Krėvė- Mickevičius in the same conversation that small peoples would disappear anyway, small states would be integrated into the Soviet Union, and the Soviet system would determine the future of Europe.64 In his programmatic speech at the Supreme Council of the Soviet Union, Molotov complained that the 1939 pacts of mutual assistance had not led to ‘rapprochement’ with the Baltic States due to their bourgeois govern-ments and, making the case for annexation of the Sovietized Baltic republics, emphasized the importance of control over the Baltic Sea ports.65

During their treaty- based alliance, which lasted almost two years, Germany

and the Soviet Union at several times had divergent interpretations of their mutual obligations to each other. For example, the Soviet Union considered it a violation of the Pact when Germany concluded a transit treaty with Finland, which belonged to the Soviet sphere of influence according to the non- aggression pact. Germany was taken aback when, in June 1940, the Soviet Union demanded— and took from Romania— not only Bessarabia but also Northern Bukovina, with the city of Chernivtsi in contemporary Ukraine (Cernăuți in Romanian).66 On 12– 13 November 1940, while visiting Berlin, the Soviet foreign minister Molotov experienced a bombing raid by the British air force but also brought with him Stalin’s wish and specification that the Soviet Union also considered parts of the Danube river, especially the Danube delta, as within its sphere of influence.67 From the perspective of the Soviet Union, the Danube proved quite important in the post- Second World War his-tory of international law, so we will return to this topic and Soviet legal for-eign policy on the region in the next chapter. As the Soviet Union’s treaty allies, Germany’s military representation even attended the 1 May 1941 military parade in Moscow, less than two months before Hitler’s Germany attacked the Soviet Union.

The German historian Claudia Weber has argued that, in terms of its ac-

tual impact, the Nazi– Soviet pact with its secret protocols was one of the most important treaties of the twentieth century.68 This must have been the case because during the Nuremberg trials in 1945 the issue of the Nazi– Soviet non- aggression pact and its secret protocols was the single most important issue or red line for the Soviets, in that they were not willing to surrender



64 Cited in Weber, Der Pakt, 2019, 177.

65 V M Molotov, ‘Vneshnyaya politika Sovetskoga Soyza. Doklad na zasedanii VII Sessii Verkhovnoga Soveta SSSR’, 1.08.1940, SGP No 8– 9, 1940, 3– 4.

66 Weber, Der Pakt, 2019, 189.

67 Ibid, 189.

68 Ibid, 71.

128 InterWar sovIet Il scholars

control over drafting the judgment.69 At the same time, the 1939 pact has often been underestimated and bypassed when telling the story of the im-pact on international law of the Soviet Union and the socialist revolution. For example, a relatively recent and otherwise highly interesting study of this very topic hardly mentions the secret protocols of the pact,70 even though highlighting that outside Europe, the Soviet Union was a model in terms of anti- imperialist struggles. However, the consequence of the pact was that in 1939– 40, the Soviet Union violated the sovereignty and/ or borders of six member states of the League of Nations, namely Poland, Finland, Estonia, Latvia, Lithuania, and Romania. The pact does not fit in the model according to which imperialism is defined primarily through capitalism; nor does it support a convenient reduction— at least in Eastern and Central Europe— that the Second World War was in simplistic terms a struggle between a ‘good’ alliance and a ‘bad’ one. The alliance between great powers in 1939– 41 was different from the alliance 1941– 45.

An additional aspect of the 1939 Nazi– Soviet pact was what it revealed

about the Soviet attitude towards international treaties. The Soviet foreign policy approach in 1939 fittingly mirrored what leading Soviet international lawyers such as Korovin and Pashukanis had consistently argued during the 1920s and 1930s, namely that treaties could be violated when the interests of the proletarian state so demanded. Throughout the 1920s and 1930s, with re-spect to its border states, the Soviet Union initiated and took upon itself a whole range of treaty obligations, which it violated with the conclusion of the Molotov– Ribbentrop Pact. In 1928 the Soviet Union was not invited to Paris when states adopted the Briand– Kellogg Pact in which they renounced war ‘as an instrument of national policy in their relations with one another’ (Article 1).71 However, Moscow proposed the same pact— which had been an example of ‘imperialist pacifism’, as Usenko later called it— 72 to its neighbours, with the result that the so- called Litvinov convention was concluded in 1929, signed by the Soviet Union and its ‘bourgeois’ border states. Furthermore, in 1933, at the initiative of Soviet foreign minister Maxim Litvinov, a treaty defining aggression was signed in London by the Soviet Union and its border states— a



69 Francine Hirsch, Soviet Judgment at Nuremberg. A New History of the International Military Tribunal after World War II, OUP, 2020, 104– 5.

70 Kathryn Greenman, Anne Orford, Anna Saunders, Ntina Tzouvala (eds) Revolutions in International Law: The Legacies of 1917, CUP, 2021.

71 See further Oona A Hathaway, Scott J Shapiro, The Internationalists. How a Radical Plan to Outlaw War Remade the World, New York: Simon & Schuster, 2017.

72 E T Usenko, ‘Printsip demokraticheskogo mira— naibolee obshchaya osnova sovremennogo mezhdunarodnoga prava’, Soviet YBIL 1973, 13– 42 at 26.

the 1939 nazI–sovIet pact 129

treaty that listed several specific activities defined as aggression. However, in June 1940, the Soviet Union violated this treaty towards the Baltic States, for example by conducting a naval blockade against Estonia. The Soviet Union had also concluded bilateral non- aggression pacts with its border states in the West. Finally, by joining the League of Nations in 1934, the Soviet Union was bound by the Covenant of the League of Nations towards its fellow League of Nations members. Before being replaced as Soviet foreign minister by Molotov, Maxim Litvinov also advocated in the League of Nations that ag-gressors should be held accountable.73 The volte- face of concluding a non- aggression pact with secret protocols with Germany in 1939 demonstrated how treaty obligations and the underlying principle of international treaty law, pacta sunt servanda, were entirely subordinated to the expansionist– revisionist agenda of the Soviet Union. Quite characteristic was what Soviet foreign minister Molotov wrote on 17 September 1939 to Grzybowski, the Polish minister in Moscow:

The Polish– German war has made apparent the internal bankruptcy of the Polish state. During ten days of military operations Poland has lost all of its industrial regions and its cultural centres. Warsaw no longer exists as the capital of Poland. The Polish government has fallen and shows no sign of life. This means that the Polish state and its government have ceased in fact to exist. Likewise the treaties concluded by the USSR and Poland have lost force. Poland, left to itself and without leadership, has been turned into a convenient field for any eventuality and unexpected occurrence, which could create a threat for the USSR. Therefore, having been neutral up to now, the Soviet government can no longer be neutral to these facts.74

How to relate to Soviet European conquests in 1939– 40 after the Soviet Union had become an ally against Nazi Germany caused some considerable head-aches in London and Washington, as Moscow demanded recognition of its conquests.75 The issue was on the table— but at the same time was not re-solved unequivocally— at the Soviet– US– UK Crimea (Yalta) conference in February 1945.



73 M Litvinov, Protiv agressii, OGIZ, 1938, 58 et seq.

74 Jan F Triska, Robert M Slusser, The Law, Theory, and Policy of Soviet Treaties, Stanford UP, 1962, 155.

75 See Kaarel Piirimäe, Roosevelt, Churchill, and the Baltic Question: Allied Relations during the Second World War, Palgrave Macmillan, 2014.

How did Soviet international lawyers deal with the 1939 Nazi– Soviet non-

aggression pact and the territorial consequences of its aftermath? In their influ-ential post- Second World War Soviet textbook on international law, Vsevolod Durdenevsky (1899– 1963) and Sergey Krylov (1888– 1958) resolved it by ‘cor-recting’ history just somewhat: in their narrative of what happened in inter-national law during the Second World War, they offered a list of twenty- six European ‘sovereign states’ as of 1 September 1939. The three Baltic States and League of Nations members of Estonia, Latvia, and Lithuania were not men-tioned;76 by 1947 they had disappeared, not just from the world political map but also from the Soviet narrative of interwar European political history.

Durdenevsky enjoyed influence as an international lawyer. Having been

educated as a lawyer during the tsarist period (graduating from Moscow University in 1911), he became a professor at the same university in 1922. From 1944 to 1961, he worked in the treaty law department of the Soviet foreign ministry and in the Soviet delegation attended the Potsdam conference, the Paris peace conference, the Belgrade conference on the regime of the Danube, and others. His fellow textbook author Krylov was a graduate of St Petersburg University from the same year, 1911. He worked as a professor of international law at MGIMO University in Moscow but, just like Durdenevsky, also worked as an expert in the Soviet foreign ministry. Krylov became the first Soviet judge at the ICJ (1946– 52) and a member of the ILC in the period 1953– 6.77

Durdenevsky also looked back at the change of Soviet borders during the

Second World War in an article dedicated to the topic in 1947.78 He acknow-ledged that the question of territory was the most important question in state succession and supported the view that the tsarist Empire ended as a state or, rather, was ‘replaced’ in 1917 by a higher type of state in terms of a new social– economic formation.79 Durdenevsky explained the territorial gains which the Soviet Union made in the Second World War, inter alia in Poland:

[In 1921] The Polish military dictatorship of Pilsudski succeeded, with the help of Western great powers, in conquering Western Ukraine and

Byelorussia, violating the most obvious ethnographic borders (‘the Curzon line’). Aiming for peace for the sake of recovery [. . .] the Soviet republics made territorial concessions, at the same time stipulating minority rights in Poland; the Poland of Pans ignored these rights, systematically violating the Riga treaty.80

Durdenevsky also had justifications for other Soviet territorial gains, for ex-ample in Bessarabia:

In 1918, the Romanian boyars seized Bessarabia, obtaining approval of the annexation of Bessarabia from England, France, Italy, and Japan in Paris on October 28, 1920, and hoped to achieve recognition of this fact from the Soviets. However, the Soviet people and government did not recognize the seizure or the Paris protocol.81

The Soviets also annexed the Republic of Tannu Tuva at the border with Mongolia:

In October 1943, the Tuva national republic— where a considerable part of the population consists of Russian emigrants from tsarist times (Old Believers who escaped persecution) voluntarily joined the Soviet Union and became part of the Russian SFSR as an autonomous oblast.82

Finally, Southern Sakhalin and the Kurile islands:

The Crimean (Yalta) agreement of February 11, 1945 between the USSR, the USA, and the UK and which was published in February 1946, arranged the return of Southern Sakhalin to the USSR. The Portsmouth treaty of conquest fell apart. The historic rights of the peoples of the Soviet Union were restored not only in Sakhalin but also in the Kurile islands.83

Thus, by 1945, the Soviet Union had reclaimed several territories which had been part of the Russian Empire but also gained further territories which had never been part of the Russian Empire. When we read the views of Soviet
