interwar international lawyers such as Korovin and Pashukanis, we can see that none of this must have come as a big surprise: the possibility of territorial acquisitions through ‘termination’ of treaties and taking advantage of the new balance of power had been kept theoretically and doctrinally open in Soviet international legal publications.





6


The Cold War: Interpretations

of Soviet State Identity, Federalism, and

Self- Determination



At the end of the Second World War, the Soviet Union annexed terri-tories which in the early twentieth century had formed part of the Russian Empire: Estonia, Latvia, Lithuania, parts of Western Belarus and Western Ukraine, and Southern Sakhalin. It also acquired territories which had not previously been part of the Russian Empire, such as Galicia (Lemberg/ Lwów/ Lvov/ Lviv region) or parts of Germany’s East Prussia (which became Soviet Russia’s Kaliningrad oblast and which had been briefly under Russian occu-pation in the eighteenth century). Moscow also achieved a situation where the regimes in neighbouring countries in Central and Eastern Europe quickly came under its hegemonic control: Poland, the German Democratic Republic, Czechoslovakia, Hungary, Romania, and Bulgaria, as well as (elsewhere) Mongolia and other territories. The sovereignty of some European states which— or whose populations— had been at war with the Soviet Union during the Second World War emerged with ‘strings attached’ to their statehood, ei-ther de facto or as a matter of law: Finland (neutrality and certain restraints in foreign policy, even though Finland successfully managed to avoid a state so-cialist regime being established) and Austria (negotiated neutrality based on a treaty in 1955 which ended Soviet occupation).

In international legal theory, the new situation in the Soviet Union was re-

flected in several imperially inspired doctrines and discourses. These illustrate the main thesis of this book, namely that there was continuity between the two different formations of the Empire: tsarist Russia and the Soviet Union.

• The first of these was the doctrine of state continuity between the Russian

Empire and the Soviet Union, although this came with interesting nu-ances and intra- Soviet debates.



Russia, the Soviet Union, and Imperial Continuity in International Law. Lauri Mälksoo, Oxford University Press.

© Lauri Mälksoo 2025. DOI: 10.1093/ 9780198955962.003.0006

134 The Cold War: InTerpreTaTIons

• The second was how federalism was construed in the Soviet Union,

claiming that both the Soviet Union and its constituent Soviet repub-lics were sovereign states at the same time— a claim that did not hold up against the political facts on the ground regarding the Soviet republics.

• The third was the doctrine of how self- determination of peoples was pro-

moted vis- à- vis the outside world (concerning other empires) and how it was construed domestically, within the Soviet Union itself.

• Fourth, another imperial continuity was the flexible Soviet approach to

terminating inconvenient treaties.

• Fifth was the idea that at least since 1945 the Soviet Union was a ‘great

power’ and generally that the world is divided into great powers and smaller states, with implications for international law and prerogatives for great powers.

• Sixth was the Soviet- promoted concept of peaceful coexistence, which

critics have come to associate with the historical concept of the balance of power in international law.

• Seventh was the doctrine of socialist international law for dependent

states, particularly in the Eastern bloc.

In the following we will examine the first three of these legal doctrines and thereafter, in the next chapter, turn to treaties, great power doctrines, balance of power, and socialist international law.



The Soviet Union’s Continuity with the Russian Empire

The international lawyer who, after the Second World War, decisively shaped Soviet thinking on the question of the Soviet Union’s state identity with tsarist Russia was Fedor Ivanovich Kozhevnikov, who later (1953) became the Soviet judge at the ICJ. In his publications, he wrote with adoration of Stalin’s ideas from the perspective of international law,1 so in this sense we can also think of Kozhevnikov as a Stalinist. Other prominent Soviet international lawyers at the time, such as Krylov— the first Soviet judge at the ICJ— were also preoccupied with what Stalin thought about international law.2 However, Kozhevnikov’s

1 F I Kozhevnikov, ‘I V Stalin ob osnavnykh printsipakh sovremennoga mezhdunarodnoga prava’, SGP No 12, 1949, 95– 109; F I Kozhevnikov, ‘Nekotorye voprosy mezhdunarodnoga prava v svete truda I V Stalina, ‘Marksizm i voprosy iazykoznania’, SGP No 4, 1951, 25– 36.

2 See also for extensive reference to Stalin’s thought in Serge Krylov, ‘Les notions principales du droit des gens’. (La doctrine soviétique du droit international)’, RCADI Vol 70, Martinus Nijhoff, 1947, 423 et seq.

The sovIeT UnIon’s ConTInUITy WITh The rUssIan empIre 135

main contribution to international law in the Soviet Union comprised two books published after the Second World War on the history and evolution of international law in pre- as well as post- 1917 Russia.3 Kozhevnikov’s under-standing of Russia’s positive and progressive role in the history of international law was so exaggerated and one- sided that even his Soviet contemporary inter-national lawyers— for example Evgeny Korovin— reproached him for having too uncritical an approach to tsarist Russia’s accomplishments in international law.4 In many ways, the debate about how to evaluate tsarist Russia’s role in the history of international law was closely related to the debate on the question whether the Soviet Union was identical to the Russian Empire as a subject of international law. It was also a debate about the present day, as behind it lurked the question: how Russian should the Soviet Union be— or should it be openly recognized that it was? Kozhevnikov’s history of international law in Russia always highlighted Russian imperial achievements. This imitated Stalin’s ap-proach in his famous toast to the Great Russian people on 24 May 1945, and the new— more openly pro- Russian— history policies adopted by the Soviet state during the Second World War.

However, here we are mainly interested in how Kozhevnikov shaped

the debate on state identity and international legal personality in the Soviet Union. In his 1948 book The Soviet State and International Law 1917– 1944, Kozhevnikov wrote that after the 1917 October Revolution, Russia became a ‘Soviet socialist state, the great Soviet Union’.5 To be as precise as possible, some of Kozhevnikov’s statements on this subject need to be cited at length here:

The Great socialist October Revolution in Russia creates a new state— a Soviet socialist state which in terms of its social class content was diametrically op-posed to that which existed in Russia previously. In other words, in place of the old Russia emerges the new socialist Russia but this is nevertheless none other than old Russia. [. . .] In other words, the Great October socialist revo-lution does not create in the juridical sense a new subject of international law. Soviet Russia steps up as successor to the rights of old Russia. Any attempt to limit Soviet Russia in these rights— but even more so to deprive it of these rights— has invariably provoked its decisive protests. [. . .] What is the Soviet Union from the perspective that interests us? Undoubtedly, this is Russia, in



3 F I Kozhevnikov, Sovetskoe gosudarstvo i mezhdunarodnoe pravo 1917– 1944 gg. (Opyt istoriko- pravovogo issledovania), Iuridicheskoe izdatel’stvo ministerstva iustitsii SSSR, 1948.

4 E Korovin, ‘Rossiya i mezhdunarodnoe pravo’, SGP No 2, 1949, 65– 6. See also S Mamulya’s review of Kozhevnikov’s book, ibid, 67– 70.

5 Kozhevnikov, Sovetskoe gosudarstvo i mezhdunarodnoe pravo 1917– 1944 gg, 1948, 6. 136 The Cold War: InTerpreTaTIons

the form of the socialist Soviet state. This is no surprise as it is generally rec-ognized that the Russian people are the leading force among all the peoples of our state. [. . .] In that way, Russia was, is, and will be.6

Upon closer reading of Kozhevnikov’s ideas, what is noteworthy is that the Soviet Union is both a new state (‘creates a new state’) as well as an old one (‘this is nevertheless none other than old Russia’). Two extremes are recon-ciled into a satisfactory whole: an expression of Soviet Marxist dialectics— reconciling contradictions— of which we will later present more examples. Moreover, Kozhevnikov speaks at the same time about state continuity as well as succession, flexibly blurring these two concepts of international law. Although Kozhevnikov became famous for emphasizing Soviet state identity with the Russian Empire, he later, in a co- authored text, had no problem in referring to the Soviet relationship with tsarist Russia as a case of ‘state suc-cession’.7 This flexibility— or lack of concern with the precision of concepts— remains characteristic of Soviet and Russian treatment of issues of state continuity and succession, with the intended meaning that today’s Russia is the same state as the Russia of the past, except in matters where Russia wants to avoid bearing the consequences of that sameness. For example, none of the Soviet advocates of state continuity (rather than state succession) acknow-ledged that the Soviet Union might also have been responsible for tsarist- era debts.

Because of this flexibility of approaches, the debate about the exact relation-

ship between tsarist Russia and the Soviet Union was also one of the debates in the Soviet Union in which a certain diversity of opinion was accepted in legal scholarship. As discussed previously, in the 1920s Korovin tried to present the Soviet Union as something different from the Russian Empire, identifying only Soviet Russia (as the core of the Soviet Union) with the Russian Empire, also with the hope that other states might still become socialist and join the union in the future. In some ways, Kozhevnikov’s strong views on Soviet continuity with imperial Russia were a response to earlier views by Soviet international law colleagues who emphasized the disruption of state continuity in 1917. Thus, in 1947 Vsevolod Durdenevsky— Kozhevnikov’s international law col-league from Moscow, fellow Soviet diplomat, and international law adviser at the Soviet UN delegation— had written in an article in the journal Sovetskoe



6 Ibid, 32– 5.

7 F I Kozhevnikov, I P Blishchenko, ‘Sotsializm i sovremennoe mezhdunarodnoe pravo’, SGP No 4, 1970, 88– 95 at 90.

The sovIeT UnIon’s ConTInUITy WITh The rUssIan empIre 137

gosudarstvo i pravo that the history of international relations knew cases of ter-mination of states.8 Durdenevsky opined that the case of Russia after 1917 was one which involved a replacement with a new, higher type of state in terms of social- economic formation. A state that comes to an end hands over succession to the new state, the most important part of which is territory.9 Durdenevsky’s frequent co- author, Krylov, also wrote this in their common textbook on inter-national law in 1947, when he discussed replacement of a state— consisting of one type of historical formation— with another type. In Krylov’s view, one such case was France after the French Revolution of 1789, when the mon-archist, absolutist, and feudal formation was replaced with the bourgeois state. Krylov added:

Or another historical example, lucky for humanity, was tsarist Russia, a prison house of peoples. In this Russia the revolution overthrew tsarist power, and instead of tsarist Russia emerged a new state, a state of a new type: our great socialist Motherland.10

Kozhevnikov’s strongly and emotionally expressed view that the Soviet Union as a subject of international law was the same state as tsarist Russia set the tone for Soviet debates during later decades, right up until the disintegration of the Soviet Union. Nevertheless, some Soviet legal scholars challenged it. In a monograph published in 1961, Mirza Mosesovich Avakov (1925– 87) inclined more towards the state succession perspective.11 Avakov was a Soviet veteran of the Second World War who lived and taught initially in Leningrad and Minsk. As a member of the delegations of the Byelorussian SSR and the Soviet Union, he attended several major international conferences in Vienna and elsewhere. During the later stage of his career, Avakov became Professor of International Law at the Diplomatic Academy of the Russian MFA in Moscow.12

The focus of Avakov’s academic work was state succession in the context of

a fundamental change in its ‘social structure’.13 He also critically claimed that

bourgeois scholarship on issues of state continuity and succession was charac-terized by ‘dogmatism, juridism, and analysis of the “pure” legal norm’.14 This was apparently also a reference to the fact that international legal theory in the West had tried to keep the notions of state continuity and succession separate from each other and even construe them as conceptual opposites. Avakov’s main thesis was that in 1917 the Russian proletariat liquidated the old, bour-geois state and created a new Soviet one.15 Conceptually, these were cases of state succession where a new subject of international law emerged after a so-cial revolution and then at least some rights and obligations of the old sub-ject of international law would be transferred to the new one, even though the limits of that transfer would be determined ‘by the class essence of the new state and the generally recognized norms of international law’.16 Avakov agreed with Krylov’s 1947 view— namely that the Soviet Union was a new state— and disagreed with Kozhevnikov’s 1948 thesis on state continuity with tsarist Russia.17 Whether or not to accept any tsarist Russian obligations depended on the ‘sovereign will of the Soviet state’.18 The theory of— and political demand for— continuity (that is, respect for tsarist- era obligations) for Soviet Russia and the Soviet Union was ‘bourgeois, reactionary, and dogmatic’.19 When a new subject of international law annulled the debts of the previous subject, this in no way stood in contradiction to international law.20 On this issue, there was no disagreement between Soviet supporters of the two opposite theories: state continuity and state succession. Moreover, just like Kozhevnikov, Avakov of course agreed that Soviet territorial expansion during the Second World War had been a positive event, as the Soviet Union had finally received ‘durable and just borders’.21

The reception of these two theories proves that Kozhevnikov’s thesis

eventually won more supporters among Soviet international lawyers. Lidiya Artemevna Modzhoryan (1911— 96) agreed that the Soviet Union as a subject of international law was the same state as the Russian Empire yet at the same time was not obliged to honour certain of the Russian Empire’s treaty or con-tractual obligations.22

Among the Soviet theorists of international law, perhaps the synergetic last

word on the question belongs to Natalya Zakharova, whose academic work focused on the impact of social revolution on treaties and statehood. Her core ideas on this subject matter were initially expressed in her Soviet Yearbook of International Law article in 1960.23 Zakharova observed correctly that, prac-tically speaking, the two concepts did not differ so much, as the representa-tives of both concepts, for example Kozhevnikov and Avakov, still assigned to the Soviet Union the right to abandon previous Russian treaties while highlighting the Soviet Russian claim to the continuity of tsarist rights in ter-ritorial situations and prerogatives such as the Aaland and Spitzbergen is-lands. Nevertheless, according to Zakharova, the correct legal theory was that the Soviet Union continued the international legal personality of the Russian Empire.24 At the same time, Zakharova asserted that the Soviet concept of state continuity in cases of fundamental changes to the structure of the state (social revolution) has ‘nothing in common with the bourgeois theory of continuity’, which assumed the continuity of treaties.25 Thus, Zakharova admitted that Soviet legal scholars were using formally the same concepts as they had been developed in classical international law— mostly developed in Europe— but when needed, those same scholars construed those concepts in line with their own different, idiosyncratic meaning. For Soviet international lawyers, it was not an insurmountable problem to announce that a certain international legal concept meant something else for the Soviet Union and socialist states.

In her 1966 monograph on the topic, Zakharova expanded these ideas on

state continuity further. Again, she started her analysis by observing that the Soviet scholars who disagreed on the question whether the Soviet Union was a new state (state succession) or continued the legal personality of tsarist Russia (state continuity) did not have any disagreement on the issue that, in the case of social revolution, a state such as Soviet Russia was entitled to abandon treaties concluded by the previous regime.26 Nevertheless, Zakharova’s main point was that social revolution did not change or terminate the subjecthood of a state in international law; therefore, there was no state succession or transfer of rights from one subject to another.27 In particular, the revolution did not change Russia’s territorial rights; in Soviet practice, continuity was simply assumed.28

According to Zakharova, this also corresponded to the expectations of the Soviet people:

The position of the Soviet government concerning the international legal subjecthood of Russia corresponded to the representation of the masses that Russia lives on as a country, as their motherland. [. . .] Neither juridical documents nor historical facts give any reasons to assert that because of the Great October Revolution the international legal subjecthood of Russia was interrupted.29

It also mattered where in the Soviet Union one thought about these things.

A Ya Esayan from Yerevan State University in Soviet Armenia emphasized that Soviet Russia was identical with Tsarist Russia but after 1917, the other Soviet republics became genuinely independent states and joined the union with Soviet Russia on a voluntary basis. In this sense at least, there could not have been full state identity between the Soviet Union as a whole and imperial Russia.30 However, this view too proceeded from the reality that there was no significant differences between the territories of the two (except mainly for Poland and Finland which were now independent states). Esayan also fol-lowed Zakharova’s concept that changes in the class structure of a state’s gov-ernance do not destroy state continuity as such.31

No further noteworthy theoretical discussions took place on the subject of state identity in the legal scholarship of the Soviet Union. This explains why we can draw the conclusion that the Kozhevnikov– Zakharova position on the state continuity of the Soviet Union with tsarist Russia had been accepted as the predominant interpretation in the Soviet Union. The Russian Empire was the Soviet Union’s past in international law. In terms of international legal subjecthood, for the Soviet Union state continuity with the Russian Empire was a legal fact. It was also a perception expressed in Western scholarship during the 1960s that the state continuity (rather than state succession) per-spective had become dominant in the legal theory of the Soviet Union.32 The political message and consequences of the state continuity doctrine were also significant within the Soviet Union— perhaps tsarist Russia was not such a

‘prison house of peoples’ (Lenin) after all if the Soviet Union controlled almost the same territory; it had been a progressive feature that all these peoples were brought together into one state by the Russian Empire.

When in 1978 the Vienna Convention on Succession of States in Respect

of Treaties was adopted, with a ‘clean slate’ doctrine for newly independent states born out of colonization, Soviet legal scholars, including Mirza Avakov, suggested that this had also been the influence of Soviet Russia and the Soviet Union’s decision not to honour Russia’s tsarist- era debts.33 We should note, however, that the Vienna Convention on Succession of States in Respect of Treaties has been ratified only by a modest number of states (currently not even thirty), and it is unlikely that any of its key controversial principles have become part of customary international law.



Soviet Federalism and the ‘Sovereignty’ of Soviet Republics

We have already seen how Marxist dialectics as a way of tackling and ‘recon-ciling’ contradictions was used in the case of state continuity in the Soviet Union. This enabled the simultaneous argument, namely that the Soviet Union preserved state continuity with the Russian Empire but was not responsible for the Russian Empire’s debts or, generally, key parts of tsarist constitutional reality. Soviet scholarship often responded that it had its own, socialist doc-trine which used the same concepts of classical international law (such as state continuity) but filled them with its own content, different from the ‘bourgeois’ doctrine of the West. At the end of the day, using this kind of dialectic says something profound about the Soviet Union— indeed, as Stalin had already stated on one occasion:

This is the Marxist formula. Is it ‘contradictory’? Yes, it is ‘contradictory’. But this contradiction is Life, and completely reflects Marxist dialectic.34

Another doctrine where a similar dialectic was used concerned the status of Soviet republics in the Soviet Union. That is, Soviet legal scholars developed

33 E D Beishembiev, ‘Rapallskii dogovor i ego rol’ v razvitii sovremennogo mezhdunarodnoga prava (k 60- letiu podpisania dogovora)’, Soviet YBIL 1981, 270– 6 at 275; M M Avakov, ‘Pravopreemstvo gosudarstv v otnoshenii dogovorov (Venskaya konventsia OON 1978 g)’, SGP No 12, 1979, 82– 5 at 85; M M Boguslavsky, V V Tsybukov, ‘Novaya kontseptsia o pravopreemstve gosudarstv’, SGP No 3, 1984, 118– 24.

the theory that both the Soviet Union and the fifteen Soviet republics which formed part of it were sovereign states at the same time. From the compara-tive perspective, this was not entirely unprecedented: for example, the con-stitutional and international legal realities of the Holy Roman Empire of the German Nation and of the Austro- Hungarian Empire had been complex and contradictory.35 The German lawyer Samuel von Pufendorf (1632– 94) had even called the Holy Roman Empire ‘an irregular body resembling a monster’.36

In parallel, there was a historical debate about the status of Soviet republics

in the period 1918– 22. While, for example, Levan Aleksidze from Georgia ar-gued that the federation formed on 30 December 1922 was treaty- based and before that Soviet republics were fully independent states and only in confed-eration with Soviet Russia, some authors from Moscow, such as Lepeshkin, claimed the opposite, namely that even in 1918– 22 ties between Russia and other Soviet republics were federal– constitutional, not based on international law.37 Nikolay Aleksandrovich Ushakov (1918– 2002), Soviet member of the ILC 1967– 86, resolved the contradiction between the two factions of opinion with the help of Marxist dialectics:

Apparently, both moments existed simultaneously in dialectical intercon-nectedness and in the final effect worked in one direction: in the direction of voluntary unification of Soviet republics in a united mighty Soviet Union.38

Ushakov emphasized that every Soviet republic in the Soviet Union was a sov-ereign state.39 At the same time, he recalled the words of the Soviet leader after 1964, Leonid Ilyich Brezhnev: communists always saw the national question through the lens of the class struggle; the solution to this question must be subordinate to the interests of the revolution and socialism.40 In other words, self- determination in terms of nationalism had to be subjugated to proletarian solidarity in the socialist federation. Because of the federalist solution, ‘back-ward’ border regions of the Russian Empire were turned into economically

and culturally developed socialist republics.41 Ushakov concluded his argu-ment with another example of Marxist dialectics, turning a common- sense contradiction into a Soviet legal reality:

The tendency of rapprochement of the peoples of socialist countries is un-breakably connected with the tendency of all- encompassing development of the peoples of those countries, with the strengthening of their sovereignty.42

In other words, by becoming more integrated, Soviet peoples strengthened their sovereignty. A further aspect in this apparent paradox was to say that while in ‘bourgeois’ federations, the units of federation could not be sovereign, the units of Soviet federation could be precisely that. An ‘organic unity’ linked the sovereignties of the Soviet Union and its constituent republics; further-more, when the Soviet republics formed the Soviet Union in 1922, they ‘not only maintained but significantly strengthened their sovereignty’.43 The Soviet constitutional law scholar Manelis argued on this account:

During the period of expanded construction of communism, a further strengthening is taking place in terms of the unity of the sovereignty of the USSR and the sovereignty of union republics. The objective foundation of this process is a new stage of development of interethnic relations charac-terized by further rapprochement of nations and their achieving full unity.44

In non- Russian union republics, the goal of such ‘full unity’ in the Soviet Union was often perceived as further Russification. However, for Manelis, sov-ereignty within the Soviet Union meant that ‘national limitedness, national ex-clusivity’ were alien in terms of the sovereignty of Soviet republics.45

Another Soviet constitutionalist, Shevtsov, maintained that the sovereign-

ties of the union and the Soviet republics were complementary; a dialectical interconnectedness, harmony, and ultimately unity of these sovereignties ex-isted.46 The two Soviet sovereignties were not ‘isolated’ from each other.47 At the same time, the Dean of the Faculty of Law of Tashkent State University

Nationalism is a complicated phenomenon which has many shades and var-ieties. It does not always express itself in open form, and knows how to dis-guise itself. At the contemporary stage there are quite dangerous expressions of nationalism at the level of ideology. With its help, imperialist circles try to shake the political unity of peoples of the USSR, peoples of other socialist countries, and undermine socialist internationalism.51

At the same time, Kormasova was confident that ‘migration processes’ be-tween the Soviet republics— that is, by which the percentage of native Russian speakers grew among the population of other Soviet republics— led to further ‘internationalization’ of the Soviet Union:

Developing based on equal rights, national languages, especially Russian, have a benevolent effect on the progressive development of the process of internationalization of the spiritual life of socialist nations.52

However, people in several Soviet republics— not least in the Soviet- occupied Baltic States— often perceived these tendencies in the Soviet Union as at a con-scious state effort towards further Russification.

A wave of legal scholarship from each of the Soviet republics asserted that

these were sovereign states— while at the same time accepting all those caveats, which were formulated by legal scholars in Moscow.53 Because the Ukrainian and Byelorussian SSR were accepted as founding member states of the UN in 1945, the claim to sovereign status of the Soviet republics came particularly strongly from these republics, in terms of scholarship perhaps particularly from Byelorussia.54 Soviet legal scholars were also acutely aware of a deep scepticism in the West as to whether the Soviet republics were ‘sovereign’ in any meaningful way; however, this was brushed away by citing the ‘imperi-alism’ of Western states.55 Western critics of the official concept of the sover-eignty of the Soviet republics were labelled ‘falsifiers’.56

In the 1970s, Henn- Jüri Uibopuu (1929– 2012), an Austrian international

lawyer born in the interwar Republic of Estonia, argued that the Soviet theory of the sovereignty of Soviet republics was a ‘fiction’ directed more to the out-side world because it offered an opportunity to potentially attract other coun-tries to join the Soviet Union and save the face of its new constituent parts at the same time as they would not have to lose their sovereignty in the process.57 According to Uibopuu, the Uzbek SSR in particular served as a Soviet show-case model for Asian and African states.58 However, I would argue that even more important still was the Soviet domestic dimension, which was imperial and whose purpose was to mislead: that is, by asserting the legal theoretical ‘sovereignty’ of Soviet republics, the Soviet Russian imperial centre could

Self- Determination during the Cold War:

Soviet Self- Representation Abroad and at Home

Soviet endorsement of the right of peoples to self- determination has been dis-cussed in a monograph written by the German international lawyer Johannes Socher59 so there is no need to repeat his detailed and highly relevant research findings here. Soviet legal scholars took pride in the fact that the USSR sup-ported inclusion of the right of peoples to self- determination in the 1945 nego-tiations leading to adoption of the UN Charter as well as the 1966 UN human rights covenants in which self- determination occupies a prominent place in Article 1 of both covenants. The Soviet approach was, of course, that Russia had progressively resolved its imperial and self- determination issues in 1917 whereas the West had not; therefore, the Soviet Union actively supported de-colonization efforts concerning the West European colonial empires.

However, at the Bandung conference of 1955, some governments of the non-

communist ‘Third World’ states raised the sincerity of Soviet rhetoric towards issues of self- determination, empire, and colonialism. To start with, there was a certain disagreement in Bandung over what constituted ‘colonialism’ and ‘im-perialism’.60 For example, at the plenary meeting in Bandung the representa-tives of Iraq, Iran, the Philippines, Turkey, and Pakistan uttered the accusation that Soviet communism was neocolonialism, while Ceylon (today’s Sri Lanka) made a similar charge in the political committee.61 Ceylon’s prime minister Kotelawala commented that ‘if we are united in our opposition to colonialism, should it not be our duty openly to declare our opposition to Soviet coloni-alism as much as to Western imperialism?’— an idea that drew some support in Bandung.62



59 Johannes Socher, Russia and the Right to Self- Determination in the Post- Soviet Space, OUP, 2021.

60 Luis Eslava, Michael Fakhri, Vasuki Nesiah (eds) Bandung, Global History, and International Law. Critical Pasts and Pending Futures, CUP, 2017, 21, 30.

61 Chen Yifeng, ‘Bandung, China, and the Making of World Order in East Asia’, in: Luis Eslava, Michael Fakhri, Vasuki Nesiah (eds) Bandung, Global History, and International Law. Critical Pasts and Pending Futures, CUP, 2017, 177– 95 at 186.

62 Rose Parfitt, ‘Newer is Truer’, in: Eslava et al (eds), Bandung, Global History, and International Law, 2017, 51.

However, the outcome of the ‘imperialism and colonialism’ debate con-

cerning the Soviet Union was less evident after Bandung. One social move-ment created as a direct result of the Bandung conference was the Afro- Asian People’s Solidarity Organization (AAPSO). This created the Afro– Asian Writers’ Conference, which held its inaugural meeting in 1958 in Tashkent, capital of the Uzbek SSR.63 Soviet writers of course praised the ‘spirit of Bandung’64 and Soviet advances to Asian and African countries were based on the assumption that this was a meeting of likeminded nations, all strug-gling against (Western) imperialism and colonialism. However, from a real-istic viewpoint it was also the expression of Bismarck’s maxim that ‘my enemy’s enemy is my friend’. Soviet support to Afro– Asian liberation movements also demonstrates that no critics of empires are as motivated and dedicated as rival great (imperial) powers.

When the Colonial Independence Declaration of 1960 was prepared at the

UNGA, the Soviet Union even demanded a binding date (the end of 1961) to be fixed for the abolition of colonialism. Johannes Socher points out the hyp-ocrisy of the Soviet position:

This championship of the termination of colonial rule based on self- determination was, of course, based on extreme hypocrisy: the Soviet Union could only support calls for ‘national liberation’ in Africa and Asia without risking its territorial integrity because it presented itself as a commonwealth of free and equal nations.65

In 1960, although many states at the UNGA identified the Soviet Union as a colonial power, references to geographical separation nevertheless domin-ated as an objective prerequisite (the so- called saltwater test). Geographical separation was one of the elements to determine which territories qualified as ‘non- self- governing’ in a UNGA resolution which defined the basic colo-nial unit.66 In that sense, supporting and ensuring success for such a distinc-tion at the UNGA was also a remarkable success of Soviet diplomacy; one that favoured the history and interests of the two Eurasian continental empires, es-pecially Russia (the Soviet Union). Important in this regard was also the Soviet

leader Nikita Khruschev’s speech at the UNGA on 23 September 1960. In his speech, Khruschev maintained forcefully that, in Russia, everything imperi-alist and colonialist came to an end with the Bolshevik revolution in 1917.67 Khruschev’s comments at the UNGA so aptly summarize the Soviet self- image and discourse about Russia’s imperial history that they are worth quoting at greater length here:

It may be said that it is easy for the Soviet Union to advocate liquidation of the colonial system, since the Soviet Union has no colonies. Yes, that is so. We have no colonies and no capital in other countries. But there was a time when many of the nationalities inhabiting our country suffered the bitter oppression of tsarism, of the landlord– bourgeois system. Conditions in remote areas of the tsarist Empire hardly differed from those of colonies because their populations were cruelly exploited by the autocracy, by capit-alism. Whereas the autocracy looked upon the peoples of Central Asia and Transcaucasia, and other nationalities inhabiting the Russian Empire, as a source of profit, after the October Revolution, when these peoples obtained complete freedom, they quickly improved their economic, cultural, and so-cial condition.

Let us take, for example, the Soviet Republics of Central Asia. Today,

Kazakhstan, Uzbekistan, Kirghizia, Turkmenistan, Tadzhikstan— all the sister republics of Central Asia— have been transformed from backward colonies of tsarist Russia into advanced, industrially developed socialist republics.68

Khruschev continued in similar vein:

In the borderlands of Russia, the tsarist Government pursued an essentially colonialist policy which differed little from what can be observed today in colonial countries: Uzbeks, Kazakhs, Tadzhiks and other non- Russian na-tionalities were scornfully called ‘aliens’. They were not considered human beings and were ruthlessly exploited. National differences, hatred and dissen-sion were fomented between these nationalities, and the tsarist Empire was held together only by bayonets and oppression. When the peoples of Central Asia and Transcaucasia were given their national freedom and equal rights

with the other peoples of Russia, they showed their capabilities in the devel-opment of their national economy and culture.

Did the development of our country suffer by granting to the peoples the

right of independence and self- determination? Is there strife and enmity be-tween nationalities in our multi- national country or a disintegration of the State? No, there is nothing of the sort, nor can there be.69

This was the Soviet self- image. However, upon critical examination of Soviet history and reality, questions remained. In this context, Socher’s conclusion on the Soviet approach to the right of peoples to self- determination is pertinent:

While the Soviet Union may therefore rightly be called one of the strongest and most aggressive supporters of self- determination outside its own sphere of influence in the context of decolonization specifically [. . .] Soviet doctrine on self- determination played a second, highly contradictory and— one might say perverted— role inside the Eastern bloc.70

Here I would add: not only in the Eastern bloc generally but— equally importantly— also inside the Soviet Union itself. When we contrast what Soviet leaders and diplomats said abroad and how self- determination issues were presented at home, in the Soviet Union, then it appears that these were two different worlds; indeed, presented differently in the same Soviet law jour-nals. Again, examples can be drawn from the leading Soviet journal of public law, Sovetskoe gosudarstvo i pravo, which naturally enough included a con-demnation of Western colonialism,71 while at the same time the patronizing imperialist tone about non- Russian peoples of the Soviet Union also persist-ently came through in discussions on implementing self- determination within the Soviet Union.

Soviet Russian legal scholars emphasized that the initial heavy blow to

the system of colonialism had already been delivered by Russia’s October

Revolution in 1917.72 Lev Matveevich Entin (b. 1928), at that time an asso-ciate professor at the University of Stalinabad (today’s Dushanbe), criticized Western approaches to decolonization, arguing that it was a ‘pseudo demo-cratic disguise’ by Western colonial powers when they pretended that decol-onization was of a voluntary nature and started to extend political rights to the population in colonies.73 Yuri Adol’fovich Yudin (1926– 2000), who headed Soviet legal studies of African countries, criticized constitutional reforms car-ried out by the colonial powers in Africa along with constitutions thus adopted, with the apparent purpose of actually slowing down the process of decoloniza-tion in Africa.74 Yet Soviet diplomacy at the UN and legal scholars writing in the Soviet Union were unanimous that decolonization was necessary in order to implement self- determination.75 Discussing the important 1960 UNGA session— in which the programmatic Khruschev speech already cited was delivered— Soviet legal scholars condemned Canadian Prime Minister John Diefenbaker (1895– 1979) who declared at the UN that the USSR, too, was a colonial country because of the situation of peoples there who were not ethnic Russian.76 Much later, when the UNGA had adopted (1974) the Charter of Economic Rights and Obligations of States, expressing ideas about the new international economic order from the Third World perspective, Grigory Tunkin with a co- author argued that the principles proclaimed in the Charter did not yet have the quality of international law but also pointed out that the main obstacle for their maturation into international law was imperialism with its intrinsic tendency to exploit less developed nations.77 These Soviet posi-tions of course implied that imperialism equalled Western imperialism.

At the same time, in narratives with respect to Soviet peoples, it was common

in the Soviet Union, especially during the decolonization period in the West, to emphasize the leading role of the Russian people in the historical processes which covered the evolution from tsarist Russia to the Soviet Union. For ex-ample, G T Taymanov wrote about Soviet Kazakhstan as follows:

All peoples living in our homeland saw their liberation in the unification with the Great Russian people which organized and rallied them to fight against tsarist autocracy and foreign invaders. Taymanov concluded with the already familiar Soviet dialectic on sover-eignty: the voluntary entry of the Kazakh SSR to the Soviet Union is ‘one of the brightest expressions of its sovereignty’.79

Another legal scholar, Kh P Vakhidov from Tashkent (Uzbekistan), argued

In the 1880s, Central Asia was finally united with Russia. By its objective con-sequences, the fact of unification of Central Asia with Russia was a deeply progressive event, determining the fate of the peoples of Central Asia. [. . .] A lasting union between the workers of Central Asia with the Great Russian people took shape. The Russian people helped the peoples of Central Asia to liberate themselves from social and national repression.80

The very same Soviet legal scholar postulated further that establishing tight economic and cultural connections with Russia was in Central Asia’s own interest because Russia ‘stood incomparably higher in the economic and cul-tural sense’.81 In contemporary conditions, too, argued the same scholar, the Central Asian peoples could ‘lean on brotherly help from the Great Russian people’.82 Another Soviet legal scholar, Sh Z Urazaev, emphasized the ‘enor-mous role of the Soviet federation in ensuring necessary help in the social– economic, cultural, and constitutional development of small nations on the part of a more developed and stronger nation’.83 Urazaev emphasized that

Soviet Russia showed intense involvement and interest in the constitutional reorganization of ‘border regions’ and provided them with ‘necessary help’ in the process.84 At the same time, other authors emphasized that the political reorganization of Central Asia in the Soviet Union was all bottom- up and ex-pressed the will of their peoples.85

Another Soviet legal scholar, M Fedorov, explained how Yakutia, with ‘de-

cisive brotherly help from Russians and other Soviet nations’ managed to achieve socialism, ‘bypassing the capitalist stage of development’.86 His col-league, V A Zibarev from Tomsk, discussed the situation with the peoples of the Russian Arctic regions and referred to their ‘utter backwardness’ historic-ally.87 The fact that the Soviet Union organized these peoples in national ter-ritories within the Russian RSFSR testified to the fact that they now enjoyed sovereignty, the right to self- determination, and political equality,88 added Zibarev before concluding:

The historical experience of the peoples of the North completely refutes fab-rications by neo- colonizers about the impossibility of peoples who are de-velopmentally retarded to have their own state existence. The development of the peoples of the North proved the possibility of creation of national statehood.89

What these examples reveal is that in the Soviet Union quite a widespread effort was made in domestic Soviet legal discourse to convince Soviet citizens— and especially legal specialists— that self- determination had been achieved for everyone under the leadership of the Russian people. What this discourse helped to ensure was that, compared to tsarist Russia, during the 1950s and 1960s the Soviet Union would not lose any territory because of self- determination claims in the style of Western colonies. The tone of Soviet discourse on the right of peoples to self- determination was very different re-garding the Soviet Union itself, on the one hand, and the outside world (the former West European empires), on the other. The Cold War: Soviet Views on Treaties,

Peaceful Coexistence, and Socialist

International Law as Imperial Doctrines



The imperial doctrines to be examined in the following concern Soviet post- 1945 views on treaties (especially possibilities for their termination), peaceful coexistence, the great power status of the Soviet Union, and socialist inter-national law.



Treaty Law and Clausula Rebus Sic Stantibus

As discussed in this book in the context of both tsarist Russia and the interwar Soviet Union, questions of the validity and termination of treaties have been central for the solution of Russia’s imperial status and territorial issues. In the Second World War, the Soviet Union expanded territorially and, on sev-eral occasions, the price for this was Soviet violations of previous treaties. In December 1939, the Soviet Union was— as we have seen— expelled from the League of Nations, essentially because of its violation of the League of Nations Covenant vis- à- vis Finland (and its refusal to undergo the dispute settlement mechanism foreseen in the Covenant). The question also arose of the context in which, in 1945, the Soviet Union terminated its earlier (1941) pact of non- aggression and neutrality with Japan. This meant that Soviet international law-yers had to somehow make sense of all this contradictory practice, of which contemporaries were keenly aware, even if in the Soviet Union they did not definitively know about important aspects such as the secret protocols of the Nazi– Soviet non- aggression pact.

Following the execution of Evgeny Pashukanis in 1937, the leading Soviet

international lawyer was Andrey Yanuarevich Vyshinsky (1883– 1954). Vyshinsky was another example of how expertise and authority in legal aca-demia was combined with high positions in the Soviet government: he started



Russia, the Soviet Union, and Imperial Continuity in International Law. Lauri Mälksoo, Oxford University Press.

© Lauri Mälksoo 2025. DOI: 10.1093/ 9780198955962.003.0007

out as a prosecutor in the Stalinist show trials during the 1930s and later be-came Soviet foreign minister in 1949, after Molotov fell out of favour with Stalin. At the end of the 1940s, Vyshinsky coedited the Diplomatic Dictionary, which explained as follows about termination of treaties. First, more than 40 per cent of treaties contained a termination clause:

However, in recent times the opinion is widespread that even when such a special clause is not present, denunciation is always possible. Several cases of unilateral termination of a treaty have occurred in practice on the basis of a radical change in the international situation.1

Reading between the lines, it was unambiguously clear that much of the rele-vant practice also happened to be Russian and Soviet practice before and during World War II. Another article in the Diplomatic Dictionary which dealt with the fate of the Soviet– Polish Riga Peace Treaty of 18 March 1921 stated:

[It] was operational until September 17, 1939 when— in connection with the collapse of the Polish state— the Soviet government annulled all treaties and agreements which had been concluded with Poland. Western Ukraine and Western Byelorussia, in correspondence with the will of the population of these oblasts, joined the USSR.2

In the aftermath of the Second World War, the leading Soviet international lawyer specializing in the law of treaties was Vladimir Mikhailovich Shurshalov (1913– 90), whose work as an international law scholar took him to the State Institute of Law in Moscow as well as to the Peoples’ Friendship University in Moscow as a professor and, towards the end of his life, to the Diplomatic Academy.3 What Shurshalov had to offer was a copy of the tsarist- era (Martens’) doctrine of the indispensability of clausula rebus sic stantibus, with a Marxist– Leninist emphasis on economic factors. Shurshalov explained:

Notwithstanding the specifics of international relations, the reality and val-idity of the norms of international law as well as these or other stipulations in international treaties depend in the result on the economic conditions that gave birth to them. From this viewpoint, only international treaties that

1 A Y Vyshinsky (ed) Diplomaticheskii slovar, Vol II, article ‘Mezhdunarodnye dogovory’, Moscow: Gosudarstvennoe izdatel’stvo politicheskoi literatury, 1950, 138.

2 Ibid, 511 (Article ‘Rizhskii mirnyi dogovor 1921’).

correspond to objective conditions are real and effective. A treaty that is con-trived will be changed by life as something alien to it, not corresponding to its needs. The failure of these or other international plans, agreements, or unions is often explained precisely by the fact that they appear to be built on subjective wishes, without consideration for the actual needs and relations of life. [. . .] Whatever the deadline of a treaty, its implementation will stop in practice if the objective conditions which preceded it cease to exist, since as a rule the scope and purpose of a treaty reflect the changing needs of inter-national life. On the other hand, the duration of treaties serves as a yardstick of their actual correspondence to international life. This has first order im-portance for understanding the nature of clausula rebus sic stantibus. ‘Eternal’ treaties last no longer than those concrete international relations which they are called upon to regulate. When those relations change drastically, inter-national treaties— notwithstanding their ‘eternality’— will stop being applied and become a matter of history. When the connection between an inter-national treaty with the needs of international life is violated, external guar-antee [of the treaty] turns into a mere formality.4

The classic Soviet example was the annulment of the Brest- Litovsk Peace Treaty in November 1918, some seven months after it was signed. Shurshalov explained that this particular treaty was a fine example of the impact of pol-itical conditions on the validity of international treaties. In the words of Shurshalov: ‘as the Brest- Litovsk treaty was built on a deplorable political foundation, it could not create a legal basis for peaceful relations between Soviet Russia and Germany.’5

The argumentation in Shurshalov’s monograph on the validity of treaties

climaxed when the author offered a list of situations in which treaties were, in his interpretation, not valid under international law. This read like a list covering almost all significant foreign policy acts of the US and other Western states after the Second World War. According to Shurshalov, the following were invalid:

• International treaties violating the peace and security of nations and

which had an aggressive character: the Washington treaty which estab-lished NATO could serve as an example.6

4 V M Shurshalov, Osnovania deistvitel’nosti mezhdunarodnykh dogovorov, Izdatel’stvo akademii nauk SSSR, Moscow, 1957, 12– 14.

5 Ibid, 36.

6 Ibid, 148.

TreaTy Law and CLausuLa rebus siC sTanTibus 157

• Treaties violating the principle of peaceful coexistence of all states, not-

withstanding their social structure, such as treaties discriminating against socialist countries in trade.7

• Treaties undermining the territorial integrity of a certain state, such as

certain territorial stipulations in the US– Japan ‘peace treaty’ (quotation marks by Shurshalov) which created a special status for Okinawa; also the US treaty with the Kuomintang- led Republic of China.8

• Treaties violating the sovereignty of states, such as the agreement

establishing US military bases in the UK.9

• Treaties that justified intervention in the domestic affairs of other states

such as the 1954 Manila pact and again the 1954 US– Kuomintang China (Taiwan) treaty.10

• Treaties violating equality and mutual benefit, such as unequal treaties

with China, as well as the US Marshall plan.11

• Treaties violating earlier recognized obligations and thus the principle of

pacta sunt servanda being not valid. For example, at the Potsdam confer-ence in 1945 it had been agreed that the victorious allies would decide on the future of Germany together, but now the Western Allies had decided to violate this obligation towards the Soviet Union. The fact of the UK and France having concluded the Paris agreements (with the Federal Republic of Germany) had at the same time gravely violated their obligations based on the Anglo– Soviet and Franco– Soviet treaties from the Second World War, Shurshalov concluded.12

Thus, according to Shurshalov, many important (Western) treaties were, in his view, void and invalid. His concept of international treaties was not entirely consequential as he seemed to measure Soviet and Western— especially US— treaty situations with a different yardstick.

Based on the interwar writings of Pashukanis and the post- Second World

War views of Vyshinsky and Shurshalov, we can say that Soviet views on the use of clausula rebus sic stantibus were extensive and highly permissive, espe-cially regarding the Soviet Union, which was seen as a state with a new social structure and which was therefore allowed to unilaterally terminate previous



7 Ibid, 149.

8 Ibid, 149.

10 Ibid, 151.

11 Ibid, 153.

158 The CoLd war: sovieT views

treaties. Even when clausula rebus sic stantibus was not explicitly mentioned, its justificatory essence in discussions on treaty law usually came through. Thus in 1954 the Soviet ICJ judge, Fedor Kozhevnikov, opined that the conclusion of the Nazi– Soviet non- aggression pact of 23 August 1939 had been ‘striking evi-dence of the wisdom of Soviet foreign policy in those historical conditions’.13

Writing in 1968, the German scholar Theodor Schweisfurth commented

that, in recognizing the doctrine of clausula rebus sic stantibus, Soviet doc-trine was in essence no different from Western international law doctrines.14 Nevertheless, even if the doctrine of clausula rebus sic stantibus had not disap-peared from Western doctrines of international law, by the mid- twentieth cen-tury15 it was not so systemic and blatant as in Soviet doctrine and practice of international law. The primacy of pacta sunt servanda was still usually empha-sized in Western approaches to treaty law because without it an international treaty cannot be more than that described in the perspective of Martens: a snapshot of the balance of power at any given moment of time. It is difficult to find leading Western international lawyers by the 1950s who would have de-fended clausula rebus sic stantibus so extensively as, for example, Shurshalov (or Vyshinsky), or who would have justified Soviet treaty violations with such commitment as Kozhevnikov. Moreover, when we look at Soviet practice of treaties as well, we see that Pashukanis interpreted and Shurshalov— already based on the experience of Soviet policy in the Second World War— reflected Soviet practice quite adequately. This is how Soviet practice regarding treaties had been, especially in the early years of the Second World War (1939– 40). In his analysis of the Soviet approach to treaties, Schweisfurth was primarily looking at the Soviet doctrine of international treaties, though without exam-ining the intrinsic links of these views with Soviet treaty practice and foreign policy needs. In discussions about pacta sunt servanda and clausula rebus sic stantibus, context was everything. Before and after the Second World War, strong support for clausula rebus sic stantibus (Pashukanis, Vyshinsky, and Shurshalov) either paved the way for or justified Soviet territorial aggrandize-ment, forcible return of the territories of the Russian Empire, and beyond.

Soviet international lawyers also justified Soviet termination of the Soviet–

Japanese neutrality treaty on 5 April 1945. The treaty had been concluded on 13 April 1941, that is, during the period when the Nazi– Soviet non- aggression

pact was in force. Galina explained that during the Second World War, Japan’s cooperation with Nazi Germany became more intense; a new situation emerged compared to the moment when the Soviet pact with Japan was con-cluded.16 This was never a convincing explanation because in 1941 cooper-ation between Nazi Germany and imperial Japan had already been very close; but at the same time, throughout the Second World War, Japan still avoided at-tacking the Soviet Union militarily. The Soviet entry into the war against Japan in September 1945 was based on decisions made by Soviet, US, and UK leaders at the Yalta conference in February 1945; this also set the termination of the Soviet– Japanese neutrality treaty of 1941.

Nevertheless, at some point during the Cold War, Soviet international law

doctrine abandoned the previous extensive clausula rebus sic stantibus jus-tification and, if it favoured termination of a treaty, instead referred to viola-tion by the treaty partner instead; this was seen as a legally stronger basis.17 Talalaev made the point that Martens, Korovin, Shurshalov, and other earlier Russian and Soviet legal scholars had been wrong to emphasize clausula rebus sic stantibus so strongly.18 Pacta sunt servanda was increasingly emphasized when the Soviet leadership started to feel that it was necessary— although in-creasingly difficult— to maintain the geopolitical status quo in Europe rather than expand further territorially. For example, after the Paris treaties were concluded on 23 October 1954 with the Federal Republic of Germany and the latter was militarily integrated into NATO, Soviet international lawyers and commentators started to invoke the principle of pacta sunt servanda.19 The ar-gument was that— with the 1945 agreements of Yalta and Potsdam as well as in bilateral UK– Soviet and Franco– Soviet treaties— Soviet treaty partners in the West had been obliged to decide matters related to the future of Germany together with the Soviet Union. Thus, Soviet legal scholars argued that ‘mili-tarizing’ the Federal Republic of Germany (FRG) was a violation of previous agreements with the Soviet Union and simply integrating the FRG into the West without Soviet approval was a violation of international treaties.20 Soviet

legal scholars also asserted that the 1945 Yalta and Potsdam territorial and geopolitical agreements had been international treaties, even though some of them were made in oral form and were not subject to ratification.21

The tandem of Durdenevsky and Krylov claimed that all major Russian

rulers— Vladimir Momomakh, Ivan the Terrible, Peter the Great, Catherine the Great— had strongly supported the principle of pacta sunt servanda.22 At the same time, while Durdenevsky and Krylov invoked the principle of pacta sunt servanda (also referring to a Pravda newspaper editorial of 16 February 1947 which had spoken about the importance of honouring treaties), they also indicated that this underlying principle did not apply to several types of treaties: unequal treaties, treaties imposed on weaker states, and those which ignored the sovereign rights of one party.23 Specifically, pacta sunt servanda did not apply in November 1918 when Soviet Russia annulled the Brest- Litovsk Peace Treaty with Germany.24 It was also clear in this narrative that the Soviet Union would decide itself to which treaties pacta sunt servanda applied and to which not. Almost simultaneously, the same views on this subject were offered by Fedor Kozhevnikov (who at that time was already a judge at the ICJ).25 That said, the volte- face in favour of pacta sunt servanda in parts of Soviet inter-national law theory was completed only— and notably— with the important condition that the principle did not apply to treaties which the Soviet Union had unilaterally terminated.

However, the coexistence of views supporting extensive clausula rebus sic

stantibus (Shurshalov) and respect for pacta sunt servanda in Soviet inter-national law publications in the 1950s demonstrates a certain level of flexibility and freedom to adopt contradictory positions and theories; of course, so long as one did not contradict Soviet raison d’état and the government’s specific positions on international issues. The Soviet international law experts’ choice as to whether to support strong pacta sunt servanda or extensive clausula rebus sic stantibus was not centrally coordinated; yet the predominant views each decade corresponded to the country’s geopolitical situation and needs. However, the more time passed from Soviet violations of treaties during the Second World War and the more the Soviets started to worry about the sus-tainability of their hegemony in Central and Eastern Europe, the greater the

tendency to advocate for a stronger principle of pacta sunt servanda and dis-miss the earlier favourite clausula rebus sic stantibus, forgetting that Soviet policy was based on a completely flexible approach to treaties when the Soviet Union (re- )gained territories in 1939– 45.

During the Cold War, the dominant Western view on Soviet treaty law and

practice was not very favourable.26 Jan F Triska and Robert M Slusser, working at the Hoover Institute of Stanford University, observed that the Soviet theory of treaties was ‘absolutely dependent’ on state practice.27 Their conclusion on Soviet treaty law and practice was the following:

Can the Soviet leaders be trusted to honor the commitments they voluntarily assume in international treaties? Basing one’s answer to this question on the past behavior of the Soviet leaders, one would have to say that the chances that they can be trusted are distinctly poor. Furthermore, the more important the subject of a given treaty and the more directly it is related to Soviet long- term objectives, the better are the chances of the Soviet Union’s honoring the treaty. [. . .] The Soviet discipline of international law performs a subser-vient and supporting function in the formulation of Soviet foreign policy, and there is no reason to think that it will soon acquire a more influential position. Soviet international law specialists have repeatedly demonstrated that they are able to provide promptly and efficiently a defense for any action taken by the Soviet leaders in the field of foreign policy, no matter how crude a violation of [. . .] international law. [. . .] For the reasons stated, we believe that there are no factors in Soviet treaty theory, law or policy that constitute a guarantee that the USSR will honor its commitments under a given treaty.28

During the United Nations Conference on the Law of Treaties in Vienna (1968– 9) the classic question of the proper relationship between the principle of pacta sunt servanda and clausula rebus sic stantibus was of course one of the central strategic issues to be resolved in the codification.29 Commenting on this process, Talalaev repeated the official Soviet statement at the Vienna conference and pointed out that, at the conference, some states such as the

US, Italy, Turkey, and Chile had taken the principle of pacta sunt servanda ‘to the extreme’.30 The Soviet delegate in Vienna emphasized that ‘historical facts and the reality of international life’ were such that, under the auspices of pacta sunt servanda, some states demand respect for treaties which were treaties in name only.31 Throughout history there had been predatory and forced treaties which facilitated colonial conquest and aggression.32 During the diplomatic conference in Vienna, the Soviet Union supported the principle of pacta sunt servanda but the Soviet delegation also emphasized that this principle only applied to ‘legal’ (pravomernye) treaties; the unequal treaties covered by Western imperialist countries were not covered by it.33 This seemed like a big change from Russia’s and the Soviet Union’s earlier support for clausula rebus sic stantibus but in practice the result was not so different from the Soviet perspective.

However, Soviet practice during the Second World War had been much

more strongly based on clausula rebus sic stantibus than authorized by the Vienna codification: Article 62 of the Vienna Convention on the Law of Treaties. During the Second World War, the Soviet Union violated several treaties and then claimed they were no longer valid.

Apart from the strategic question how to relate to pacta sunt servanda, Soviet

diplomacy at the Vienna conference was active on the issue of universal par-ticipation in treaties, criticizing that Western states were willing to unduly re-strict access to certain (regional) multilateral treaties.34 In reality, the debate about the ‘universality of treaties’ was a proxy debate for expanding UN mem-bership to socialist states such as the GDR, the PRC, and socialist Vietnam.35

During the late Soviet period in the 1970s and 1980s, it became mainstream

in Soviet scholarship of international law to support pacta sunt servanda and dismiss clausula rebus sic stantibus as a manifestation of bourgeois abuses.36 Within the Soviet Union, where access to information was tightly controlled, many people were not even aware of the treaty- related circumstances by which

the Soviet Union expanded its territory during the Second World War, or how Soviet international lawyers such as Pashukanis anticipated treaty violations. This new Soviet literature favouring pacta sunt servanda often neglected the fact that leading imperial Russian and Soviet legal scholars had in the past supported extensive use of clausula rebus sic stantibus when the needs of the Russian or Soviet state required a major revision of the status quo, and that they were themselves writing in a state which was already enjoying a new, post- Second World War territorial status quo.



Great Powers and Small States

A further imperially inspired doctrine which the Soviet Union used, espe-cially during the early stages of the Cold War era, was to emphasize that it was a ‘great power’ (velikaya derzhava) in the context of international law and the United Nations, as a founding nation of the UN and permanent member of the UN Security Council.37 The Korean War (1950– 3) also brought to light the veto power of permanent members due to the controversial Security Council voting in which a resolution on Korea was adopted without Soviet participa-tion (as the Soviet delegation boycotted the Council’s meeting over the ques-tion which government was entitled to represent China). Soviet scholarly opinio juris was unanimous: the ‘unanimity of the great powers’ was a founda-tional constitutional principle at the UN and thus sacrosanct.38 This view was consistently defended in the decades that followed beyond the Korean War.39 The UN Charter acknowledged that the great powers played a ‘special role’.40 Ushakov used Marxist dialectics to explain the importance of the principle of unanimity of great powers: while not contradicting the principle of sovereign equality at the UN, the principle of unanimity of great powers was one of the most important means of implementing and strengthening the principle of

sovereign equality.41 During the later stages of the Cold War, the phrase ‘unity of the great powers’ faded away in Soviet doctrine to an extent, but principled defence of the veto power of permanent members of the UN Security Council remained.42

In the history of international law, the idea that the ‘great powers’ played a

special role had often been present but it was understood as a political reality or principle, not a legal one. However, after the Second World War, Soviet international lawyers— including Grigory Tunkin— started to emphasize that this was now anchored in international law, and smaller states had to accept key agreements between great powers as well.43

However, the Soviet discourse on ‘great powers’ was more widespread and

systemic than just in the context of the UN; it was more like a lens through which to see the international community. Thus, Soviet authors would high-light that the USSR was also a ‘great sea power’44 and point out that the most challenging disputes were those between great powers.45 Moreover, Soviet legal scholars would also mean it as a sincere recognition of other nations con-sidered friendly, such as in statements that China or India are ‘great powers’.46 But it could also mean a warning and criticism of rivals, for example— when relations between the USSR and the PRC had already turned sour— when saying that the PRC had turned itself into a ‘mighty militarist great power’.47

The great power doctrine had many implications, including in the context

of the law of the sea. For example, Soviet claims against the backdrop of trad-itional understandings of internal waters were extensive. These claims also constituted vivid examples of state continuity between the Russian Empire of the tsars and the Soviet Union. To illustrate, when the Soviet Union made extensive claims, the argument was usually based on tsarist- era explorations, adoption, or legal acts. For example, the question of rights over the Arctic seas and islands became highly topical in the Soviet Union as early as the 1920s.48 At the end of the Stalin era, Vyshnepolsky argued that all seas in the Russian Arctic sector— the Kara, Laptev, East Siberian, and Chukchi Seas (the latter

within the limits of the Soviet polar sector)— had to be recognized as Soviet in-ternal waters and therefore closed seas and areas of Soviet sovereignty.49 His ar-gument was that Tsar Ivan Grozny had already regarded the Arctic seas as ‘our land’50 and that Russian legal acts had been issued such an ukaz of Tsar Boris Godunov in 1619 regarding the Kara Sea.51 Another reason why, according to Vyshnepolsky, sovereignty over the Arctic seas had to belong to the Soviet Union was that they were seas of a special kind, sui generis.52 Moreover, the Northeast Passage was never an international waterway, either juridically or de facto.53 These claims were based on the sectoral theory according to which the Soviet Union would claim unusually extensive sovereignty in the Arctic seas, as an exception to the generally accepted treaty or customary rules of the law of the sea.

Another example concerned the Peter the Great Gulf, the largest gulf in the

Sea of Japan, near Vladivostok. On 21 July 1957, the Soviet Union declared that for historical reasons Peter the Great Gulf constituted Soviet internal waters.54 The US, Great Britain, and Japan protested this claim and designation. The argumentation of Soviet international lawyers focused inter alia on a Russian legal act of 1901, ‘Rules for the production of marine fisheries in the territorial waters of the Amur Governorate General’.55 The Soviet legal scholar Romanov argued that this was a case of state succession, in that the Soviet Union simply inherited tsarist Russia’s rights in the Peter the Great Gulf.56 In our own time, too, in the twenty- first century, Russian international lawyers— referring to state practice— claim that Peter the Great Gulf is Russian historical waters.57

The notion of great powers also implied a sense of great power rivalry,

which always had a spatial dimension. As the Soviet Union established its he-gemony over Central and Eastern Europe after the Second World War, it pro-moted the idea that the rules of navigation on the Danube River were in need of revision by the riparian states.58 The result was a diplomatic conference in Belgrade which resulted in the signature of the Convention regarding the

166 The CoLd war: sovieT views

regime of Navigation on the Danube on 18 August 1948. This convention re-placed the Convention instituting the Definitive Statute of the Danube which was signed at Paris on 23 July 1921. The main difference was that powers such as France, the UK, Belgium, Italy, and Greece— who had been co- signatories of the 1921 Convention and had seats at the table on management of Danube River matters— were now excluded. In preparation for the Belgrade confer-ence, France, the UK, and the US raised the argument of acquired rights but, for example, the foreign minister of socialist Hungary, Erik Molnár (1894– 1966), rejected these claims, arguing that so- called acquired rights based on previous conventions were enjoyed by the great powers at the expense of small riparian states, with the purpose of bringing the Danube under control of imperialist policies and influences.59 The Soviet foreign minister, Andrey Vyshinsky, also attacked the earlier 1921 Convention by pointing out that Soviet Russia had not been a signatory state, even though the Russian Empire used to have a say in regulation of Danube issues in the treaties of 1856 and 1883.60 Thus, we can once again see here that the state continuity claim and the attack on British and French ‘acquired rights’ in a way meant restoration of Russia’s imperial rights of access and participation, while elbowing out other, Western ‘great powers’. In principle, free navigation was ensured in both the 1921 and 1948 conventions, but in terms of access to and management of the river there were also changes in terms of who was in charge. Symbolically it also mattered that the he first language of the original of the 1948 Convention was Russian.

This is also how various great power doctrines related to each other: changes

in the balance of power in favour of the Soviet Union by the end of the Second World War had to be reflected in treaties. However, Soviet attempts did not al-ways successfully invoke clausula rebus sic stantibus in geopolitically sensitive territorial issues. For example, towards the end of the Second World War, the Soviet Union failed in its proposals to Norway to revise the 1920 Spitzbergen (Svalbard) treaty, while Turkey did not agree to revise the 1936 Montreux Convention regulating navigation in the Turkish straits: the Bosporus and the Dardanelles.61 The new balance of power also had its limits and sometimes re-sistance to Soviet pressure proved successful.

GreaT Powers and smaLL sTaTes 167

The opposite of the great powers in Soviet discussions about international

law were ‘smaller states’. These were not defined strictly but the concept was a rather broad one, apparently encompassing most states which were not great powers. In Soviet narratives of international law, ‘small and weak states’ or ‘peoples’ (malye i slabye gosudarstva; narody) needed protection and de-served full rights in the international community.62 They were in constant danger of being abused by large capitalist states such as the US which would not treat them equally.63 At the same time, the Soviet Union, led by Soviet Russia, had gathered ‘small and weak’ peoples under its wing. Of course, no contradiction was seen in the fact that, at the same time, the Soviet Union was also promoting special rights and prerogatives for the ‘great powers’. However, a certain warning to small states could be found in the Soviet literature on international law: they should consider neutrality instead of joining military blocs adversarial to the communist countries led by the Soviet Union.64 For example, Durdenevsky and Osnitskaya made the point that, for small states in Europe and elsewhere, neutrality was a way of ensuring their independence.65 Soviet international law scholarship was generally quite preoccupied with the institution of neutrality in international law and related historical prece-dents, not only in Europe but also, for example, in Afghanistan, as the Soviet Union preferred states that were not part of the Soviet- led alliance to stay at least neutral.66 However, when citing the neutrality of Switzerland as a positive example, one could ask whether neutrality of this kind would also have been stable— in the immediate geographical neighbourhood of the Russian Empire and the Soviet Union— for states which had in the past seceded from Russia or on whose territory wars involving Russia were carried out (Although general Suvorov also had a Swiss campaign in 1799.)

Peaceful Coexistence, Balance of Power, and Jus Ad Bellum Issues

One of the most popular Soviet slogans during the Cold War in the context of international law was ‘peaceful coexistence’.67 This was also an example of successful migration of ideas, because India and China included ‘peaceful co-existence’ in the preamble to their April 1954 bilateral treaty, as one of the five principles (Panch Shila),68 and a year later it was included in the outcome of the Bandung conference. However, the exact contours and implications of this principle were not always clear. In the ways it was expressed in the count-less interaction points of international law and politics, the Soviet concept of peaceful coexistence had different political connotations in different contexts and times.69 In our context of investigating the imperial and great power back-ground of Soviet interpretations of international law, some Western criticisms of this principle are also of interest. Namely, Boris Meissner (1915– 2003), a West German expert on Soviet Union and international law, highlighted the connection between peaceful coexistence and Realpolitik, making the point that substantively it was surprisingly like the age- old European legal– political principle of the balance of power (iustum potentiae aequilibrium).70 The ra-tionale for this view was also that peaceful coexistence assumed acceptance of the territorial status quo, which was seen as acceptance of Soviet hegemony in Eastern and Central Europe.71

Interwar Soviet legal scholars such as Evgeny Pashukanis had asserted

that balance of power lay behind treaties and generally backed observance of international law.72 However, after the Second World War, the balance of power as a historical concept was already associated with the ‘evil’ power pol-itics of imperialist states, which Soviet legal scholars Natalya Zakharova and

For recent discussion of this principle, see also Kaj Hobér, The Perestroika Era and International Law, Edward Elgar Publishing, 2024, 42 et seq.
