Yuri Barsegov assumed that the Soviet Union had nothing to do with.73 They maintained that, after the Second World War, the balance of power was already directed against the Soviet Union and that several Western authors had postu-lated that such a balance could be achieved in the alliance and cooperation of small states against a great power such as the Soviet Union.74 Zakharova also argued that capitalist states favoured balance of power arrangements because Western security organizations such as NATO were of a closed nature; socialist states were not allowed to join them.75

Even more linked to the old balance of power than the principle of peaceful

coexistence— the exact normative meaning of which remained somewhat nebulous— was a principle which the Soviet Union and Soviet legal scholars started to raise from the 1970s onwards, namely the principle of ‘equal security’ (odinakovaya bezopasnost’; ravnaya bezopasnost’).76 According to Malinin, it meant that no party was entitled to receive one- sided advantages in terms of ensuring its security; it meant, literally, preservation of parity or balance between powers.77 During the late 1970s, other Soviet legal scholars also em-phasized the importance of this parity or balance in international law.78 Titov, discussing the idea of creating a nuclear- free zone in Northern Europe, poten-tially including parts of Soviet territory, maintained that any implementation of such an idea should take ‘balance of power’ as its basis.79 So in practice, al-though on the surface Soviet discourse on international law sounded solidly idealist and revolutionary, the age- old realist balance- of- power thinking never disappeared from among its foundational ideas— even when the concept of balance of power appeared under the guise of different labels.

A key element in ensuring the balance of power was regulation of the use of

military force (jus ad bellum). During the interwar period, Soviet international lawyers had promoted the right of intervention by the USSR as a state of and for the proletariat, whereas after the Second World War— as a consequence of which the USSR could satisfy its territorial and hegemonic appetite— the Soviet approach to jus ad bellum became much more restrictive. international lawyers were against expanding the right to self- defence and, as a rule, favoured the narrowest interpretation of when military force could be used (self- defence only if an armed attack occurred or with UN Security Council authorization).80 However, wars of national liberation against imperi-alist states were considered both legal and legitimate.81

As we will see in the next section, according to the Soviet approach, these

rules and considerations did not apply to relations between the Soviet Union and other socialist countries. The Soviets also interpreted the right to carry out military intervention by invitation82 extensively, especially justifying Soviet military intervention in Afghanistan after 1979. Although the UNGA condemned the Soviet invasion of Afghanistan, in Soviet legal narratives the US and other Western states which supported the Afghan mujahedeen fighters were acting illegally under international law.83

During the 1970s, a special dimension of non- use of force was Soviet

interest in promoting the aspect of the inviolability of borders;84 this became a key component of the Helsinki Accords (1975) and thus part of international law, at least within Europe.85 As early as during the 1960s, Secretary- General Nikita Khruschev had proposed concluding a treaty which would have high-lighted the illegality of the use of force for changing state borders.86 At the 25th Soviet Communist Party Congress in 1976, the Soviet Union made a proposal to conclude a new universal treaty on the non- use of force, referring to the ex-istence of nuclear weapons in the world as a new circumstance compared to the time of adoption of the UN Charter in 1945.87

PeaCefuL CoexisTenCe and baLanCe of Power 171

Peaceful coexistence and balance of power were also directly related to nu-

clear weapons in the sense of who had them and who did not. After the Soviet Union managed to acquire nuclear weapons in 1949, it continued to promote disarmament and a ban on nuclear tests, and even nuclear weapons as such.88 According to Soviet diplomatic proposals, the use of nuclear weapons had to be banned altogether; or the Soviet Union and the Warsaw Pact countries— though the Soviet Union was the only nuclear state in the group— pledged not to be the first to use nuclear weapons in a military conflict.89 However, the Soviet Union’s rivals also regarded these proposals against the backdrop of Soviet proposals in the 1930s for complete disarmament— in other words, ac-knowledging the propagandistic element to them.

On 14 December 1979 the UNGA adopted— on a Soviet initiative— a reso-

lution entitled ‘Inadmissibility of the policy of hegemonism in international relations’ in which states (at page 5) inter alia resolutely condemned ‘imperi-alism, colonialism and neo- colonialism’. At the same time, the document also reflected Sino– Soviet rivalries and the so- called Sino– Soviet split during the Cold War, from 1960 onwards. Initially, relations between the PRC and the USSR had been filled with mutual admiration and praise, for example when the Treaty of Friendship was concluded between Mao and Stalin in 1950.90 However, as time went on, the debate about the history of the mutual border— but especially on the nuclear status of the PRC and the leadership claims of the Soviet Union in the socialist bloc— for a while turned the two biggest socialist countries into one another’s biggest critics. The Soviet Union and the People’s Republic of China started to accuse each other of hegemonism.91 The PRC constitution of 1975 even declared the aim of fighting against imperialism and ‘social- imperialism’, which meant the Soviet Union.92 In contrast, Soviet legal

scholars argued that PRC policies at the time in the UN ‘denied international law’ and in particular worked against Soviet proposals for disarmament.93 Mirroring what the Chinese communists stated and noticed about the Soviet Union, Soviet legal scholars wrote that the PRC was discriminating against non- ethnic Chinese peoples in the PRC.94 The Soviet Union (and Soviet legal scholars) also promoted the idea of collective security in Asia, which the PRC criticized as directed primarily against it,95 and criticized states such as Japan and the US for their policies of rapprochement with the PRC.96

The Soviet conflict with the PRC also spoiled the prospects for success of

socialist international law which would have encompassed all socialist states during the Cold War. It fundamentally challenged the Marxist– Leninist prop-osition that relations between socialist countries were on a qualitatively dif-ferent level. Moreover, Soviet international law theoreticians did not have a good answer to this conflict- ridden reality, which was there for everyone to see. When Soviet international lawyers published a six- volume showcase trea-tise on international law with its last volume dedicated specifically to socialist international law, all they could do with respect to the PRC was to criticize Maoist policies.97 Nevertheless, as we will see next, the Soviet Union demon-strated the real- life consequences of socialist international law— particularly decisively in Central Europe.



Socialist International Law

A special Soviet hegemonic doctrine was ‘socialist international law’.98 While in principle Soviet legal scholars wanted it to be applied throughout the socialist or socialist- leaning world— for example, also in Mongolia and Cuba— 99its

main area of application was Central and Eastern Europe. Among its most visible effects was that it was used to justify Soviet military interventions in Hungary (1956) and Czechoslovakia (1968). In 1956, this theory was based on interpretations of the Soviet government’s declaration of 30 October 1956 which justified the intervention in Hungary on the basis of ‘proletarian inter-nationalism’.100 The UNGA and Western organizations also became involved and Soviet intervention against Hungary was condemned as a violation of international law.101 However, at least in their domestic discourse, leading Soviet international lawyers presented a narrative that it had been imperi-alist states which had violated international law in Hungary, by supporting the ‘counterrevolutionary rebellion’.102

The first Soviet Yearbook of International Law was published just after the

Soviet intervention in Hungary, and the main point of Evgeny Korovin’s art-icle on ‘Proletarian Internationalism and International Law’ was to justify that intervention.103 According to Korovin, a new type of international rela-tions had emerged among the socialist states,104 where— so went Korovin’s narrative— no antagonistic controversies arose, nor was there any place for fighting or animosity:105 a somewhat surprising assertion after Soviet mili-tary suppression of the Hungarian uprising. Korovin continued that the main point of socialist international law was that, among socialist states, established concepts of international law— sovereignty, equality, non- intervention, and the like— acquired a new content, a new quality. For example, concerning the principle of equality of states, this meant that the equality of socialist states rad-ically differed from the ‘equality’ between imperialist and smaller states, pos-tulated Korovin.106 An example of the equality between socialist nations was the Soviet– Hungarian declaration of 28 March 1957— adopted after Soviet military suppression of the revolution— which, in the spirit of socialist inter-nationalism, was concerned with the ‘welfare of workers in both countries’.107 Korovin maintained:

Exactly because of this goal and in the interests of the socialist camp as a whole, the Soviet Union offered and offers to Hungary comprehensive eco-nomic and other help so that the Hungarian people could as quickly as pos-sible overcome the consequences of the counterrevolutionary mutiny and pursue the path of further material and cultural advancement.108

Moreover, just as the principle of equality of states meant something else for socialist states, the same was the case with sovereignty generally. For example, the Soviet– Albanian declaration of 17 April 1957 emphasized that ‘only in the commonwealth of socialist states’ did Albania ‘for the first time in its history’ acquire and enjoy full national sovereignty. Moreover, this statement about the centrality of exercising sovereignty together, in a commonwealth, was true for all socialist states.109 In the words of Korovin, again referring to dialectics:

What is genuinely beneficial for a socialist state is always beneficial also to the whole socialist camp. What is beneficial to the whole socialist camp is uncon-ditionally beneficial to every country which forms a part of it.110

For Korovin, the final conclusion of the Hungarian ‘mutiny’ was that the at-tempt by the ‘renegade’ Hungarian leader Imre Nagy (1896– 1958) to turn Hungary away from the Warsaw Pact— which was founded in 1955— and de-clare Hungary neutral was futile: ‘not a single socialist state can expect its se-curity to be ensured by proclaiming its neutrality.’111

Sebastian Gehrig has highlighted Korovin’s writings on socialist inter-

national law and argues that Korovin concentrated on technocratic and eco-nomic cooperation as examples of an emerging socialist international legal system.112 Gehrig thus sees strong similarities between the Soviet doctrine of socialist international law and Western functionalist scholars on regional in-tegration. However, to the extent that Korovin’s writings also justified Soviet military intervention in the commonwealth of socialist states, the essence of socialist international law was not merely technocratic but was also geopolit-ical: based on the resolution to use force within the socialist commonwealth, if necessary.

Another article reacting to the Hungarian events of 1956 was by Grigory

Tunkin, who argued that when relations between socialist and capitalist states were governed by the overarching principle of peaceful coexistence, then be-tween socialist states such an overarching principle was different— proletarian internationalism.113 It all boiled down to the understanding of the right of peo-ples to self- determination. Indeed, the following further passage from Tunkin in many ways sums up the Soviet imperialist approach to international law at the time:

The principle of proletarian internationalism is dialectically connected with other postulates of Marxist– Leninist theory and first of all with the principle of self- determination of nations. [. . .] In contrast to bourgeois nationalists, conflating the right to self- determination with secession, with fragmentation of a centralized state into small state units, Marxism– Leninism sees the in-ternal dialectical connection between the principle of self- determination of peoples and the principle of proletarian internationalism. V I Lenin indicated that it is impermissible to confuse the question of the right of a nation to se-cede freely from the question whether to do so makes sense in this or another moment for each specific nation. Recognition and protection of the right of peoples to self- determination does not at all exclude the struggle for unifica-tion of the proletariat of various nations, for the unification of socialist states. Between these principles exists dialectical unity.114

As already exemplified earlier, the dialectical method was an important tool in Soviet approaches to international law, a way to overcome apparent contradic-tions and even offend against common logic. We will return to more of these examples later.

During negotiations in January 1948, Stalin clashed with Yugoslav negoti-

ators over how to shape relations in the socialist bloc and what rights the USSR would have over its socialist satellites.115 In his 1959 article, Tunkin criticized Yugoslavian ‘revisionists’ such as the Union of Yugoslavian Communists, who argued that between socialist states, too, relations should be based on general— not particular— socialist international law.116 It was commonly

114 Ibid, 89.

115 Arno Trültzsch, Sozialismus und Blockfreiheit. Der Beitrag Jugoslawiens zum Völkerrecht 1948– 1980/ 91, Wallstein Verlag, 2021, 124.

a view in Soviet publications at the time that the Yugoslavs had not under-stood the principle of proletarian internationalism correctly.117 Later, when Albania sided with Mao’s PRC, Soviet authors also criticized the Albanian leadership for not having correctly understood the essence of proletarian internationalism.118

Furthermore, in his 1959 article Tunkin highlighted the importance of pri-

oritizing socialist international law in relations between socialist countries:

To argue that relations between socialist countries must be regulated only by principles of general international law means denying the different class character of relations among socialist countries, sliding onto the rails of non- partisanship, sliding into the swamp of bourgeois normativism.119

A Soviet legal scholar who took an interest in issues of socialist international law was Evgeny Trofimovich Usenko (1918– 2010). Usenko postulated that, in general international law, the key principle was peaceful coexistence, whereas in socialist international law it was proletarian internationalism. This meant that all key concepts of general international law acquired a new meaning for relations among socialist states: for example, the principle of non- use of force did not even exist between socialist countries as ‘it lacked any founda-tion’ between them.120 The mistake made in 1956 by the Hungarian leader— the ‘right- wing revisionist’— Imre Nagy was to look at his country’s status only through the prism of ‘petty bourgeois nationalism’ and equality of states, without considering the aspect of proletarian internationalism between the Soviet Union and Hungary.121 Usenko wrote on these issues regularly, for ex-ample highlighting in 1970 that state sovereignty was important for socialist nations but not in terms of the survival of nationalism or national limited-ness.122 Usenko’s core interpretations of socialist international law remained the same over the Soviet period; only the troublesome cases which he warned about changed: when writing in the early 1960s, these were Hungary in 1956

and the Yugoslavian ‘revisionists’,123 then later, in the early 1980s, Poland with its anti- Soviet Solidarność popular movement.124

Grigory Tunkin, too, regularly returned to the themes of socialist inter-

national law, in anticipation of events in Czechoslovakia as well as after the Prague Spring was suppressed in August 1968 by the Soviet Union and its Warsaw Pact allies. Tunkin argued that capitalist and socialist states had a dif-ferent concept of self- determination: while the first created nation states as an expression of self- determination, the socialist concept was based on the brotherhood of nations and their unification.125 Tunkin justified the August 1968 Soviet- led military intervention against Czechoslovakia based on the principles of socialist internationalism.126 This time, one of Tukin’s main lines of defence was a citation from Lenin, namely:

The purpose of socialism is not the destruction of humankind in small states and any kind of isolation of nations, but their rapprochement and their merger.127

At the same time, Tunkin observed that imperialism was kindling nation-alism in the socialist commonwealth as well, as the events in Czechoslovakia in 1968 demonstrated.128 The main point, according to Tunkin, was that in the socialist commonwealth the principles of sovereignty, equality of states, and non- intervention in domestic affairs were subordinated to the higher prin-ciple of socialist internationalism which, however, ‘in no way means denying or belittling the national aspect in these relations’.129 The socialist principles of respect for sovereignty, equal rights, and non- intervention differ qualitatively from the principles of general international law bearing the same name; in so-cialist international law these principles are present to ‘strengthen and develop the brotherly commonwealth of socialist countries’.130 Here, Tunkin relied on another citation from Lenin which supported his understanding of socialist international law:

V I Lenin, formulating the Marxist concept of the self- determination of peo-ples, decisively dissociated himself from the bourgeois concept of this prin-ciple, with its purpose of creating the ‘nation state’. Lenin emphasized that the principle of self- determination of peoples in its Marxist understanding is a means for the rapprochement of nations. [. . .] ‘We demand,’ wrote Lenin, ‘the right of secession of suppressed peoples not because we dream of economic fragmentation or of the ideal of small states, but on the contrary, because we want large states and rapprochement, even merger of nations but on a genuinely democratic, genuinely internationalist basis which is unthinkable without freedom to secede.131

Tunkin concluded that the understanding of sovereignty in socialist inter-national law meant that countries faithful to socialism were obliged to inter-vene and defend socialist sovereignty elsewhere, along the lines of proletarian internationalism, just as five socialist states led by the Soviet Union had done so militarily in Czechoslovakia in August 1968. This was the foundation of a new type of international law— 'the socialist international law of the future’.132 Nevertheless, for some reason Tunkin was later still sometimes superficially seen in the West as someone who represented a ‘retreat from ideology towards greater objectivity’ in Soviet scholarship of international law.133

A further specific phenomenon of socialist international law consisted in

bilateral treaties between the Soviet Union and other socialist states: treaties which— according to Soviet lawyers— formed a ‘system’.134 Soviet legal scholars emphasized that these treaties were meant to defend socialism and its achieve-ments.135 Saltanov referred to the preamble of the Soviet– Czechoslovak Treaty of Friendship of 6 May 1970, proclaiming that ‘the support, strengthening and defense of socialist achievements which have been gained at the price of heroic efforts and self- denying work of both peoples, remains the common international debt to socialist countries’.136 As this example demonstrates, such treaties were sometimes also renegotiated— or, rather, imposed— right after Soviet intervention, again asserting the principle of proletarian internation-alism. At the same time, in the West— for example in the Federal Republic of

Over the last decades of the Cold War, the focus of Soviet international

lawyers in the context of socialist international law was mainly on two international organizations created in the Soviet bloc, one dealing with the economy (the COMECON Council of Mutual Assistance)140 and the other with security (the Warsaw Pact).141 Soviet legal scholars emphasized that— unlike in the capitalist world— the organizations of the socialist common-wealth were based on the complete equality of bigger and smaller states: every member state had the same number of votes.142 This fact did not change much in the Western perception that, de facto, the Soviet role in these organizations of socialist states— and beyond these organizations in Central and Eastern Europe— was hegemonic.143 Writing during the Nazi period in Germany

Increasing Faith in International Law?

The US legal scholar Boris Mamlyuk argues that the Soviets showed ‘increasing faith’ in international law over the course of the Cold War.153 Mamlyuk high-lights that the Soviets felt they had not received what they thought they had been promised by the Western Allies during the Second World War. American officials leaked copies of the original text of the secret protocols of the Nazi– Soviet Non- Aggression Pact of 23 August 1939— which they had received from the German archives— to the Western media and the German defence lawyers at the Nuremberg trials,154 which, according to Mamlyuk, contributed to the Soviet ‘crisis of faith’ regarding international law:

The mainstream historical narrative posits that after facing numerous be-trayals at the hands of Western powers, including the Molotov– Ribbentrop Pact, the Allies’ delay in opening the Western front, and breach of the abovementioned ‘gentlemen’s agreements’ at Nuremberg, the Soviets had a crisis of faith.155

152 Kaj Hobér, The Perestroika Era and International Law, Edward Elgar Publishing, 2024, 85.

153 Boris N Mamlyuk, ‘The Cold War in Soviet International Legal Discourse’, in: Matthew Craven, Sundhja Pahuja, Gerry Simpson (eds) International Law and the Cold War, CUP, 2020, 339– 75 at 365.

154 Francine Hirsch, Soviet Judgment at Nuremberg. A New History of the International Military Tribunal after World War II, OUP, 2020.

In the same context, Mamlyuk also refers to Soviet ‘traumatic international law encounters’.156 This narrative accommodates the Soviet imperial historical perspective because what is seen as a relevant ‘trauma’ lies primarily not in the 1939 Nazi– Soviet division of Eastern Europe but in US officials making it public in 1945, thus disappointing the Soviet Union and disturbing its pre-ferred narratives.

To an extent, we can see ‘increasing faith’ in the way the Soviet doctrine of

international law flips from strong support of clausula rebus sic stantibus to pacta sunt servanda or from support of revolutionary interventions to advo-cacy of a strict prohibition of use of force in international law. However, these changes were related to Soviet conquests in the Second World War, and the country’s territorial saturation as a result. A formerly revisionist power challen-ging the status quo became a keen guardian of its possessions. Nevertheless, as demonstrated in this chapter, Soviet doctrines of international law during the Cold War were to an important extent shaped by Soviet imperialist thought.

From Perestroika to Putin’s Russia:

Swinging Back to the Imperial Concept

of International Law



Many years have passed since the dissolution of the Soviet Union in December 1991. Nevertheless, what particularly interests us is the transformations of mentality and discourses in post- Soviet Russian international law. In the post- Soviet space, the Russian– Georgian war of 2008, Russia’s annexation of Crimea in 2014, and in particular its full- scale military invasion of Ukraine since 2022 have been transformative events. But what mindsets and mentalities in Russian international legal discourses prepared the ground for the country’s aggression against Ukraine?

The Perestroika era was a time of revelations in the Soviet Union. Suddenly

it was possible to question everything that had been the governmentally man-dated or favoured truth in Soviet international and constitutional legal dis-courses. Soviet citizens could finally learn the historical truth about Soviet policies at the beginning of the Second World War— for example, that the Soviet Union had committed aggression against Finland in late 1939 and had executed some 22,000 Polish officers— prisoners of war— in 1940 at Katyn and other locations in the Soviet Union.1 In December 1989, the Soviet parliament declared the secret protocols of the Nazi– Soviet non- aggression pact of 23 August 1939 null and void from the very beginning. At a meeting between the Warsaw Pact members in Moscow on 4 December 1989, the 1968 invasion of Czechoslovakia was declared illegal.2 However, while many in the West cele-brated Gorbachev’s new policies in international law as a grand and peaceful gesture, a remarkable act of goodwill from Moscow, many citizens in Central and Eastern Europe understood it as an end to either an illegal situation or

2 Kaj Hobér, The Perestroika Era and International Law, Edward Elgar Publishing, 2024, 81.

Russia, the Soviet Union, and Imperial Continuity in International Law. Lauri Mälksoo, Oxford University Press.

184 From Perestroika to Putin’s russia

one which did not correspond to their peoples’ preferences. What is so heroic about ending an illegal situation which should never have materialized in the first place?

In the following, I have organized the discussion around the following key-

words, which were already central in earlier chapter(s) concerning the Soviet Union: federalism; continuity versus state succession; ideas about Russia’s great- power status; treaties; and pacta sunt servanda versus clausula rebus sic stantibus.



Federalist Ideas, Self- Determination, and International Law

Post- Soviet Russia has been developing in much the same way as the Soviet Union in the 1920s: initially, federalist ideas were celebrated and even an-chored in ‘treaties’; but soon the centralizing vision of statehood gathered itself and restored the imperial, unitary vision of Russia.

Struggling against the centrifugal process during the last years of the Soviet

Union, Soviet legal scholars who wanted to maintain the territorial integrity of the state suggested going back to the drawing board, arguing that the Soviet Union should be preserved but under new, more democratic conditions. For example, in 1990 three Soviet Russian lawyers proposed:

The Communist Party of the Soviet Union considers that— for the sake of the interests of the peoples themselves and bearing in mind worldwide tenden-cies towards integration— it is important to preserve the integrity of the union as a multinational state. This year we already need to work out a new treaty on the union of the republics as sovereign socialist states, based on a strictly vol-untary nature, mutual benefit, and freedom of national self- determination.3

The only problem with this approach and promise was that it had already been tried, but not with lasting success: in 1922, the Soviet Union was formally based on a treaty but— while the sovereignty of the constituents was emphasized— over time the rights of the constituent parts of the union vanished to the ex-tent that the concept of the ‘sovereignty of union republics’ remained a mere fiction, upheld by the Soviet government and legal scholarship. In response, two Armenian legal scholars wrote in the same journal, Sovetskoe gosudarstvo i



3 BS Krylov, IN Kuznetsov, NA Mikhaleva, ‘Kontseptsia Soyuznogo dogovora’, SGP No 10, 1990, 3– 12 at 3.

Federalist ideas, selF-determination 185

pravo, in 1990: ‘If nowadays someone tries to speak of the development, the ex-pansion of sovereignty, of the Soviet republics, then it transforms into a game of words because in reality [Soviet] republics never enjoyed sovereignty.’4

Some years later, the US legal scholar Jeffrey Kahn put it even more radic-

ally: the USSR was a ‘federal facade that barely masked the most centralized state in modern history’.5

To understand the disintegration of the Soviet Union and the emergence of

the post- Soviet Russian Federation and other post- Soviet states, it is important to keep in mind that the decision- makers of that era had in mind as a key ref-erence point the transformation of the Russian Empire in 1917– 22 through Soviet Russia and other Soviet republics to the Soviet Union. Just as in 1922 the Soviet Union was founded on— and the core Empire preserved thanks to— a treaty, now it was again meant to be about renewal of treaty relations. The failed coup d’état of part of the Soviet leadership in August 1991 was about preserving the Soviet Union as a unitary state. The coup was organized to pre-vent the forthcoming signing of the new union treaty between the Gorbachev- led Soviet government in Moscow and the Soviet republics, which would have changed the nature of the Soviet Union from federation to confederation. With the failure of the coup, secession of the former Soviet republics went ahead re-gardless, so that in December 1991 they became formally independent states. The three Baltic States— which maintained that they had been illegally occu-pied and annexed by the Soviet Union since 1940— restored their statehood in August 1991.

When the Soviet Union dissolved in December 1991 and the Commonwealth

of Independent States (CIS) was created, Russian international lawyers started to ponder the legal nature of the CIS. Writing in 1994, Grechko and Shinkaretskaya observed that the second session of the interparliamentary assembly of the CIS in December 1992 decided to examine the question ‘of the development of confederation relations between the participants of the Commonwealth’ in the coming years.6 But what did this formulation mean? Grechko and Shinkaretskaya pointed out that, in the Soviet Union, the no-tion of ‘confederation’ had significantly changed over time. The Great Soviet Encyclopedia of 1973 defined confederation as a ‘permanent union of sover-eign states’. However, Soviet textbooks, the Great Soviet Encyclopedia, and the Diplomatic Dictionary published in the 1980s started to refer to ‘confederation’

4 LM Karapetyan, GM Khachatryan, ‘Suverenitet narodov v mnogonatsional’nom gosudarstve’, SGP 1990 No 11, 3– 14 at 5.

5 Jeffrey Kahn, Federalism, Democratization, and the Rule of Law in Russia, OUP, 2002, 1.

6 LV Grechko, GG Shinkaretskaya, ‘Poniatie konferedatsii i SNG’, Moscow JIL 1994, 61– 73 at 61. 186 From Perestroika to Putin’s russia

as a ‘form of government’ (forma gosudarstvennogo ustroistva) between sov-ereign states. Grechko and Shinkaretskaya suggested that this modification to the Soviet encyclopedia became a gamechanger since ‘form of government’ can only mean ‘form of state’.7 The two authors concluded that ‘from the be-ginning’, the CIS was therefore a confederation— that is, a ‘form of state’.8 Thus, again, as during the Soviet period, a legal– political problem was resolved by assigning a different meaning to a concept, because, traditionally, ‘confeder-ation’ means a union of sovereign states. Again, this was done in support of centripetal forces in the Empire. According to this logic, former Soviet repub-lics could not become independent of Russia even after declaring independ-ence and becoming member states of the UN, as long as they were part of the new confederation (CIS).

In the 1990s, the newly established Moscow Journal of International Law

became one of the platforms for the defence of the Empire and pushback against further disintegration of the Russian Federation. The key theme for the Russian Federation after the dissolution of the Soviet Union was federalism. Russian international and constitutional law journals in the 1990s were filled with articles criticizing separatism in regions, their authors worried about the further dissolution of the Russian Federation. Writing in 1995, Andrei Aleksandrovich Gromyko- Piradov (b 1966) concluded: ‘We can say that to preserve Russia as an integral and indivisible state, it is necessary to clear the “mine” laid in 1917 by the October Revolution, which proclaimed the idea of “national self- determination”.’9

Much later, when justifying the annexation of Crimea and the war against

Ukraine, Putin used the same metaphor of a ‘mine’ which the Bolsheviks argu-ably laid under the edifice of the Russian Empire in and after 1917, with rhet-oric supporting the right of peoples to self- determination.

The main contested point remained as to whether the post- 1991 Russian

Federation itself was based on constitution or treaty. Scholars writing in Russia’s international and constitutional law journals vehemently defended the idea that the Russian Federation was based on a constitution, not on a treaty or treaties.10 Nevertheless, on 31 March 1992 the Federation Treaty was signed by the Russian government and the regions, a treaty that acknowledged re-publican ‘sovereignty’ and their right to self- determination. Technically, the

Federalist ideas, selF-determination 187

Federation Treaty consisted of three separate treaties and two protocols.11 Tartarstan and Chechnya, the two regions of the Russian Federation with the most advanced sovereignty claims at the time, refused to sign the Federation Treaty because, for them, it did not go far enough. Tartarstan also held a ref-erendum in which 61.3 per cent favoured Tartarstan as a ‘sovereign state’.12 For regional political elites, a ‘treaty- constitutional’ approach for the Russian Federation meant not federation but a loose confederation of sovereign states; in contrast, federal elites held the reverse view, namely that the Federation was a free- standing sovereign entity that did not need justification from its member- units.13 Regional political elites were also relying on President Yeltsin’s initial statement that regions could take ‘as much sovereignty as they could swallow’.

In 1993, when a new Russian Constitution was drafted, key commitments

made to the Russian republics and regions in the Federation Treaty of 31 March 1992— commitments both symbolic and practical, such as power- sharing— were unilaterally repealed by Moscow.14 Here is a clear historical parallel with how the 1922 treaty of the establishment of the Soviet Union was essentially pushed into irrelevance and oblivion when the imperial centre reestablished control over the Soviet republics, which remained ‘sovereign states’ only formally. The tsarist Empire, too, unilaterally eviscerated the au-tonomy of Ukraine in the eighteenth century as well as Finland and the Baltic provinces in the late nineteenth century.

During the 1990s, several bilateral ‘treaties’ were also concluded between

Moscow and the ethnic republics of the Russian Federation such as Tartarstan, Kabardino– Balkaria, and Baskhortostan. For example, the Treaty on the Demarcation of Objects of Jurisdiction and the Mutual Delegation of Powers Between the Bodies of State Power of the Russian Federation and the Republic of Tartarstan was signed on 15 February 1994. At least the Tartar side led the treaty negotiations with Yeltsin’s Russia with the understanding of Tartarstan’s old statehood predating its conquest by Muscovy in the sixteenth century.15 Tartarstan was described in the preamble of its treaty as ‘a state united with the Russian Federation’, less than the ‘associated’ status claimed in the republic’s constitution— but still a remarkable statement of autonomy.16 Altogether, forty- seven of the eighty- nine units of the Federation negotiated bilateral treaties

with the federal government.17 The last treaty between the Russian central gov-ernment and a federal unit was signed on 16 June 1998.18 Some started to call the period 1994– 8 in the Russian Federation ‘the parade of treaties’ or ‘parade of sovereignties’.19 However, a gathering of dark clouds over these treaty repub-lics was indicated when the Ministry of Justice of the Russian Federation an-nounced in late 1996 that nineteen out of twenty- one republican constitutions violated the federal Constitution.20

Indeed, a key policy of President Putin was established— with strong help

from the Russian Constitutional Court, which rejected claims of ‘sovereignty’ by the republics— 21 that eventually all such bilateral treaties would be termin-ated and phased out. The last such bilateral treaty— with Tartarstan— expired in 2017.22 With some parallels (even though the Russian army never left the ‘sovereign states’ within the Russian Federation), this also echoes the experi-ence of the Baltic States and Georgia as new states concluding treaties with Soviet Russia in 1920. In 1920, the sovereignty of the Baltic States and Georgia was proclaimed ‘for ever’, but Moscow unilaterally snatched it back from Georgia in 1921 and from the Baltic States (de facto even if not de jure) in 1940. For former parts of the Russian Empire, it is very difficult to negotiate a treaty on sovereignty and secession with Russia because— from the perspective of the rulers of Russia, whether Soviet or post- Soviet— such treaties primarily reflected the fluctuating balance of power and consequently could be repealed when the central government’s power consolidated once again. Put differ-ently, a sentiment exhausted from the Empire could over time easily turn into a mood nostalgic for the Empire. Caroline von Gall, a German constitutional law scholar, has concluded that in the Russian Federation the very purpose of federalism differs from Western models, as its essence in Russia is state unity rather than support of diversity and pluralism.23

A special post- Soviet situation is the ‘union state’ between the Russian

Federation and Belarus. Unlike Chechnya and Tartarstan, Byelorussia (the previous official name of Belarus) was one of the leading Soviet republics, since 1945 a UN member state whose leader participated in a core group of Eastern

However, on 8 December 1999 the leaders of Russia and Belarus signed a treaty forming a ‘union state’ between the two ‘sovereign states’.24 From the beginning, Russian scholars such as Nadezhda Pastukhova played with the idea that the union state between Russia and Belarus was a unique confederation which had the potential to quickly become a federation, as both the Russian and Belarus peoples ‘did not want the dissolution of the Soviet Union’ and most of them ‘perceive each other as one people’.25

Writing in 2001, Galina Georgievna Shinkaretskaya from the Institute

of State and Law of the Russian Academy of Sciences, also a member of the international law council at the Russian Ministry of Foreign Affairs, reminded her readers that under the treaty concluded on 8 December 1999 unification had become an international legal obligation for both Russia and Belarus.26 That is, unification was no longer an option: they were legally obliged to do so. Another legal scholar, NA Mikhaleva, added that unification between the two states would mean ‘the highest form of integration between two sovereign states’.27 Mixing international legal analysis and geopolitical argumentation, Sergey Nikolaevich Baburin (b 1959), a legal scholar and politician (including one presidential candidacy), suggested that unification of Russia and Belarus was a symbol of the ‘awakening’ of the Empire, and that the Russian Federation could not be restricted to the constraints of a nation state.28

Writing in 2012, Dmitriev and Mironov argued that the union treaty be-

tween Russia and Belarus foresees ‘a state of the confederate type’— an uncon-ventional definition of ‘confederation’, already familiar to us— but specified that in history a confederation was usually only a temporary phenomenon. Either the union dissolves as a confederation into separate sovereign states or it becomes a genuine federation so that, for example, the Republic of Belarus could join the Russian Federation as a republic with special status, or its six oblasts could join.29 Before President Putin used the territory of Belarus to at-tack Ukraine in February 2022, some Russian legal scholars also highlighted

The inner- Russian discussion on the meaning of the right of peoples to self-

determination continued in the post- Soviet Russian Federation. Writing in 2004, Mamonov maintained that the international legal principle of the right of peoples to self- determination was not applicable to the Russian Federation, ‘because it covers nations who are living in territories which are colonies’.31 Instead, what was needed was to ‘work out the politics of protectionism with respect to the state- forming ethnos and adopt a special federal solution for this problem’.32 Thus, times had changed so that according to this author, at that time, after the dissolution of the Soviet Union and with the ‘parade of sover-eignties’ in the Russian Federation, these were already ethnic Russians who needed special measures and governmental protection.33

The academic legal discourse on ethnic minorities in the Russian Federation

can still resemble patronizing discourses already familiar during the Soviet era. The common theme is that, historically, joining the Russian Empire was good for other ethnicities— especially for the minority indigenous peoples. Writing in 2004, Yakovlev concluded that the unification of Yakutia with Russia ‘had a progressive meaning as it corresponded with the formation of the centralized, multinational Russian state’.34 Furthermore, Yakovlev argued: ‘The arrival of the Russians and establishment of the jurisdiction of Russian legislation helped to direct the development of societal relations of the Yakutians in a civ-ilized legal channel. Yakutia became part of a strong, mighty, civilized state.’35

a 2005 Gosudarstvo i pravo article by Tikhomirov:

In other words, there has been a continuity of legal– political legitimation of Russia as a multinational state and— at least historically— an empire. Incidentally, such analyses usually do not problematize the fact that Russia’s regions such as Yakutia are among the richest in natural resources.



State Continuity or Succession?

Putin’s Russia solved the dilemma— of state continuity with or succession to the Soviet Union— with a unique constitutional compromise which suggested that the Russian Federation is both: it is the same state as the Soviet Union, but at the same time not the same state. When the Russian Constitution was amended by the ‘all- Russian vote’ on 1 July 2022, one change among others was the addition of Article 67.1, paragraph 1 according to which:

the Russian Federation is the state successor of the USSR on its territory and also state successor (continuator)37 of the USSR in terms of membership in international organizations and their organs, membership in international treaties, and also when foreseen with international treaties with respect to ac-tions and obligations of the USSR beyond Russian borders.

What is remarkable about this constitutional statement is how it uses the concepts of state succession (правопреемство) and continuity (правопродолжательство; also континуитет) essentially as describing one and the same state: today’s Russian Federation. The reason for this is that, on the one hand, the Russian Federation in its state practice has emphasized that it has continued the rights and personhood of the USSR, especially on the UN Security Council; while on the other, in post- Soviet Russian scholarship of international law, disagreements have arisen about whether state continuity or



36 Yu A Tikhomirov, ‘Geopoliticheskie imperativy bezopasnosti rossiiskogo gosudarstva’, GP 2005 No 1, 80– 87 at 81.

37 Russian: pravopreemnik (pravoprodolzhatel).

192 From Perestroika to Putin’s russia

state succession is the right concept to use, in other words whether the Russian Federation is, as a subject of international law, the same state as the USSR or not. Both concepts have been used interchangeably. Stanislav Valentinovich Chernichenko (1935– 2021), one of the leading international law academics in post- Soviet Russia, criticized the simultaneous use in Russia of both concepts— state continuity and state succession— to characterize the inter-national legal status of the post- 1991 Russian Federation.38 Chernichenko favoured the concept of state continuity, just as the Russian MFA has claimed it in the UN Security Council and elsewhere in state practice.39

However, Russian scholarly opinions have been more diverse. Some ex-

amples of how the concept of ‘state succession’ has continued to be favoured by Russian international lawyers— at least besides ‘state continuity’ with the Soviet Union— are the following. In an article in the Moscow Journal of International Law, Galina Shinkaretskaya discussed the problem of state succession and the borders of the Russian Federation,40 observing that after the dissolution of the Soviet Union the population in several post- Soviet states found the borders between Soviet republics unjust.41 Although the guiding principles of the for-mation of former Soviet— now national— republics had been ethnic, some of these territories— since December 1991 outside the control of Moscow— were still up to 100 per cent inhabited by ethnic Russians:

On the psychological level interrepublican borders were not perceived as borders between states [. . .] Thus, neglect of the most important prin-ciple of international law— the principle of sovereignty of the people— led to the emergence of borders which create obstacles to normal interstate cooperation.42

Again speaking of the ‘sovereignty of the people’ (as ‘one of the most important principles of international law’), in her analysis Shinkaretskaya demon-strated the flexibility of basic international legal concepts. She asserted that post- Soviet borders between the former constituent republics of the Soviet Union needed a different approach from that by which the Soviet border had been dealt with. Additionally, Article 5 of the CIS agreement enshrined the

obligation to guarantee ‘openness of borders’, freedom of movement of citi-zens, and transfer of information in the framework of the CIS.43 Moreover, Shinkaretskaya pointed out that the succession of borders, according to inter-national legal opinion, was not always absolutely certain.44 What is more, she also opined that as Russia declared itself ‘sovereign’ from the Soviet Union, the Russian Federation as a subject of international law was identical with the Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic (SFSR); with respect to the Soviet Union, the new Russian Federation was only a state successor, like the remaining fourteen ex- Soviet republics.45 When the heads of state of the CIS decided that the Russian Federation would continue membership of the Soviet Union at the UN and in other international organizations, this was— according to Shinkaretskaya— an expression of agreement by other successors of the Soviet Union, namely that the Russian Federation ‘succeeded’ to Soviet posi-tions in these organizations.46 Shinkaretskaya further observed:

The case of inheriting the borders of the USSR and the lines of administrative division drawn by the Soviet authorities, when not colonies, but republics that were previously equal in rights, became sovereign states, is still unique. But the mechanism for inheriting borders is the same as in Latin America in the last century and in Africa in the middle of this century. [. . .] Thus, uti possidetis is not an independent legal principle and acquires a legal character, becoming part of the broader principle of the inviolability of borders. [. . .] The situation is much more complicated with that part of the borders which, before the collapse of the USSR, was the border between the union republics. Neither the population nor the leadership of the CIS countries and Russia perceive this part as a ‘real’ state border, which in the perception of the Soviet people should always be ‘locked’. The appearance of such a border between groups of the population that traditionally lived the same life is perceived as an injustice. The concept of justice plays a greater role in the problem of boundaries than in any other area. No border will be stable if the people living on both sides do not consider the border to be fair. In the case of the borders of independent states— the former republics of the USSR— a solution can only be fair if it makes the presence of the border almost invisible, that is, it preserves the status quo as much as possible.47

194 From Perestroika to Putin’s russia

In other words, what Shinkaretskaya proposed was that although international legal principles of state succession applied to the dissolution of the Soviet Union and its borders, the borders between Soviet republics should not have become ‘real’ (guarded, ‘locked’, and so on) borders as this would be ‘unjust’. Soon, with respect to Georgia since 2008 and Ukraine since 2014, revision of these Soviet- era borders became official Russian policy.

Other Russian international lawyers came to differently nuanced conclu-

sions regarding the issue of state identity (continuity) and state succession. Georgi Velyaminov made the point that although the territory of the Russian state (‘in the broad sense’) had changed and its socio- political system changed twice, its international personality was invariably preserved.48 Where this sounds like state continuity (identity), Velyaminov qualifies the situation fur-ther by calling it ‘partial succession’ instead: ‘In relation to the RSFSR, one can speak of nothing more than “partial succession”, but not of a complete change of international personality and “full succession”.’49

This assessment reflects Soviet Russian policy after 1917, namely that it ac-

cepted some earlier treaties and rejected others. Furthermore, in November 1996, France and the Russian Federation reached an agreement on the sym-bolic repayment by Russia of previous, tsarist- era debts.50 Velyaminov con-cluded that the post- Soviet Russian Federation remained a ‘state successor’ to the Russian Empire, the Russian SFSR, and the Soviet Union.51 Petr Kremnev, another international law scholar from Moscow, has expressed doubts as to whether state continuity is the right term for characterizing the Russian Federation’s post- 1991 status.52 Kremnev concluded that the Belovezhe agree-ments which ended the Soviet Union in December 1991 were both unconstitu-tional and illegal under international law, as they “violated the right of peoples to self- determination”. Those Soviet people who wanted to continue living in one state had the right to do so— a right which was illegally ignored, argues Kremnev.53

Putting together both the concepts of ‘state succession’ (Shinkaretskaya,

Velyaminov, Kremnev) and ‘state continuity’ (Chernichenko)— as did the Russian constitutional amendment of 1 July 2020— created the necessary flexi-bility for the Russian Federation. This is not a solution for legal purists but

No faction of domestic international lawyer– experts is neglected or dismissed either. This flexibility in the use of concepts is a Russian imperial continuity with which we have already become familiar based on the earlier debate, namely whether the Soviet Union was a continuator state of the Russian Empire. This also epit-omizes the continuity of the Soviet tradition of dialectical thinking and ‘re-solving’ contradictions by ‘reconciling’ apparently contradictory concepts and positions.54 Besides declarations of state continuity, ‘state succession’ also continues to be a popular classification in Russian scholarship of international law after the constitutional amendments of 1 July 2020.55 However, in the end a certain revisionist claim to state continuity tends to prevail in the Russian legal- political argumentation. For example, in May 2025, Anton Kobyakov, an advisor of President Putin, announced at the St Petersburg Legal Forum that in 1991 the Soviet Union was not dissolved in a constitutionally proper way, and that therefore it continues to exist legally, and the war in Ukraine is not inter-national but ‘a domestic process’.56



Clausula rebus sic stantibus

One of this book’s leitmotifs has been use of the clausula rebus sic stantibus ar-gument in justifying Russian and Soviet conquests and in geopolitical changes. A general debate revolves around whether this argument has experienced a certain renaissance in international legal practice, compared to the restrictive formulation of the Vienna Convention on the Law of Treaties of 1969.57 In the Russian Federation, this argument has been reused to justify seizure or— as it is seen in Russia— ‘return’ of originally Russian imperial territories. In 2014, Russia annexed Crimea, violating its previous treaty commitments such as the 1994 Budapest memorandum and the 2003 border treaty with Ukraine. Was there any way to justify the Russian annexation in the context of inter-national law, beyond a reference to the right of Crimea’s Russian speakers

In 2014, one of the most experienced Russian inter-national lawyers, Georgi Velyaminov, tackled this question in an article in the journal Gosudarstvo i pravo, the successor of Sovetskoe gosudarstvo i pravo. Velyaminov argues that although Crimea had been handed over from the Russian Soviet Federalist Socialist Republic (SFSR) to the Ukrainian SSR in 1954 ‘unconstitutionally’, this did not matter from an international law per-spective as, in the Budapest memorandum of 1994, Russia had indeed impli-citly recognized Crimea as part of Ukraine.58 However, Velyaminov further argues that the assurance given by Russia to Ukraine was not given abso-lutely and forever but only in the context of the Treaty on Nonproliferation of Nuclear Weapons.59 Thus, at the end of his argumentation, Velyaminov draws his legal trump card— the good old principle of clausula rebus sic stantibus, al-ready so familiar to us from tsarist Russian and Soviet history. According to Velyaminov, the Budapest memorandum was concluded under the circum-stances prevailing in 1994— but those circumstances, order, and legality no longer existed in Ukraine in 2014.60 In the same article published in 2014, Velyaminov, himself a Soviet Second World War veteran, also advocated for preventive military strikes against Ukraine: ‘The experience of World War II and the Munich “policy of appeasement” regarding aggressive fascism which preceded it demonstrates that defense must be preventive. One need not wait until [the enemy] gathers forces for an open attack.’61

This psychology and guidance for action is exactly what President Putin ex-

pressed and implemented on 24 February 2022.

Other Russian international lawyers also justified Russia’s violation of

treaties concluded with Ukraine in the 1990s. International lawyers Voronin, Kulebyakin, and Nikolaev admitted the existence of these treaties be-tween Russia and Ukraine fixing the border of 1991 but pointed out that few had bothered to ask whether they were in correspondence with an im-perative norm in international law (jus cogens), namely the right of peoples to self- determination.62 In their view, enforcing the right of peoples to self- determination is not aggression; after all, Russia’s border with Ukraine was fixed against the backdrop of the 1997 Treaty on Friendship, Cooperation and Partnership— which Ukraine was no longer honouring since Maidan.63

In its essence, this is the same argument on treaties presented by the Russian

Empire in 1870 regarding its navy in the Black Sea region and, as this book has demonstrated, on which Russia and the Soviet Union have since repeat-edly relied. It is only that nowadays, the argument of jus cogens can addition-ally be used on such occasions— but in a way showing how the argument of ‘self- determination of peoples’ goes back and forth in Russian history, at one time justifying secessions from Russia, and then at another justifying an-nexations by Russia. According to its recent uses in the Russian Federation, self- determination of peoples does not apply as a ground for secession in non- colonial situations where the political aspirations of arguably non- Russian eth-nicities in the Russian Federation are concerned (for example, Chechnya and Tartarstan). However, it does apply concerning the rights of ethnic Russians or native Russian speakers living compactly outside the Russian Federation in other ex- Soviet republics (such as Crimea and the Donbas). Behind this paradox lies the principle of ‘might makes right’ and the idea that great powers formulate and guarantee international law according to their interests and preferences.

Because reference to clausula rebus sic stantibus and unilateral termination

or violation of treaties seems such a constant in Russian and Soviet history, researchers have also asked where it originates from. The semiotician Yuri Mikhailovich Lotman (1922– 93) traced it back to deeper structural cultural differences in the Middle Ages, when Western Europe developed a ‘chivalrous’ contractual culture and ancient Rus developed a culture of what Lotman called ‘submission’.64 Lotman postulated:

In imbuing the concept of government with religious sentiment, social psychology of this type demands from society that it transfer its entire semi-osis onto the tsar, who becomes a symbolic figure, a kind of living icon. The remaining members of society are left with behavior having zero semioticity; only purely practical activity is required of them.65

Russian and Soviet proclivity for the use of clausula rebus sic stantibus re-garding treaties in international law also reflects how Russia as a country and society has experienced constitutional law in its history, that established rules

65 Ibid, 57.

were volatile and in the end, notwithstanding occasional formal constitutional assurances, the tsarist- centralized model of governance prevailed.



Russia as a Great Power, Balance of Power,

and the Defence of History

The predominant doctrine of international law during the Putin era can be described as realism. During the late 1980s one of the Soviet Union’s most promising international lawyers, Rein Müllerson (b 1944), advocated for the revival of balance of power as a foundation for international law, writing in the context of Russia’s justifications for annexing Crimea.66 This is a con-trast to some Soviet scholars who formally rejected balance of power as too ‘bourgeois’ and imperialist a doctrine,67 even though, as we demonstrated above, parts of the Soviet doctrine of international law accepted the im-portance of balance of power under various labels. The problem with this kind of realism based on balance of power is that it tends to make inter-national law a mere figleaf for great- power interests. One of Russia’s con-temporary leading international relations experts, Dmitri Trenin (b 1955), writes that international relations are based on force, not on law.68 This had, even earlier, led to changes of discourse in Russian understandings of international law. When— during the Soviet area— in Soviet legal discourse ‘small states’ deserved equality and needed Soviet protection from US im-perialism, now— based on Russia’s post- Soviet experience— they had be-come problematic, such as when Lukashuk observed that small states could behave irresponsibly and exhibit the ‘psychology of the weak’.69 Making a similar point, Müllerson criticized tendencies of ‘anarchophilia’ which are promoted with the principle of self- determination in the international community.70

Just as during the Soviet period, one area in which the revival of great- power

thinking is clearly evident is the international law of the sea and maritime claims. The tradition of the Vyshnepolsky article of 1954 lives on: recently,



66 Rein Müllerson, Dawn of a New Order: Geopolitics and the Clash of Ideologies, IB Tauris, 2017, 154– 61.

67 NV Zakharova, I Yu Barsegov, ‘O tak nazyvaemoi teorii ’ravnovesia sil’, Soviet YBIL 1959, 272– 82.

68 Dmitri Trenin Novyi balans sil. Rossiia v poiskakh vneshnepoliticheskogo ravnovesia, Alpina Publisher, 2021, 25, 53.

69 II Lukashuk, Mezhdunarodnoe pravo. Obschchaya chast, 2nd edn, Beck, 2001, 323.

extensive claims to historical waters have again been presented in international law scholarship in Russia.71

Russia’s Eurasianist integration project has also been linked to great- power

needs and aspirations. The most pertinent examples are regional organiza-tions in post- Soviet Eurasia, for example the Commonwealth of Independent States. To illustrate, Yuri Ilyich Skuratov (b 1952) writes that a key role in the project belongs to Russian culture and language.72 He postulates that Eurasian integration is about positioning Russia as a superpower, as a first- order centre of world influence, as a great power.73

Finally, history remains contested by the example of the 1939 Nazi– Soviet

non- aggression pact and its appraisals in Russian legal academia. Grigory Serafimovich Starodubtsev (b 1954) suggests that we need to separate the fully normal, usual, and legal non- aggression pact itself from the secret proto-cols, which remain controversial.74 Writing in 2015, Starodubtsev argues that when the Soviet parliament declared the secret protocols of the pact void in 1989, this was not preceded by serious research; the matter of the secret proto-cols still needs further study instead of rushing to conclusions.75 The current editor- in- chief of the journal Gosudarstvo i pravo and member of the Russian Academy of Sciences, Aleksandr Nikolaevich Savenkov (b 1961), has gone further by arguing that the December 1989 resolution of the Soviet parliament to declare the secret protocols of the Nazi– Soviet pact of 1939 void and illegal was itself ‘legally invalid and illegal’.76 There is little in terms of reasoning be-hind this claim by Savenkov, except that ‘the decision on this question was al-ready taken by the international military tribunal at Nuremberg’.77 This is also a good example of why any continuity with previous decisions of the Russian and Soviet states needs to remain flexible: a Soviet decision from 1989 does not have to be binding on today’s Russia or its advocates of international legal history policy.

200 From Perestroika to Putin’s russia

The government of the Russian Federation has also maintained continuity

with the Soviet discourse on imperialism and colonialism. For example, a press release from the Russian MFA of 5 December 2024 commented on the fact that on 4 December 2024 the United Nations General Assembly (UNGA) adopted a resolution titled Eradication of Colonialism in All Its Forms and Manifestations, which was drafted by the Group of Friends in Defense of the UN Charter ‘with the guiding role of Russia’.78 A total of 113 countries voted for it while 63 states abstained, with no UN member state voting against. The Russian official press release stated: ‘The core of the abstaining group is made up of the former Western parent states, which bear the brunt of responsibility for centuries of colonial oppression of Asian, African and Latin American countries.’79

In debates at the UN, Ukraine, Poland, and the Baltic States criticized what

they saw as the Russian government’s ‘hypocritical’ position on this matter.80

9


In Conclusion



The intended contribution of this book is that it shows in some detail how im-perial and imperialist ideas, doctrines, and practices played out in the history of international law and legal discourse in the Russian Empire and the Soviet Union. This aspect has so far been somewhat neglected in parts of the inter-national law literature even though extensive justificatory arguments based on international law have played an important role in the expansion and pres-ervation of the Russian Empire and its transformation in the Soviet Union. International law and its concepts, often interpreted idiosyncratically (for example, assigning a new meaning to a legal– political concept), served as a pool for justificatory reasoning in favour of the Soviet Union. The Israeli inter-national lawyer Eyal Benvenisti has argued that manipulating international law may be less sinister than completely ignoring it.1 However, the opposite argument can also be made, because a ‘great power’ that openly ignores inter-national law is in some ways more honest in its brutality whereas manipulation in bad faith may fool at least some people somewhere, as the impression is in-voked that, well, at least the ‘great power’ somehow justified its action in terms of international law and still cares about it. But what if it does not? Here is where the arguably indeterminate nature of some international legal doctrines can also become a problem, especially for smaller states who cannot impose their understanding of international law by force.

Russian imperialism has been a major force in the history of international

law. Even though— in the Soviet self- understanding— imperialism for Russia ended in 1917 with the end of capitalism, the picture which emerges in the present study is that the Soviets managed to prolong the life of the Russian Empire until at least 1991, extensively using international legal arguments and doctrines in support. In that sense, Russia was more successful in keeping its Empire than were the West European maritime empires: Russia’s Empire lasted longer and, perhaps most importantly, many people and nations, especially



1 Eyal Benvenisti, ‘The Resilience of International Law in the Face of Empire’, Just Security,

17.02.2025, https:// www.justs ecur ity.org/ 107 820/ res ilie nce- intern atio nal- emp ire/ .

Russia, the Soviet Union, and Imperial Continuity in International Law. Lauri Mälksoo, Oxford University Press.

© Lauri Mälksoo 2025. DOI: 10.1093/ 9780198955962.003.0009

202 In ConClusIon

outside the West, learned to dissociate Russia from the evils of imperialism, just as suggested by Lenin, Khrushchev, but also Pashukanis. Various camouflage and facilitating doctrines were used to prolong the life of the Empire, including the concept that Soviet republics were ‘sovereign’, even though substantively they lacked the characteristics of state sovereignty and were subjugated to the Russian centre. In terms of decolonization, what happened for West European empires in around 1960 happened for the Soviet Union only in 1991, although at the time not even primarily based on demands by the formerly colonized Central Asian peoples. What is more, since President Putin came to power in Russia, the Russian government has tried to push back and rehabilitate its Empire, including with the help of international legal arguments. Russia’s ag-gression against Ukraine since 2014 and especially since 2022 demonstrates that Russian imperialism continues to be a major factor in international pol-itics, a challenge for international law as traditionally understood because im-perialist states often have an agenda for revision of the territorial status quo. Russia’s predecessor, the Soviet Union, co- created the international law of 1945 from a position of military strength plus territorial gains in Europe and Asia; but, now that Russia’s territory has diminished since 1991, the new Russia of Putin is attacking the very foundations of international law of 1945, at least from the perspective of smaller neighbouring states such as Ukraine.

This book has argued that it makes sense also to understand the Soviet pe-

riod in Russia’s history from both the imperial and the imperialist perspectives. When we understand imperialism as a desire to territorially expand and motiv-ation to defend land seizures— facilitated by international legal arguments and justifications, as discussed in this book— then Lenin’s concept of imperialism (‘as the highest stage of capitalism’) is inadequate. On too many occasions was Soviet rhetoric in contexts of international law fiercely ‘anti- imperialist’, in stark contrast to Soviet expansionist behaviour towards its neighbours. Moreover, Muscovy started to expand and in this sense was imperialist long before the arrival of capitalism.

A particular tool of international legal policies in Russia and the Soviet Union

has been treaties. At least in territories which have aimed to break away from the Empire throughout history, Russia and the Soviet Union have favoured a flexible approach to treaties, based on which any agreement reflects the balance of power at any given moment. This means that such treaties are not very stable and can be reversed by Russia when circumstances are geopolitically ripe.

Another conclusion from this study is the continuity between imperial ideas

and doctrines of international law in the Russian Empire, the Soviet Union, and often also Putin’s Russian Federation. Soviet approaches to international

In ConClusIon 203

law were studied quite extensively in the West during the Cold War but not all researchers saw the connection between the doctrines of tsarist Russia and the Soviet Union, especially regarding Russia’s flexible thinking on treaties.

An imperial continuity links Russian, Soviet, and post- Soviet international

law. Although previous doctrines and interpretations of international law have several times been rejected and transformed, nevertheless the key interest in preserving the territory of the Empire has usually remained paramount. Characteristically, Maxim Anatol’evich Isaev (1966– 2021), a constitutional lawyer from MGIMO University, has written that tsarist Russia was not a ‘prison house of peoples’ (as Lenin claimed) but a ‘family of peoples’, a dynamic system which offered its population peace and constituted their world (мірь).2 Unlike for early Soviet legal scholars and politicians, the Russian Empire is no longer something to be condemned and criticized; one can and should be proud of it, its grandeur and might. Apparently, this conviction has never disappeared, and has influenced generations of international lawyers in Russia and the Soviet Union.

Considering that the imperial— and repeatedly also imperialist— trajectory

has been characteristic of Russian and Soviet international law traditions for some four hundred years, and that so far there has been very little critical con-frontation of this history among the international law community in Russia, it would be too idealistic to expect that Russia’s foundational imperial trajectory in its international legal approaches will quickly or easily fade away. Some first steps can be witnessed among Russian international lawyers and human rights defenders in exile. For example, when they endorsed punishment of the crime of aggression and war crimes committed in the war against Ukraine in their 2023 Brussels declaration, they concluded that ‘this war became possible be-cause previous grave crimes committed by Russia’s leadership and military on its own soil and abroad went unpunished’.3 While this view does not highlight an explicit connection with the history of imperialism in Russia, others make that link. Representatives of the Russian opposition currently living outside the Russian Federation such as the former chess world master Garry Kasparov have made the point that, in the future, it will be necessary for the Russian state and society to confront crimes committed in the past and the state’s imperialist legacy.4 Vladimir Kara- Murza considers it a cardinal mistake that critical en-gagement with Russia’s past and its state crimes was not consequentially tackled

in Russia during the 1990s, unlike in most other Central and East European states.5 The Belarus opposition leader in exile, Sviatlana Tsikhanouskaya, has celebrated the memory of Kastus Kalinouski (1838– 64), who ‘led the 1863– 1864 uprising against imperial oppression’.6

In his recent interpretation of constitutional evolution in Russia, the

Australian constitutional law scholar William Partlett has argued that Russia is not historically fated to remain a centralized, authoritarian country, despite a long history of commitment to the centralized state.7 Partlett suggests that in making this positive claim for a balanced constitution, Russians can learn from the reasoning and arguments of the tsarist- era liberal statist movement and thus have a suggestion for interaction with post- Putin Russia:

This has implications for the international community as well. Those hoping to support Russian democratic reform will need to look beyond personal guarantees from a new Russian leader or the promise of more constitutional rights. Instead, they must seek to encourage deeper structural and normative constitutional changes that can allow Russia to finally break with its deeply held commitment to a centralised state.8

However, from the perspective of the history of international law and inter-national legal ideas as represented in Russia and discussed in the present study, a direct connection also exists between the centralization of power and the Empire; the first facilitated and justified the second. Identities con-structed around the Empire are apparently one of the main reasons why lib-eral and rights- based reforms have not been successful in Russia. As far as the tsarist- era liberal statist movement was concerned, we should also not forget the legacy of Martens and Mandelstam, who wanted to reform the Empire and make it ‘liberal’ while also keeping it territorially intact. Reluctance to let the Empire go may have been one of the key reasons why liberal constitutional re-forms in Russia failed in the first place, both in 1917 and after 1991. Thus, the main question for post- Putin Russia’s leaders and legal elites is not just whether they would be willing to give up a centralized state and take human rights ser-iously; perhaps even more crucial is whether Russia is willing to critically work on and abandon its imperial identity in international law.
